Rabbi Binyomin Pruzansky
The Greatest Destination
And you shall rejoice on your festival—you, your son, your 
daughter…:” (Devarim 16:14)
It was Chol HaMoed, and Rabbi Fischel Schachter wanted to take 
his children on an outing. Like many parents, he was looking 
for something fun, but also budget-friendly. So he gathered his 
children, told them to get into the van, and off they went.
“Where are we going, Tatty?” they asked. “You’ll see,” he re
plied. Truthfully, he had no destination in mind. He just started 
driving, hoping inspiration would strike. As they got on the high
way, the kids spotted signs for JFK Airport.
“Are we flying somewhere?” the kids asked. Rabbi Schachter 
smiled. “You’ll see.”
And then it hit him. It was a brilliant idea. They’d go to JFK, ride 
the AirTrain, and watch planes take off and land. A free trip, fun for 
the kids, and light on the wallet.
When they arrived at the airport, he realized he wasn’t the only 
one with this idea. The terminal was packed with families from 
Boro Park, all riding the AirTrain in circles, waving to one another 
and enjoying the adventure.
At one stop, a pilot entered the train. He looked the part: uni
form, suitcase, the whole package. Rabbi Schachter asked one of his 
children to give up their seat so the pilot could sit. The man grate
fully accepted and struck up a conversation.
64
Tishrei Companion 5786
“I see you’re flying with the family,” the pilot said. “Where are 
you headed?” “Oh,” Rabbi Schachter hesitated, “nowhere, real
ly.” The pilot looked puzzled. “No, really, tell me. I know this air
port inside and out. I can help you find the right terminal.” Rabbi 
Schachter smiled. “We’re not going anywhere. It’s Chol HaMoed, 
and this is our trip.” “You’re telling me you’re just riding the Air
Train in circles?” the pilot asked, stunned. “Pretty much,” Rabbi 
Schachter said, laughing. “That’s our outing.” 
The pilot sat back, thinking deeply. Then he turned to Rabbi 
Schachter and said something unforgettable:
“You know, every day I get up and I know exactly where I’m 
going. I know the destination, the flight path, the exact timing. But 
my life? My wife left me and my kids don’t talk to me. So yes, I trav
el the world, but I have no destination. But you—you don’t have a 
destination, and yet you have everything. Watching you with your 
kids, seeing their happiness… that’s the greatest journey there is.”
With that, the pilot stood up, grabbed his suitcase, and walked 
off the train.
Rabbi Schachter later reflected on this telling encounter. We of
ten think joy comes from going somewhere exotic or doing some
thing exciting. But the truth is, the greatest joy isn’t found in a des
tination; it’s found in togetherness. It’s found in being present with 
the people we love and in cherishing the beracha of family.
And that’s the best destination of all.

Rabbi Dovid Goldwasser
From Blue Thread to Blue Ink
“I caused the Children of Israel to dwell in booths” (Vayikra 23:43)—they 
were Clouds of Glory (Sukkah 11b)
According to R’ Eliezer, we are commanded to build a Suk
kah in memory of the Ananei HaKavod, the Clouds of Glo
ry, which enveloped Klal Yisrael in the wilderness (Sukkah 
65
Tishrei Companion 5786
11b). 
The Tzemach Tzaddik, Rabbi Menachem Mendel Hager, posed 
a profound question. Why do we commemorate the Ananei HaKa
vod and not the other miracles? What about the manna, the mirac
ulous bread from Heaven that provided perfect sustenance, or the 
Be’er Miriam, the ever-flowing wellspring that gave us fresh water 
in the desert?
The Tzemach Tzaddik explains that food and water are essen
tial to life. Hashem had to provide them to the Jewish nation. But 
the protective clouds are something we could have survived with
out. We’ve all seen people living without roofs over their heads, 
tragically, but they still survive.
The Ananei HaKavod represent lovingkindness that goes beyond 
necessity. Hashem gave us more than we needed. He didn’t just 
sustain us; He surrounded us with love. That’s why we build Suk
kos. Not to remember our survival, but to remember His love. 
Years ago, I traveled to the former Soviet Union to give a clan
destine shiur (Torah class). Torah learning was illegal, and we had 
to move cautiously. I was passed from contact to contact, each one 
unaware of the final destination. Eventually, we descended into a 
basement, its surroundings damp and dingy, but holy. 
The shiur was unforgettable. The fire in their eyes and the hun
ger for Torah was unlike anything I had seen.
At precisely 10:00 p.m., I stood to leave, knowing there was a 
curfew. If I wasn’t back at my hotel soon, the authorities would 
notice. But as I approached the door, the host blocked my path. “Od 
chamesh dakot? Just five more minutes?” he pleaded. How could I 
say no? These Jews were thirsting for Hashem’s word.
I sat down again. Five minutes turned into fifteen. When I fi
nally stood up once more, he smiled again. “Od chamesh dakot…” 
Eventually, he said, “Tomorrow, I want to host a seudat peredah for 
you, a farewell meal. Please come to my apartment.” I agreed. 
That night, I wanted to bring him a gift. Something meaning
ful. I had brought along various Judaica items, so I began to think 
what I could give him. I considered a siddur, knowing that surely, 
he would treasure it. I also of a becher, a beautiful kiddush cup. But 
neither felt quite right. And then I saw it. It was a white challah 
cover, trimmed with blue thread, embroidered with a Magen David 
66
Tishrei Companion 5786
and the words “Shabbat Shalom.” That was it. I tucked it deep into 
my bag and went.
The next day, the seudah was something from another world. 
Holy, humble and filled with neshamos glowing in a tiny Soviet 
kitchen. At one point, the host—his name was Sasha—stepped out 
briefly. I approached him. “Sasha,” I said, “I have a matana (gift) for 
you.”
He didn’t respond. I repeated myself. “Sasha, here, this is for 
you.” Still unsure, I gently placed the bag in his hands.
He opened it slowly. And when he saw the white cloth, the blue 
embroidery, the words Shabbat Shalom, he broke down in tears. 
Tears of memory. Tears of longing. Then he motioned for me to fol
low him into the foyer. He reached up to a crumbling shelf, pulled 
out a folded paper napkin, and opened it carefully.
On it, drawn by hand in blue marker, was a Magen David. And 
written beneath it, in shaky but loving letters, were the words: 
Shabbat Shalom. Every week, for who knows how many years, this 
paper napkin had been Sasha’s challah cover. He took my cloth 
cover, kissed it, and placed it reverently on the shelf. Then he hand
ed me the napkin.
As I stepped out of that apartment, I was overcome with emo
tion. Hashem hadn’t just sent him a gift; He had matched the de
sign, the color, the sentiment to perfection. From cloth to paper, 
from blue thread to blue ink, it was Hashgachah Pratis in its purest 
form.
Sukkos reminds us that Hashem doesn’t just give us what we 
need. He gives us more. He gives us the Ananei HaKavod. He gives 
us the extra comfort, the extra love, the quiet embrace of Divine 
presence that whispers, “You are seen. You are remembered. You 
are Mine.”
May we sit in our Sukkah this year not just as a remembrance of 
the miracles, but as a testimony to Hashem’s infinite kindness; as a 
testimony to His love that shelters us, even when we don’t deserve 
it, even when we didn’t ask for it.
And may we, in return, offer our own matana to someone else, 
as an act of kindness that’s not required, but deeply remembered.
67
Tishrei Companion 5786

Rabbi Ephraim Wachsman
Faith that Creates Reality
When a person sits beneath this abode, the Shadow of Faith 
(Zohar, Parshas Emor 103a)
When Yaakov Avinu beheld the vision of the sulam, the celes
tial ladder, he saw, “Malachei Elokim olim v’yordim bo—Angels as
cending and descending (Bereishis 28:12). But this was not only a 
vision of angels; it was a vision of nations. The Midrash Tanchuma 
(Parshas Vayeitzei, 2) teaches that Yaakov saw the sar (ministering 
angel) of Bavel ascend seventy rungs, and then descend. He then 
saw the sar of Greece, Persia, and Rome, all rising and ultimately 
falling. And then Hashem turned to Yaakov and said: “Lamah ein 
atah oleh—Why are you also not ascending?”
But Yaakov hesitated. “I am afraid,” he replied. “If I rise, I too 
might fall.” To that, Hashem responded, “If you ascend, you will 
never fall.”
But Yaakov declined. He didn’t believe he could ascend with
out eventually descending. And from that moment, Hashem de
clared: “Because you did not believe, your children will be subject 
to exile.” Not as a punishment, but as a consequence. The failure 
to believe in one’s ability to spiritually rise and remain there led to 
generations of spiritual exile.
This begs a difficult question. Yaakov was an ish emes, a man 
of truth. If he believed he might fall, wasn’t that the truth? Was it 
wrong of him to be cautious? Isn’t it realistic to expect that a spiri
tual high will eventually give way to a low? After all, who doesn’t 
experience a post–Yom Kippur decline?
And yet, this is where the Torah introduces us to a deeper idea. 
There is a level of truth that is created by emunah; a reality that 
emerges not from logic, but from faith.
The world often teaches us that faith must follow truth. First 
we see, then we believe. But Judaism teaches that sometimes, faith 
creates truth. The Zohar (Parshas Emor, 103a) refers to the Sukkah as 
Tzilah d’Mehemenusa, the “shadow of faith.” What is a shadow? It is 
68
Tishrei Companion 5786
a reflection that follows us wherever we go. When our faith is deep 
enough, it casts a spiritual shadow upon reality. It makes real that 
which was not yet real.
The Sukkah is not merely a temporary hut. Its halachic dimen
sions—the height, the walls, the entryway—are halachically valid 
even when, in the world of tangible fact and reality, it seems flimsy 
and makeshift. What gives it status? Halacha L’Moshe Mi’Sinai, a tra
dition rooted in emunah (see Sukkah 6b; Rambam, intro. to Mishnah). 
The faith in the halachic process creates reality.
Yaakov’s mistake was not in being honest, but in limiting his 
belief to what he perceived. Hashem wasn’t asking him to be naive. 
He was offering him a spiritual leap beyond the normal patterns of 
ascent and descent. But Yaakov didn’t believe in himself enough. 
He did not believe that he could rise and stay there. And so, his 
children would have to learn that lesson through centuries of exile.
We, the children of Yaakov, are now at the end of that exile. 
And precisely because we are still here, our emunah today is more 
powerful than ever. The Jewish people, scattered across continents, 
oppressed through generations, still cling to their G-d, their Torah, 
and their destiny. That alone is a miracle of faith. It defies history, 
it defies realistic facts, and yet, it is made possible only through 
emunah.
This is the meaning of the verse, “L’hagid baboker chasdecha 
v’emunasecha baleilos—To declare Your kindness in the morning, 
and Your faithfulness at night” (Tehillim 92:2). Daytime is when 
we see clarity and can experience and declare Hashem’s kindness 
openly and more easily. But nighttime is when we hold onto faith. 
Faith in the darkness that creates the dawn.
That is why immediately after Yom Kippur, we run into the 
Sukkah. The Sukkah represents not a reward for spiritual work, but 
the continuation of it. The Sukkah is a physical structure that is val
id only because of our faith. Its walls are halachically walls because 
we believe they are. Its schach is kosher because we received a tradi
tion that says it is. The Sukkah is not about dwelling in something 
real, but dwelling in a reality created by faith.
In that sense, the Sukkah is not just a booth, but the prototype 
for geulah (redemption). The world of Moshiach will be a world of 
emes (truth), but it will be an emes that transcends our current lim
69
Tishrei Companion 5786
itations. A miraculous emes, a Divine emes. And what will bring it 
about? Emunasecha baleilos, our faith in the night of exile; our refusal 
to give up when all seems lost.
The world Yaakov Avinu knew was a world of rise and fall. But 
today, standing at the threshold of redemption, we are called to a 
higher truth: to believe that we can change, that we do grow, and 
that our aliyah is real.
Every Jew who walks into Yom Kippur and says, “I want to be 
better,” is speaking a truth deeper than facts, because the neshama 
of a Jew is never lost. The soul of a Jew can achieve above and be
yond the ladder of its dreams.
So let us enter Sukkos not just with joy, but with faith.
Faith in Hashem. Faith in the Jewish people. And yes, faith in 
ourselves.

Rabbi Fischel Schachter
The Palm Tree That Saved
“In booths you shall dwell for seven days” (Vayikra 23:42)
Sometimes the most powerful moments in life begin with the 
smallest, simplest decision.
A secular Israeli couple decided one year to build a Sukkah. 
Why? “Our kids know almost nothing about Judaism,” said the 
wife. “Let’s build a Sukkah, just so they have something, some 
connection.” Her husband was surprised. “But we’re not religious. 
We’re very leftist.” “Yeah, well... sometimes when you go far enough 
left, you wind up on the right,” argued the wife. So they built it.
They headed outside and gathered branches from a palm tree 
that stood outside their home to use as schach, the covering for the 
Sukkah. It was a small act of just one mitzvah, but to the family, it 
meant a lot. 
Then came October 7th.
The sirens, the terror, the rockets. The family ran into their ma
70
Tishrei Companion 5786
mad, their reinforced safe room, as terrorists stormed their commu
nity. Once inside, the father gripped the door handle as hard as 
he could, while terrorists tried again and again to break it open. 
Eventually, the terrorists gave up, but not without leaving their 
mark. They lit the house on fire, as flames began to rapidly spread, 
consuming the house. The door handle, jammed from the struggle, 
wouldn’t budge, leaving the family trapped inside the safe room, 
the only option being the window.
“Are you crazy?” the wife cried. “There are terrorists outside!” 
“We have no choice,” the husband said. “We’ll die here from the 
f
ire.” One by one, the family climbed out the window and into a 
world of smoke and fear. And then they began to run toward a 
palm tree. Its low branches offered a fragile shield, enabling them 
to camouflage as they crouched and hid. Hamas fighters passed by 
again and again, yet never noticed them.
At one point the father said, “I think I see a better place to 
hide—a burnt-out shelter across the street. Let’s run for it.” But the 
wife stopped him. “Hashem saved us here. Let’s stay.” And they 
did. They stayed by that tree until, hours later, they were rescued.
When they finally emerged, they saw that their home had been 
burned to the ground. Everything was gone. Except one thing.
The Sukkah. It remained untouched. And then they turned back 
and looked again at the tree that had sheltered them. 
It was the very palm tree from which they had taken the schach.
That is what happens when a Jew, no matter how distant, 
makes even a small decision to reconnect. One mitzvah. One step. 
One action to say, “I want to live a life of meaning. I want to give 
my children something to hold onto.”
And in return, Hashem gives us shelter.
Sometimes, quite literally.

Rabbi Yisroel Majeski
Keep the Hat
“He who loves money will never be satisfied with money” (Koheles 5:10)
71
Tishrei Companion 5786
There’s an old story about a community that wanted to buy 
their beloved rabbi a new hat. He hadn’t gotten one since 
his bar mitzvah, and they figured it was time. And so, they 
approached him one day. 
“Rabbi, we want to take you shopping for a new hat and get 
you something nice. Come look with us.” The rabbi smiled warmly. 
“I appreciate it… but I should warn you, it’s going to be a very ex
pensive hat.” “Expensive?” they asked, confused. “What, you want 
a Borsalino? Maybe a special Italian felt hat?” “No, no” said the 
rabbi, shaking his head. “This hat is going to cost you one million 
dollars.”
They stare at him, stunned. “A million dollars? For a hat?” “You 
see, I’ll come home with this beautiful, stylish new hat and look in 
the mirror and say, ‘This hat is too elegant for this old suit.’ So now 
I’ll need a new suit.” They nodded, trying to keep up. “Okay… so 
we’ll buy you a suit.” The rabbi continued. “Well, now that I have 
a new suit and a stunning hat, how can I wear them with these 
scuffed shoes? I’ll need new shoes. And a tie, of course.” “Fine, Rab
bi, we’ll get you the whole outfit.” “But what about my Rebbetzin? 
I’m walking around in brand-new clothing, and she’s in her old 
wardrobe. It’s only fair to her if she gets a new sheitel. And once she 
gets a new sheitel, she’ll need a new wardrobe to match.” “Okay, 
okay,” they say. “We’ll get your wife a wardrobe too.” “But what 
about the kids?” the rabbi said. “You expect us to walk into shul, all 
dressed up, while the kids are in old hand-me-downs? They’ll need 
clothing too.” The congregants were now starting to sweat.
“And when we walk home,” the rabbi continued, “we’ll sit 
down at our old, scratched-up table. That won’t do. We’ll need a 
new table, new chairs, a new couch, new curtains, new kitchen, 
new bedrooms… We’ll need to redo the entire house. And by the 
time we’re finished, well… that hat is going to cost about a million 
dollars.”
The committee looked at each other, then back at the rabbi. 
“Okay, Rabbi,” they said. “Keep the hat.”
One thing is for sure. Desire has no ceiling. 
Shlomo HaMelech writes in Koheles, which is read over Suk
kos: “He who loves money will never be satisfied with money” (Ko
heles 5:10). The Pasuk doesn’t say that he who has money will never 
72
Tishrei Companion 5786
be satisfied. It talks about someone who loves money. The Torah 
doesn’t critique wealth; it critiques attachment. If you love it, chase 
it, need it, it’ll never be enough.
As the Midrash (Koheles Rabbah 3:13) puts it, “One who has 100, 
wants 200. If he has 200, he wants 400. And so on…” But the Mid
rash doesn’t leave it at that. It concludes with something haunting. 
“A person will not leave this world with even half his desires ful
f
illed.”
There’s one odd part about this statement, notes Rav Eliyahu 
Lopian zt”l. If a person has 100 and wants 200, then at least he has 
half? How can the Midrash say that he will not have half his desires 
fulfilled?
His answer is brilliant. When a person chases the next thing, the 
thing he already has becomes worthless in his eyes. The moment 
he sets his sights on the next desire, the current one loses meaning. 
Even the half he does have, he doesn’t truly enjoy.
And then comes Sukkos and tells us: Enough.
We leave our secure homes, our lavish furniture, our cli
mate-controlled walls, and we sit under flimsy schach, exposed 
to the wind, the noise, the world. And we say, “Baruch Hashem. 
Thank You, G-d, I have what I need.” Sukkos reminds us that joy 
comes not from accumulation, but from appreciation. It’s not about 
the hat or the house or the harvest. It’s about living with gratitude.
The Dubno Maggid put it this way in his inimitable mashal. 
There once was a simple, poor wagon driver who stopped at 
an inn for the night. Almost as soon as his head hit the pillow, he 
began drifting off into a dream. In the dream, he walked outside, 
dug into the earth behind the inn, and discovered a chest of gold.
That was enough to jolt him. He woke up, heart pounding. 
Could it be? Running outside, he began to dig. And to his amaze
ment, he hit something hard: gold coins! He was ecstatic. But not 
wanting anyone else to find out, especially the innkeeper, he quiet
ly went to the owner and said, “I want to buy your inn.” “It’s not for 
sale,” the innkeeper replied. “Name your price,” the man insisted. 
“Any price.” Eventually, the owner gave in. The man raced home, 
sold everything he had—his cart, his furniture, even his family’s 
beds—and returned to purchase the inn.
The next night, his family joined him. They grabbed shovels 
73
Tishrei Companion 5786
and began to dig frantically, searching for the treasure…
But nothing ever turned out.
All night they dug. Nothing. They then dug some more, all 
throughout the day, and still… nothing. Finally, the man stood 
back, breathing heavily. “I forgot to mention something…” he sud
denly said. “What?” they all shouted. He lowered his eyes. “It was 
a dream.”
They stared at him in shock. “We gave up everything for a 
dream?!”
The Dubno Maggid concluded. So many people give up their 
entire lives chasing a dream that was never real to begin with. A 
fantasy of wealth, prestige, power… and at the end, it’s all hevel 
havalim, vanity and illusion.
Sukkos tells us to live awake and to realize what truly matters. 
We are reminded to build our homes not with bricks and stone, 
but with love, faith, family and Torah. Sukkos tells us with utmost 
clarity that the most important things in life aren’t bought in stores 
or measured in dollars.
They’re sung in the Sukkah. They’re danced with our children. 
They’re whispered in prayer. They’re lived in moments of clarity 
and simplicity.
So as we enter Sukkos, let’s put the million-dollar hat aside, 
and step into the joy of knowing the truth of truths… “I have what 
I need.”

And you shall eat, and be satisfied and bless Hashem, your G-d. 
From this קוספ we learn the אתיירואד בויח to say a הנורחא הכרב  
after we have finished eating. 
The food that Hashem provides for us  
gives us nourishment and energy throughout the day. 
Hashem instructed us to make a הכרב after we finish eating, 
to bring home the knowledge and awareness  
that all is from Him. 
As we delve deeper into הנורחא הכרב,  
let’s use it as a vehicle to bring us closer to Hashem,  
Luving Living Like a Yid even more!

Avrohom Avinu’s Kiruv Tool 
 
Avraham Avinu reintroduced monotheism back into the world. How did he do it? 
   
The קוספ tells us: 
 ַ טִּ יַו ֥ע  ֶא֖ לֶ ש  ֵאְבִּ ב ֣ר  ָ ש֑ עַב  ִּ יַו ֨ א ָר ְק  ָ ש֔ם  ֵ שְ ב ֥ם  ֵק 'ה ֥ל  ָלֹוע )גל:אכ תישארב( ם 
And he planted an לשא in עבש ראב and he called there in the Name of 'ה. 
 
Rashi explains that this לשא was either an orchard or an inn.  
םישרפשמ explain that both are correct for לשא actually stands for: 
היול ,היתש ,הליכא – Giving to eat, to drink, and accompanying the guests. 
 
Rashi expounds further. 
  םֶׁהָל רֵמֹוא םי ִתֹו ׁשְו םי ִלְכֹואֶׁ ׁש רַחַאְל " , ֹולֶׁ ׁשִמ םֶׁ ּתְלַכֲאֶׁ ׁש י ִמְל ּוכ ְרָּב 
םֶׁ ּתְלַכֲא י ִ ּלֶׁ ּ ׁשִ ּמֶׁ ׁש ,םֶׁ ּתַא םי ִרּובְס - םֶׁ ּתְלַכֲא םָלֹועָה הָי ָהְו רָמָאֶׁ ׁש י ִמ לֶׁ ּ ׁשִמ ! " 
After his guests would finish eating and drinking  
he would instruct them to bless the provider of their food. 
“Do you think it was I who gave you your food? 
No, it was the One who said and created the world” 
 
Through the realization and acknowledgement  
of Hashem being the Provider of food to the world,  
Avraham Avinu was able to turn the tide  
and bring many back to serving Hashem.

ד"סב 
General Halachos of Bracha Acharona - 3 
How long do we have to say a הנורחא הכרב on food? 
If you ate food and were not full, you should try to bentch right away.  
If you didn’t, you may only make bentch up to 72 minutes after eating. 
(Some hold that in this case if you waited more than a half hour,  
it is preferable to have another  תיזכ of bread for ןוזמה תכרב,  
or food for the other תונורחא הכרב.) 
                
If you ate and are full, you should still bentch within 72 minutes. 
After 72 minutes, you may bentch as long as you are still full. 
However, if after 72 minutes, you are a little hungry,  
you can no longer bentch. 
If it’s past 72 minutes and you are unsure whether you are still full, 
you can bentch,  
however, it is preferable to eat another תיזכ of bread or food for ןוזמה  תכרב,  
or food for the other תונורחא הכרב. 
                
If you had a very long meal and continue to have food or drink, 
you may bentch as long as the meal continues, even 4 or 5 hours later. 
In this case, you should aim to eat or drink every 72 minutes,  
however, if you didn't, you may still say bentch. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 5 - 
ד"סב 
General Halachos of Bracha Acharona - 4 
How long do we have to say a הנורחא הכרב on drink? 
You should always try to say the הנורחא הכרב right away.  
If you didn’t, you may say the הנורחא הכרב  
as long as your thirst is still quenched from the drink. 
If it’s past 30 minutes and you are unsure whether you are still quenched, 
you can say the הנורחא הכרב, but it is best to have something else that will 
require that הנורחא הכרב. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 6 - 
ד"סב 
General Halachos of Bracha Acharona - 5 
If you were required to make a הנורחא הכרב  
and sipped your drink over a period of time, 
as long as there was less than 30 minutes in between sips,  
you can make a הנורחא הכרב at the end. 
If it will be 30 minutes or if you are unsure if it will be 30 minutes 
before the next time you drink,  
you should make a הנורחא הכרב now. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 7 - 
ד"סב 
General Halachos of Bracha Acharona - 6 
If you made a הנורחא הכרב  
and there was food stuck in your teeth, 
you can swallow it without making a bracha. 
                
Similarly, if you made a mistake and recited a הנורחא הכרב  
even though you thought that were going to eat more afterwards, 
you don't need to make a הנושאר הכרב for the food you had in mind.

ד"סב 
The Reward for Bracha Achrona - 1 
After Yosef Hatzadik finished eating, he thanked Hashem for his food.  
One day Potifar asked him what he was saying?  
He replied that he was thanking Hashem for his food.  
Since Yosef fulfilled Birchas Hamazon even while a slave in Mitzrayim,  
he was rewarded  
with being the one to provide food to the whole world! 
)בשיו 'פ ןהכ יתפש(

ד"סב 
ןוזמה תכרב
Layout & Formation of Birchas Hamazon 
The first three תוכרב in ןוזמה תכרב are אתיירואדמ. 
The fourth is ןנברדמ. 
The text that we have today was composed by different great people  
throughout the generations,  
and solidified by Ezra HaSofer and his Bais Din. 
The 1st  הכרב of ןזה (Who provides) 
was composed by Moshe Rabbeinu when the ןמ began to fall. 
While saying this הכרב we should have in mind Hashem’s kindness  
how He provides food for all living creations, tiny to huge,  
across the whole world, every single day, for all generations! 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 11 - 
ד"סב 
Layout & Formation – 2nd הכרב 
The 2nd  הכרב of הדונ (We are thankful) 
was composed by Yehoshua,  
when he and the nation entered into Eretz Yisroel. 
In this brocho, 
Yehoshua listed 7 things for us to be especially thankful to Hashem for. 
Since this is the הכרב of ‘Thanks’,  
we add the special ‘thank you’ of םיסינה לע on Chanukah and Purim. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 12 - 
ד"סב 
Layout & Formation – 3rd הכרב 
The 3rd הכרב of םחר (Have mercy) 
was composed by Dovid Hamelech & Shlomo Hamelech,  
and focuses on Yerushalyim and the Bais Hamikdash.  
Although bentching, and all mitzvohs, should be done with simcha,  
when we mention Hashem having mercy on Klal Yisroel  
and rebuilding of the Bais Hamikdash,  
we should say it with extra emotion. 
We add the word "ןמא" at the end of this הכרב  
to show that this is the end of the תוכרב that are אתיירואדמ, 
and from here forward are ןנברדמ תוכרב. 
We mention הצר and אוביו הלעי in this הכרב  
for these are תוליפת also wherein we ask Hashem for mercy. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 13 - 
ד"סב 
Layout & Formation – 4th הכרב 
The 4th  הכרב of ביטמהו בוטה (The Good, Who does good) 
52 years after the שדקמה תיב was destroyed,  
a Jewish warrior named Bar Kochva from Beitar 
led the Yidden in a revolt against the Romans.  
Bar Kochva had exceptional success.  
Unfortunately, Bar Kochva ended up attributing the success to himself,  
instead of to Hashem.  
As such, Hashem gave the Romans the ability to defeat the city.  
They killed thousands of Yidden in Beitar and did not let them be buried.  
Eventually, when Bnei Yisroel received permission to bury them, 
all the bodies were still fully whole 15 years later! 
In thanks for this tremendous סנ, 
Rabban Gamliel and his Bais Din composed this brocho  
thanking Hashem for this kindness and added it to ןוזמה תכרב. 
Since this הכרב is ןנברדמ  
it does not end with a הכרב to show it is different. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 14 - 
ד"סב 
Layout & Formation – 4th הכרב 
ןמחרה (The Merciful One) 
After doing a הוצמ, it is a good time to daven for personal requests.  
After recognizing all that Hashem does for us  
in the mitzvah of Birchas Hamazon,  
we then are ‘zoche’ to say the Horachamon’s  
requesting various things. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 15 - 
ד"סב 
Say it with Feeling 
Since we say תוכרב frequently, 
we run the risk of saying them by rote.  
It is important to know what we will be saying  
and to understand the words as we say them. 
It is especially important to focus on the introductory words of: 
'ה התא ךורב 
Hashem you are the source of all blessings 
וניקולא 
You are our G-d and how fortunate we are to have such a connection with You 
םלועה ךלמ 
You are the King of the vast universe,  
yet You care about and look after me! 
We should be careful to focus on the meanings of the words, 
and to say them with feeling and emotion 
( א:ה הרורב הנשמ) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 16 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 1 
1) We are required to say Birchas Hamazon 
if we ate a תיזכ of bread (around half a slice of rye bread). 
We should try to eat this amount within 4 minutes, 
however, as long as we ate this amount in the meal, 
we may bentch. 
                
2) If we ate this amount and combined with our meal we are satiated,  
then our bentching becomes אתיירואדמ,  
as the קוספ says "תעבשו" “and you are satisfied”. 
Otherwise, it is ןנברדמ. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 17 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 2 
ןוזמה ת 
כרב
covers all foods we ate in the meal.  
Therefore, even if we made a separate brocho on desert,  
or drank liquids during the meal,  
we do not need to say a separate 
הנור
ח
א הכרב
,  
rather they are ‘covered’ by saying ןוזמה תכרב. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 18 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 3 
We are supposed to say וזמה תכרב
ן 
in the place where we ate. 
(היחמה לע has the same ןיד for Ashkenazim.) 
1) If you intentionally left where you ate, 
you must return to the place where you ate to bentch.  
If you bentched in the second place, you are אצוי.  
2) If you unintentionally left where you ate: 
if you have bread (even less than a זכ
תי ) in the second place,  
you can eat it and bentch there. 
If you don’t have bread in the second place,  
you should return to the place where one ate unless there’s a great need.  
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 19 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 4 
3) Before bentching  
there is a minhag to say a קרפ of םיליהת: 
On days we say ןונחת, we say the קרפ of לבב תורהנ לע. 
On non-ןונחת days we say תולעמה ריש. 
                
4) Many have the הכלה  
to wash their hands with םינורחא םימ (‘the last waters’) before bentching. 
We should wash at least until the second knuckle. 
We may not talk from after םינורחא םימ until after bentching. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 20 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 5 
5) Even though the קוספ says "תכרבו" “And you shall bless” in singular,  
we have a rule of "ךלמ תרדה םע בורב" –  
“The more people doing the הוצמ, the more beauty for the King”.  
Therefore, when 3 or more men eat a ‘washing’ meal together, 
they must make a ןומיז. 
Women who are part of that meal are also required to answer to the ןומיז. 
                
If 10 or more men make up the  ןומיז, the word ‘וניקולא' is added in. 
                
Women on their own can make a ןומיז. 
Even if there are more than 10 women, they would not add in 'וניקולא'. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 21 - 
ד"סב 
Hilchos Pre-Birchas Hamazon - 6 
6) When bentching with a minyan, 
the גהנמ is  
to bentch with the leader holding a cup of wine. 
When three people make a ןומיז,  
it is an enhancement to the הוצמ to use a cup of wine.

ד"סב 
Hilchos Birchas Hamazon - 1 
1) Birchas Hamazon should be said  
while in one place, sitting, and sitting properly (not leaning back etc.) 
                
2) As we are talking to The King, 
we should be dressed appropriately when bentching. 
                
3) We should not do anything else while bentching, or saying any  הכרב, 
including clearing the table, signaling, saying nu nu etc

ד"סב 
STORIES AND INSPIRATION 
The Golden Dove 
Rabba bar Bar Channa once was traveling with a group of non-Jews.  
He ate a meal but forgot to say Birkas Hamazon until they were on the way.  
As the הכלה is that we are to bentch in the place that we ate,  
he said to himself “What should I do?  
If I tell them that I forgot to bless Hashem,  
they will tell me to bless Him over here.  
They will say, ‘Wherever you are when you bless Him, He will be blessed.’  
I had better tell them that I left behind my pure gold statue of a dove.” 
When he told them, they agreed to stop and wait for him.  
He ran back to the place where he ate and said Birchas Hamazon.  
Hashem assisted him,  
and he found in that location a pure gold statue of a dove.  
(Berachos 53b)  
Rabba bar Bar Channa was allowed to say this since the mitzvohs, hence לארשי ינב,  
are compared to a dove, and mitzvos are precious like gold. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 26 - 
ד"סב 
No Warm Slippers 
Harav Ben Tzion Abba Shaul always said Birkas Hamazon  
with a lot of feeling and concentration.  
One snowy Shabbos, Rav Abba Shaul walked through the snow  
to give his shiur in the afternoon.  
When he came back home, his shoes and socks were soaking wet,  
and his feet were freezing cold.  
His family members quickly brought him dry socks and his slippers. 
After he ate Seudah Shelishis, he asked his wife for his shoes. 
“Why do you want them?” she asked. 
“For Birchas Hamazon, of course.” 
“But they’re all wet. Say Birchas Hamazon while wearing your slippers.” 
“What?” Rav Abba Shaul shuddered to hear this.  
“I should say Birchas Hamazon while wearing slippers?” 
The family had no choice,  
and Rav Abba Shaul put on his cold, wet shoes again to say Birchas Hamazon. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 27 - 
ד"סב 
For This I Have Plenty of Time 
Someone was sitting with Rav Yaakov Kaminetzky"l (I believe)  
while he was eating.  
After talking for some time,  
Rav Yaakov hurriedly finished the conversation and excused himself  
because he had to bentch and go.  
The person watched as Rav Yaakov then bentched slowly and carefully. 
Although Rav Yaakov was in a rush,  
he cheshboned on the amount of time to bentch properly,  
as if he had all the time available. 
(Heard from Rabbi Aaron Shmuel Wealcatch) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 28 - 
ד"סב 
Bentching for Life
Reb Shimon was an elderly Yid who lived in Yerushalayim.  
When he was young (before the Holocaust), he lived in Lublin.  
One day, Rav Meir Shapiro zt'l came to his cheder to test the talmidim.  
Sometimes, after testing the children, they would receive a treat. 
This time Rav Meir told them, 
"Instead of a gift, I will tell you a wonderful segulah.  
If you follow my advice, you will be successful and always lead a tranquil life. 
“It is brought down that one who is careful in bentching  
is especially beloved to Hakadosh Baruch Hu.” 
“Secondly, the Bach states, if you look through bentching,  
you will notice that every letter in the Alef Beis is represented there,  
except for one, the “final fey” “ף”.  
There is a reason for this: 
The word “ףא”, which means anger, ends in a fey.  
The word “ףצק”, which means fury, also ends with this letter.  
We don’t mention things that could indirectly be referring to the ‘anger’ of 'ה.  
If you bentch with kavanah, you will be zoche  
to never see the ףא or ףצק of Hakadosh Baruch Hu directed against you.” 
He also told them the Be'er Heitav that we should always be careful  
to say birchas hamazon from a siddur and not by heart." 
“Finally, the Sefer Hachinuch says that anyone who bentches with kavanah  
will have no difficulties with parnassah his entire life.” 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 29 - 
ד"סב 
Reb Shimon took the lesson to heart.  
He decided that he would be very careful with birchas hamazon.  
He kept this kabalah throughout his entire life.  
In cheder, his friends would bench quickly and go out to play,  
while he lingered behind, in order to bench properly.  
It was challenging,  
but he didn’t want to abandon this wonderful gift that Rav Shapiro gave him. 
A few years later, the Nazis invaded Poland.  
Reb Shimon was standing in line for a 'selektzia.'  
Whoever was short was sent to the left for execution.  
Reb Shimon prayed that he should be saved from 'wrath and destruction'  
in the merit of his birchas hamazon.  
When it was his turn, he stood on his toes to appear higher;  
the Nazi signaled him to the right.  
His life was saved in the merit of birchas hamazon. 
Soon afterwards, Reb Shimon found himself standing in line again.  
This time, each person had to tell the Nazi what he could did for parnassah.  
Reb Shimon didn’t know what he could say;  
he was only a teenager, taken away from yeshiva. 
As he waited in line he davened,  
"In the merit of birchas hamazon, which is mesugal for parnassah, Hashem, 
please help me…" 
All of a sudden, the person behind him tapped him on the shoulder and said, 
"Tell the Nazi that you are a cook and that I'm your helper."  
He said this and they were both sent to work in the kitchen.  
As the Chinuch promised, because he was careful with birchas hamazon,  
he was sent to work in the kitchen, where he had enough food to eat. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 30 - 
ד"סב 
Even in the camps, Reb Shimon continued to be careful with birchas hamazon. 
For example, if he understood that he wouldn’t have enough time to say  
birchas hamazon properly, then he wouldn’t eat bread to begin with. 
Once, a Nazi saw Shimon working in the kitchen and he wasn't pleased that he 
had this 'good' position, surrounded with food. 
"What are you doing here? You look like a young child." 
"I work here," Shimon explained. "I am a cook." 
The Nazi took Shimon outside and showed him a stony area, outside the kitchen. 
"Dig a two meter ditch here.  
You have two hours to finish, or you will be buried in it."  
With that, he gave him a tiny shovel. 
The task the Nazi gave Shimon was absolutely impossible! 
The ground was covered with heavy stones, he only had two hours to finish, 
and he was given only a small shovel to work with. 
Reb Shimon raised his eyes to heaven and said,  
"I say birchas hamazon with kavanah, please save me! 
I was saved before. Please save me again in this merit." 
A few moments later a German jeep drove by, filled with Nazis. 
Seeing this young lad trying to dig a ditch with a small shovel, they began to 
tease him, throwing tomatoes, potatoes, carrots, and other vegetables at him. 
Reb Shimon thought,  
"I see that in the merit of birchas hamazon, I again received abundance.  
Now I need the blessings for protection from wrath and destruction as well." 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 31 - 
ד"סב 
Shortly afterwards a jeep with Polish soldiers showed up.  
When they saw the vegetables around Shimon, they stopped to ask for them. 
With an authoritative voice, Shimon told them,  
"When there is a pit two meters deep here, I will give you the vegetables." 
The soldiers had the right tools for digging.  
They took them out of their jeep, and began digging the trench.  
There were several of them, so they were able to finish the job in a half-hour. 
The Nazi returned and saw that the task was completed.  
He said, "I always knew that G-d takes care of you.  
I just didn’t realize to what extent."  
Shimon was saved once again, in the merit of birchas hamazon. 
Shimon returned to his job at the kitchen in Auschwitz.  
Through incredible nissim, he was zoche to survive the war.  
He moved to Eretz Yisrael where he built a beautiful family.  
Throughout his life, he always had a parnassah  
and was able to support his married children as well. 
Shimon would always tell over the story  
of his miraculous, food-themed, survival of the war  
and of his subsequent financial hatzlacha,  
always attributing it to his being careful in birchas hamazon  
as he had been taught by R’ Meir Shapiro so many years before. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 32 - 
ד"סב 
Look inside 
Reb Mattisyahu Solomon, zt”l, once spent a day observing Rav Shach.   
Every person who came to Rav Shach, regardless of the reason for their visit, 
was given the same הצע- "Bentch from inside a Bentcher."   
One of the people who came that day was an בושח םדא,  
and was noticeably disappointed with what Rav Shach told him.   
Rav Shach said,  
"You know, Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzensky was a big Gaon. 
He had a tremendous memory,  
and even so he used to look inside when he bentched..."   
(As heard from Reb Shmuel Felder, Shlit"a, by Rabbi Shimmy Sternfield) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 33 - 
ד"סב 
Write It Down 
Rav Shach once told someone to bentch from inside a bentcher.   
When the man returned and told Rav Shach that his situation hadn't improved, 
Rav Shach asked him if he had been bentching inside.   
The man replied that he had been,  
except once when he was on the plane and didn't have a bentcher. 
"A weak Kabbalah," said Rav Shach. "How did you bentch?" 
"I bentched by heart." 
"You know the words of bentching,  
so why didn't you write them down and bentch inside!" 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 34 - 
ד"סב 
V’lo Nikashel 
A man in Eretz Yisroel was bentching out loud and said at the end of םחר  
as per his minhag "דֶׁעָו םָלֹועְל לֵ ׁשָ ּכִנ אֹלְו םֵלָ ּכִנ אֹלְו ׁ שֹובֵנ אֹ ּלֶׁ ׁש".  
An irreligious looking lady next to him said  
"there's no  לֵ ׁשָ ּכִנ - its only “ 
םֵלָ ּכִנ 
אֹלְו ׁ שֹובֵנ אֹ ּלֶׁ ׁש
". 
He was surprised that she knew this and she said that she used to be frum. 
That day he went to a library that had old manuscripts  
and found an original text that had his nusach.  
He made himself a copy of the nusach.  
The next day he found her (they ate lunch in the same area) and showed her.  
She was surprised and grateful that he took the time. 
Years later he got a letter in the mail with a wedding invitation from that lady. 
She explained that at the time that they spoke  
she was about to sadly marry an Arab she had met at University.  
She was inspired by this person bentching  
and she began to think how he was showing her the words "  לֵ ׁשָ ּכִנ אֹלְו " –  
which Chazal tell us means we should not stumble off the path of Yiddishkeit. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 35 - 
ד"סב 
Realizing that this was a sign from Hashem,  
she took the message to heart and decided to cancel her engagement.  
The Arab showed his true colors and began to be violent and she ran away. 
She slowly started to return to Yiddishkeit  
and is now marrying a properly religious boy. 
Enclosed with the letter was an invitation to her wedding. 
All in the Zechus of him saying a bentching clearly and out loud. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 36 -

ד"סב 
The הכרב of שלש ןיעמ (commonly known as היחמה לע)  
is an abridged version of  ןוזמה תכרב,  
and incorporates ideas from its’ first three תוכרב,  
hence the name 'שלש ןיעמ' – ‘a sampling of three’. 
                
שלש ןיעמ is said after eating/drinking the required amount of: 
• Non-bread foods from the 5 types of grain: wheat, barley, spelt, oats, & rye 
(that are baked, cooked, or fried. Roasted, however, would be תושפנ ארוב) 
• The fruits from the םינימה תעבש: grapes, figs, pomegranates, olives, dates. 
• Juice/wine made from grapes. 
The הכרב of תושפנ ארוב is said after all other (non-bread) food items. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 44 - 
ד"סב 
Starting & Ending Nusach of  שלש ןיעמ 
Grain 
Fruit 
Starting  
Nusach 
Wine 
היחמה לע 
הלכלכה לעו 
ץעה  
ירפ לעו ץעה לע
ןפגה ירפ לעו ןפגה לע 
Ending  
Nusach 
היחמה לעו ץראה לע 
)הלכלכה לעו( 
תוריפה לעו ץראה לע 
ןפגה ירפ לעו ץראה לע 
On fruits from Eretz Yisroel: 
היתוריפ לעו ץראה לע 
On wine from Eretz Yisroel: 
הנפג ירפ לעו ץראה לע 
When eating from more than one of these categories,  
the nusach for the הכרב is combined. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 45 - 
ד"סב 
Halochos of שלש ןיעמ - 1 
If you ate fruits from the םינימה תעבש along with other fruits,  
the הכרב of שלש ןיעמ counts for all,  
and you do not say a separate תושפנ ארוב on the fruits. 
Similarly, if you drank wine along with other drinks,  
ןפגה לע counts for all,  
and you do not make a separate תושפנ ארוב on the drinks. 
                
If you said תושפנ ארוב instead of שלש ןיעמ,  
you are not אצוי. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 46 - 
ד"סב 
Halochos of שלש ןיעמ - 2 
For the הכרב of שלש ןיעמ,  
you should go back to the place where you ate 
and the הכרב should be said sitting down.  
For  תושפנ ארוב 
you do not need to back to the place where you ate.  
Although you do not need to sit down for תושפנ ארוב,  
the הכרב, like all תוכרב, should be made while stationary. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com  - 47 -

 שלש ןיעמ תכרב 
This brocho is called  שלָׁש ןיֵעֵמ because it is a summary of the first three brochos of   ב  ר  כ  ה ת ָׁמוֹז ן . 
 
Thank You Hashem for the goodness You give us and for Eretz Yisroel.  
Please have mercy on us and rebuild the Bais Hamikdash.

Halochos of תושפנ ארוב 
תושפנ ארוב is made after eating the required amount of foods  
that are not from the 
םינימה ת 
עבש
and don’t grow from the ground,  
and drinks aside from wine and grape juice. 
                    
One תושפנ ארוב suffices  
if we ate and drank foods and drinks  
that would independently require a תושפנ ארוב.
