ד"סב 
תיציצ 
HOLDING תיציצ IN DAVENING: רמאש ךורב - 1 
At the end of רמאש ךורב some hold their תיציצ. Why?  
One of the  םיקוספ discussing the mitzvah of תיציצ states, 
"ךל השעת םילידג" - “You shall make for yourself twisted threads”. 
The אירטמג of the word םי ל ד  ג is 87, 
hinting that when we recite Baruch She’amar, which contains 87 words, 
we should hold our תיציצ. 
What is the specific connection between רמאש ךורב and  תיציצ? 
See upcoming Lu.L.L.A.Y.’s 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    52 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
HOLDING תיציצ IN DAVENING: רמאש ךורב - 2 
At the end of רמאש ךורב some hold their תיציצ. Why?  
Why only two? 
In the first part of Baruch She’amar we say Baruch (“blessed”) 10 times. 
(excluding when it is said as an added praise when mentioning Hashem, such as Baruch Hu) 
These 10 correspond  
to the 10  תורמאמ (utterances) with which Hashem created the world,  
as well as to the 
תורבדה
תרשע
, the bedrock of the הרות. 
As we prepare to sing praises to 'ה with the opening words of הרמזד יקוספ,  
we hold the two front tzitzis which contain five knots each,  
together making ten knots,  
reminding us of Hashems Mastery of the world and His הרות. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    53 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
HOLDING תיציצ IN DAVENING: רמאש ךורב - 3 
At the end of רמאש ךורב some kiss the front two תיציצ. Why?  
Why only two? 
Each set of תיציצ has eight strings and five knots, adding up to 13.  
When we gather the two front fringes together, we have 26,  
which is the אירטמג of Hashem’s Name,  
This reminds us as we enter into הרמזד יקוספ,  
not just to say the words but to focus on Hashem,  
Whom we are saying them to.  
(Rav Schwab on Prayer) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    54 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
HOLDING תיציצ IN DAVENING:  עמש תאירק 
When we say the words ץראה תופנכ עבראמ in the הכרב of  הבר הבהא 
and ask 'ה to gather of from the four corners of the earth,  
some gather all the תיציצ together, also known as תופנכ עברא,  
and hold them in the left hand between the pinkey and ring finger.  
(Some hold this is a hefsek and don’t gather them) 
When we begin saying רמאיו, the השרפ which discusses the הוצמ of תיציצ, 
we take the  תיציצ also into the right hand,  
kissing them when the word 'תיציצ' is said  
and placing them over the eyes when saying ותוא םתיארו.  
(The הרורב הנשמ says placing over the eyes is a הלוגס for sight.) 
They are also kissed when saying תמא and דעל in ביציו תמא.

תיציצ 
Buckle up your tzitzis! 
There was a talmid who was struggling with the mitzvah of tzitzis. 
His parents tried many incentives but he didn’t ‘bite’. 
They asked him to wear them one more day, afterwards they wouldn’t push. 
The boy agreed and headed off to yeshiva. 
That day, on the way (not sure to or from yeshiva) there was a terrible storm. 
Due to the torrential rain and winds,  
the bus driver lost control and went straight into a tree.  
Upon impact the windshield shattered and the driver was injured.  
When Hatzolo arrived on the scene they were made aware  
that there was still a talmid in the back of the bus.  
They went back to rescue this talmid who should have been thrown across 
the bus from the impact, but he was still in his seat…  
stuck to it, because his tzitzis got caught in the seat,  
locking him into place like a seat belt and protecting him from harm  
(Heard from Rabbi Moshe Liebowitz) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    56 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
For just a few kopeks 
At the end of his life, the Vilna Ga’on was lying in a bed and crying.   
One of the people standing near him came close to him and asked  
“Rebbi, why are you crying?” 
The Ga’on picked up his תיציצ and began to cry harder and said  
“In this world for just a few kopeks (cents) you can buy a pair תיציצ,  
wear it all day, and get rewarded for every moment that you are wearing it.   
Where I am going, no money in the world can give me even one more הוצמ, 
even with effort I cannot reach to a greater level!” 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    57 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
Shem and Yefes covered Noach, their father, when he became drunk and unclothed.  
As a reward they both received a type of a covering in the future.  
Shem’s grandchildren were given the mitzvah of tzitzis.   
Yefes’ descendants in the war of of גוגמו גוג will merit to be buried.  
Chom, who degraded his father, was punished with his descendants becoming slaves. 
The Shem Mishmuel enlightens us on the three sons and their differences. 
Chom was unfazed and unabashed by his father being unclothed, as he related to man 
no differently than to an animal. Just as animals do not wear clothing, neither do 
humans need clothing. Therefore, he was punished to be a slave to serve his fellow 
man, just as animals function for the service of man. 
Yefes recognized the beauty and dignity of man and felt it was out of place for man to 
be unclothed. Therefore, his reward was to be accorded the proper respect for men to 
be buried, and not lay as carcasses in the open field. 
Shem recognized something deeper.  
He saw the neshama inside man and his inner spiritual beauty. He recognized that the 
body of a human is like a garment to the soul. Not only was it undignified for Noach to 
be unclothed, and a disgrace to the holy neshama that’s inside, but he also recognized 
that the body, the housing for the neshama, is holy and needs to be covered.  
This prompted him to jump and initiate the covering of his father.  
In line with his action, Shem was rewarded with tzitzis, a spiritual mitzvah  
that transforms and gives a higher purpose to the physical garment they are on. 
Tzitzis reminds us to use our bodies, the clothing for our true essence, our neshama, 
for its’ higher purpose. 
(Rabbi Baruch Bodenheim) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    58 
ד"סב 
תיציצ 
Why optional? 
Rav Chaim Pinchos Scheinberg ל"צז was well known  
for his hiddur in wearing many garments of תיציצ.  
We have learned from all that above that  
תיציצ is an extremely special  הוצמ which has so much in it! 
If so, why did Hashem make it optional –  
one only needs to wear תיציצ if they are wearing a four cornered garment? 
(םימכח instituted the הוצמ of wearing a ןטק תילט being that it is so special) 
As תיציצ represents all the תווצמ, Hashem specifically made it optional, 
teaching us that the true way to come close to Hashem and his הרות,  
is by exercising our הריחב, free choice, and making the right decisions in life. 
Even the תוצמ we are obligated to do,  
we should be doing because we want to, not because we must. 
May we be zoche. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    59 
ד"סב 
השיבל רדס 
Clothes makes the man/woman 
םירצמ 
תולג
was extremely difficult, with לארשי ינב being enslaved  
to the most powerful and influential country in the world. 
Even so, ל"זח tell us, one of the ways that לארשי ללכ stood apart was in 
their manner of dress. This safeguarded לארשי ינב to the point that the 
Torah itself testifies )רבדמב ףוס( that aside from one exception out of millions 
of people, there was no promiscuity and intermarriage with the Egyptians, 
Knowing that we are the children of the םישודקה תוהמא & תובא, 
assisted them and us to maintain the dignity and self-value  
throughout תולג of being a proud member of לארשי ללכ. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    60 
ד"סב 
השיבל רדס 
The whole world is filled with His glory. 
Our body was formed and given to us by Hakadosh Baruch Hu. 
Even when it is dark and/or in private,  
e.g. a bedroom or bathroom,  
there are halochos of תועינצ that apply. 
These תוכלה are not due to our bodies being an object to be ashamed of,  
rather of an acute awareness that ודובכ ץראה לכ אלמ,  
the whole world is filled with His glory. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    61 
ד"סב 
השיבל רדס 
Do it right 
Throughout the הרות, and with regard to many  תוצמ,  
the right side is given more תובישח. 
הלבק יפ לע, the left side represents ןיד,  
while the right side represents םימחר.  
Understandably, we want the םימחר to rule over the ןיד. 
This concept is not just in תווצמ but applies to many areas,  
including getting dressed. 
When getting dressed,  
the right side should be dressed first and then the left. 
When getting undressed,  
the left side should be removed first, then the right. 
(Due to this concept, some are particular that the flap covering the buttons 
should be on the right side with the buttons on the left) 
Giving precedence to the right side for these halochos is the same  
even if one is a ‘lefty’. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    62 
ד"סב 
םילענ - השיבל רדס 
‘Hilchos Shoes’ - the greatness of Yiddishkeit 
An officer in the Russian government,  
in an attempt to belittle the Jewish nation,  
told the Czar how foolish the Jewish rules are.  
“They are so detailed 
they even have rules telling them how to tie their shoes!” 
The Czar in his wisdom replied,  
that having rules even for shoes,  
actually shows the greatness of Yiddishkeit,  
for it is not just random rules that are applied at random times,  
rather it is a lifestyle that is governed  
at all times by the rules of their Torah. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    63 
ד"סב 
םילענ -  השיבל רדס 
Tie them right left 
The א ר מ ג brings a ת ק ול ח מ which shoe should be put on first –  
the right or the left. 
One ר מ א ד ןא מ holds the right shoe is put on first, 
since the right is always more important in Yiddishkeit. 
The other   ר מ א ד ןא מ holds that just like ןי לי פ  ת is put on the left hand, 
we should first put the shoe on the left foot. 
(More on the shoe/ןיליפת connection in next Lu.L.L.A.Y ה"עב) 
We pasken that we fulfill both of these – 
we put on the right shoe first, but don’t tie it, 
then we put on the left shoe, tie it, and then tie the right shoe. 
Lefties, who put ןיליפת on their right hand, have a different order,  
“Righties”: 
Put on right shoe 
Put on left shoe 
Tie the left shoe 
Tie the right shoe 
“Lefties”: 
Put on right shoe 
Tie the right shoe 
Put on left shoe 
Tie the left shoe 
When removing shoes, we should take off the left one first. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    64 
ד"סב 
םילענ - השיבל רדס 
Why do we put on and tie our shoes in such a manner? 
Although we usually give importance to the right side first,  
by shoes we tie the left first, since ןי לי פ  ת is put on the left hand. 
By other  תוצמ we don’t have this deference.  
What is this special connection between shoes and ןי לי פ  ת  
that we say since ןי לי פ  ת is on the left, we tie the left shoe first? 
HaRav Akiva Eiger ל" צ ז ( ע"וש לע א"ער תוהגה) explains in the name of the ך ל מ ה ק מ ע: 
After the war of the 4 kings against the 5 kings,  
the king of םודס offered   וני ב א ם ה ר ב א a part of the spoils of war. 
 
וני ב א ם ה ר ב א refused to take anything, for he did not want anyone to think  
that they are responsible for his wealth, rather it is all only from Hashem. 
In the  ת וכ ז of ם ה ר ב א refusing with saying  
"ל ע נ ך ור ש
the ל א ר ש י י נ  ב were  ה כ וז to two תו ו צ מ. 
ד ע ט וח מ ם א"
,  
Because of “ט וח מ ם א - from a thread”,  
we were given the ה ו צ מ of תי צי צ & ת ל כ  ת, which are threads. 
Because of  "ל ע נ ך ור ש ד ע" – or a shoestrap,  
we were given the ה ו צ מ of ןי לי פ  ת ת וע וצ ר – the straps of the ןי לי פ  ת. 
Since we were  ה כ וז to  ןי לי פ  ת ת וע וצ ר in the ת וכ ז of ם ה ר ב א refusing "ל ע נ ךֹור ש ד ע" 
“not even a shoestrap”, the two became automatically connected. 
Therefore, just like ןי לי פ  ת is placed on the left hand, we commemorate and give 
ת ובי ש ח to the left shoe strap (lace) which is the source for  ןי לי פ  ת ת וע וצ ר. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    65

םילענ - השיבל רדס 
Saved by his shoes - 1 
There was a Yid who unfortunately was engaged to marry a non-Jew.  
On the day of his wedding, as he was getting dressed,  
he realized that instinctively he put on his right shoe,  
then his left shoe,  
then tied his left,  
and then tied his right.  
Shocked and realizing that halocho was still rooted within him,  
he quickly rethought his life and what he was about to do. 
On the spot, he ran out of the wedding hall, never to return to the hall, 
instead to a full return to his Jewish heritage.     
(Heard from Rabbi Moshe Liebowitz) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.edu@gmail.com    66 
ד"סב 
םילענ - השיבל רדס 
Saved by his shoes - 2 
A soldier was in the process of becoming religious. 
He wanted to take upon himself a halocho 
that he could keep easily while in the army. 
He decided to keep the halocho of 
putting on and tying his shoes in the proper order. 
One morning, he realized that he put on his shoes wrong 
and decided to start over from scratch. 
As army boots can take some time to lace and unlace 
he was delayed in getting out and to his unit. 
His unit, that left without him, was on a helicopter mission, 
which unfortunately crashed, claiming the lives of those on board. 
His adherence and determination to keep this halocho, saved his life.

ד"סב 
OVERVIEW & GUIDE 
Lu.L.L.A.Y. on Middos & Menchlechkeit can be utilized as an independent limud, or as a 6 
week+ program covering five aspects of middos and menchlechkeit. 
The booklet contains insights , source material, stories, programs, and songs. It is advisable to 
review the lesson in advance before giving it over to the talmidim/os. 
Our mascot “Anan” (which is in the process of being copyrighted beH) is present throughout the 
booklet. In the shared folder, there are many middos scenarios for display on bulletin boards, 
based on each week. Please do not create or share ‘Anan’ without express permission. 
Introduction:  
Rebbe/Morah role 
• Discuss the Ideas, concepts, and stories, introducing the importance of good middos. 
Week 1 – See the Good: Focusing on seeing good middos and good in others.  
Rebbe/Morah role 
• Discuss the concept of Hakoras Hatov and how it is essential to being a Yid. 
• Encourage talmidim/os to notice good that others do. 
• Encourage appreciating what Hashem constantly does for us by making brochos properly 
including Birchos Hashachar (printable card in folder). 
Projects: 
• Talmidim/os receive cards (for a raffle) to fill out when they see someone doing something 
nice. Some of these can be put into the yeshiva newsletter and displayed on the bulletin board. 
• English teachers assign a secret buddy to each talmid/ah, whom they will track and observe 
throughout the week and document the nice things the person does. 
Week 2 – Greeting Others 
Rebbe/Morah role: 
• Encourage saying of please, thank you’s, good morning, have a nice day etc. to parents, siblings, 
bus drivers, custodians, school guard etc. 
• Discuss utilizing our power of speech to make a kiddush Hashem wherever we go.  
Projects:  
• Select individuals (lunch attendant etc.) have cards to give out to talmidim/os when they hear 
them say please, thank you etc. Raffle following. 
• Create a list of people we come across and should greet. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   4 
ד"סב 
Week 3 – Thinking About Others 
Rebbe/Morah role:  
• Discuss the concept of  ינירחאל בטימל אלא ארבנ ומצעל אל along with the many gemachs 
and chessed organizations that Klal Yisroel has. 
• Encourage others to do for others even when it is difficult – hold open the door for others (of 
course the talmid/ah should say thank you), sharing snack when someone doesn’t have, etc. 
Projects:  
• Work with your talmidim on putting together a list of as many chessed organizations your 
class (and parents) can think of. Create one for within your class or school. 
• Put together a list of easy ways I can help others (fill up washing up etc.). 
• Food drive for Chessed 24/7 or Tomchei Shabbos 
• Trip to help at Tomchei Shabbos / Nursing home etc. 
Week 4 – Neat & Clean 
Rebbe/Morah role:  
• Discuss why a talmid chochom who has a stain is  'התימ בייח' and why it is important to look 
neat and clean. 
• Encourage keeping the bedroom, home, classroom, hallways, and bathrooms clean – even if 
we didn’t make the mess. Cleaning lady/Janitors job is not to be your slave.  
Projects:  
• CCC – Clean Classroom Contest – classroom, desks, lockers 
• I helped clean up at home program – dishes, playroom etc. 
Week 5 – I Am A Prince 
Rebbe/Morah role:  
• Discuss what it means to be a  שודק יוגו םינהכ תכלממ.  
• We say  ונכלמ וניבא – if my father is a King, that means I am a prince. 
• Encourage prince-like behavior – kindness to others, looking neat, not pushing, not running 
to food, eating sitting down etc. 
Projects:  
• Talmidim put together a list of ways to behave royally. 
• Upper grade skit 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   5

ד"סב 
ץרא ךרד IS  הרותל המדק 
The first Rashi on Chumash asks 
“If the purpose of the הרות is the תווצמ, 
why did 'ה start the הרות with the creation of the world 
instead of beginning the הרות with the first הוצמ of שדוחה שודק?” 
The ב"יצנ in his introduction to רבד קמעה answers: 
הרותל המדק ץרא ךרד is not just a catch phrase but rather a fact. 
Before we can accept תוצמ and the הרות at יניס רה,  
we first must learn what it means to be a mentch. 
Chumash תישארב is an integral part of being a Yid, and can’t be ‘skipped’ 
for within we learn so much about proper middos: 
humility from  'ה saying םדא השענ, 
how to be a  חנ and not a דורמנ, 
an םהרבא and not a טול, ךלמיבא and a הערפ, 
a  קחצי and not a לאעמשי and a לאותב, 
a בקעי and not an ושע and a ןבל,  
about the הדימ of jealousy and how to care for others 
and so much more. 
Once these middos and menchlechkeit are internalized,  
then, and only then, we can move on to accept and keep the הרות properly.
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   7 
ד"סב 
What Makes a Gadol 
A group of bochurim cam to Rav Elyashiv asking him for an eitzah or a segulah 
of how to merit ahavas hatorah and hasmadah. 
They expected him to share a particular approach or style, 
but he surprised them. 
Rav Elyashiv told them: 
The human being who merited soaring to the utmost heights 
of Torah was Moshe Rabbeinu. 
In order to answer your question,  
it is worth contemplating what he did to merit this gift. 
"The Torah does not tell us of Moshe Rabbeinu’s brilliance, nor of his hasmada.  
What the Torah does tell us about him is: 
)ג 
:בי רבדמב(
הָמ ָדֲאָה יֵנ ְ פ לַע רֶׁ ׁשֲא ם ָדָאָה לֹ כ ִמ דֹאְמ ויָנ ָע
הֶׁ ׁשֹמ ׁ שי ִאָהְו
“And the man Moshe was exceedingly humble,  
more than any other person on the face of the earth” 
Rashi explains that this was a humility that expressed itself  
in submission to and respect for every other person. 
“This”, said Rav Elyashiv, “was the unique feature of Moshe Rabbeinu,  
the greatest leader we have ever had, 
and the background to his accomplishments Torah.” 
(A Heart for Another, Rav Yaakov Bender, pg 101)

Advice For A Lifetime

ד"סב 
 Middos are the way we act,  
and how we think and feel  
(Marvelous Middos Machine by Abie Rotenberg)  
It’s true, middos are the way we act and how we think and feel. 
But it actually is much more than that. 
The meforshim explain: 
the שדוקה ןושל word הדמ refers to our character traits.  
At the same time it also refers to the measurement of an item.  
These two are the same שרוש, for they are greatly intertwined. 
The measure of who we are, our true status and greatness, 
is not dependent on our material wealth, nor our knowledge level.  
It is based on who we are as a person,  
how we act, and on the measure of our character traits. 
So yes,  
Middos are the way we act,  
and how we think and feel, 
but they are truly so much more!

ד"סב 
The Prospective Shidduch 
Rav Shach was once approached by a father 
asking him about two prospective matches that were suggested for his daughter. 
One suggestion was of a bochur  
who was a wonderful learner but his middos were not that great; 
the other had wonderful middos, but his learning was not so great. 
Rav Shach responded that of course he should look into the one with good middos,  
as eventually his middos with the right wife will bring him to greater levels of learning. 
However, the one without the good middos, his learning is severely lacking,  
as middos are the foundation for Torah. 
          
Rav Ephraim Wachsman once said: 
If a bochur has good middos, but doesn’t learn, 
he has one maalah and one chisaron. 
However, if a bochur is a good learner, but doesn’t have good middos, 
he has two chisronos!!!

ד"סב 
No! You Can’t Join! 
If a  ירצמ would like to convert to Yiddishekeit,  
even though his ancestors tortured and killed our forefathers, he can. 
If an יקלמע, upon whom there is a הוצמ to wipe out,  
would like to convert to Yiddishekeit, he can. 
If an SS commander would like to convert to Yiddishekeit, he can. 
If a male from  ןומא or באומ would like to convert to Yiddishekeit, he cannot! 
Why can the ירצמ, and the יקלמע, and the Nazi convert,  
while the ינומא and  יבאומ cannot? 
The הרות tells us that when לארשי ללכ were traveling through the רבדמ,  
the people of Amon and Moav did not greet the  לארשי ינב with bread and water. 
Additionally, they were involved in the plot with  םעלב  
to undermine the השודק of  לארשי ללכ.  
This was a direct lack of בוטה תרכה for their forefather טול  
being saved by our forefather וניבא םהרבא.  
The הרות is telling us – you can be mean and cruel, yet still join us, 
however, if in your DNA you are lacking fundamental בוטה תרכה,  
you have no place among the Jewish nation!

ד"סב 
Appreciate Hashem’s gifts 
ונבר השמ did not perform the first three תוכמ  
out of  בוטה תרכה for the river that protected him when he was an infant,  
and for the ground that ‘helped’ him when he killed the ירצמ. 
Rav Chaim Shmuelevitz explains 
The river and ground would not have cried if השמ struck them,  
so why couldn’t he perform the first three תוכמ? 
ונבר 
השמ
didn’t perform these  תוכמ for the purpose of his middos development, 
for striking an object that helped him,  
would display a lack of בוטה תרכה to that item. 
Wow! If one shows בוטה תרכה to objects which have no feelings or free will,  
we surely need to show appreciation to people for what they do for us. 
And if we need to show appreciation for people,  
how much more so do we need to show appreciation to Hashem,  
for everything is from Him. 
Properly making brochos on food or תווצמ,  
is one of the ways we show appreciation  
and inculcate within ourselves the הדמ of בוטה תרכה.

ד"סב 
Birchos Hashachar 
Chazal in their wisdom,  
began our mornings with various תוכרב  
which put us in the mind frame of appreciation.  
With the הכרב of םידי תליטנ לע, we thank Hashem  
for the opportunity to raise up our hands for a higher purpose. 
We then thank Him in the תוכרב of רצי רשא and המשנ יקולא  
for the amazing body he gave us, with all of its innumerable miracles within,  
and for our precious  המשנ that He put inside it. 
We move on to הרותה תוכרב (some say this before המשנ יקולא) 
thanking Hashem for His most precious gift, the Torah,  
and for choosing us to be His special nation. 
The 15 רחשה תוכרב focus on more gifts that Hashem gives us. 
Yes, life has its challenges, but ל"זח are telling us: 
focus on the gifts that you do have, appreciate them,  
and it will completely transform your day.

ד"סב 
Thank You! I’ll Say It Myself 
Many years ago many people were illiterate and unable to daven.  
In order to enable them to be  אצוי shemoneh esray, 
ל"זח, with the הכלה of הנועכ עמוש instituted  ץ"שה תרזח,  
wherein the  ןזח says each  הכרב aloud,  
with intention to be איצומ those who were unable to daven on their own. 
There is one anomaly to ץ"שה תרזח; 
if the ןזח is being  איצומ the רובצ,  
why is there a separate םידומ, that each individual says (known as ןנברד םידומ)? 
The Avudraham explains: 
I can have someone be  איצומ me with a הכרב praising 'ה,  
I can have someone be  איצומ me with a הכרב asking for health & parnassa,  
but I cannot have someone be איצומ me with gratitude,  
for gratitude has to come from within me  
and I cannot fulfill that obligation through someone else, 
hence the separate הכרב of ןנברד םידומ.

ד"סב 
A Rainy Day Funeral 
It was a rainy winter day and Rav Shach called his grandson, 
“I want you to hire a cab to take me to a funeral in Chaifa.” 
They got in the cab together as the grandson thought 
“there must be some adam gadol in Chaifa that my grandfather knew.” 
When they arrived, lo and behold, it was the levaya of an old woman. 
There was barely a minyan, and the grandson couldn’t figure out why his grandfather 
would go to this woman’s levaya. 
Following the levaya in the funeral parlor,  
Rav Shach went along to the cemetery even though it was pouring rain.  
He waited until the woman was buried, and then recited Kaddish for her.  
He then stood there in the rain over the grave.  
The grandson was amazed; he couldn’t figure it out.  
When they went back into the car, Rav Shach did not say anything.  
Finally, the grandson asked his grandfather: “Who was this woman?” 
Rav Shach explained:  
When I was in Yeshiva in Europe, it was not like today with a dormitory.  
The boys learned in a shul.  
The “dormitory” was the benches of the shul and there was a seniority system.  
The older bochurim and the ones who were in the “yeshiva” for longer  
were more likely to have a bench to sleep on.  
I was the youngest bochur there,  
so I did not get a bench, rather I slept on the floor.  
In Europe, in the cold Lithuanian winter nights, it was freezing cold. 
I decided “I can’t take this anymore!”  
One day, I received a letter from an uncle offering me to take over his business.  
I had decided that I was going to take this job!  
I just could not take the freezing cold any longer. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   21 
ד"סב 
That night, a woman, whose husband had just died, got up from shiva.  
Her husband had owned a blanket factory  
and she came into the shul offering blankets from the factory. 
“Does anyone here need blankets?”  
I responded “Yes. I need a blanket.”  
With the blankets on the floor and the blankets on top of me,  
my sleeping accommodations became ‘bearable’. 
I decided to stay — and became who I am today!” 
Rav Shach told his grandson:  
“This woman from Chaifa was the woman  
who gave me the blankets in Lithuania so many years ago.  
Without this woman, there would be no Rav Shach,  
no Avi Ezri, no Ponevezh Rosh Yeshiva, no nothing!  
I kept track of this woman, even though this was decades later!  
So, when I heard she was nifteres, I felt I had to go to her funeral. 
The grandson turned to his grandfather,  
“I now understand why you went to the levaya. 
I also understand why you stayed for the kevura and said Kaddish.  
But why did you remain out there standing over the grave in the rain  
after the burial was all over?”  
Rav Shach answered,  
“I wanted to remember what it felt like to be cold.  
I wanted to fully appreciate what she did for me so many years earlier.  
That is why I stayed out there.” 
Hakoras Hatov – an integral part of being a Torah Jew.

ד"סב 
Don’t Walk On By 
A man sat at a metro station in Washington DC began to play the violin.  
During that time, since it was rush hour, it was calculated that thousands of people 
went through the station, most of them on their way to work.  
Three minutes went by and a man noticed there was musician playing.  
He stopped for a few seconds and then hurried up to meet his schedule.  
A minute later, the violinist received his first dollar tip:  
a woman threw the money in the till and without stopping continued to walk.  
A few minutes later, someone leaned against the wall to listen to him, but the man 
looked at his watch and started to walk again. Clearly he was late for work.  
The one who paid the most attention was a 3 year old boy.  
His mother hurried him along, but the kid stopped to look at the violinist.  
Finally the mother pushed hard  
and the child continued to walk turning his head all the time.  
This action was repeated by several other children.  
All the parents, without exception, forced them to move on.  
In the 45 minutes the musician played, only 6 people stopped & stayed for a bit. 
About 20 gave him money but continued to walk their normal pace.  
He collected altogether $32.  
When he finished playing and silence took over, no one noticed it.  
No one applauded, nor was there any recognition.  
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   23 
ד"סב 
No one knew this, but the violinist was Joshua Bell,  
one of the best musicians in the world.  
He played one of the most intricate pieces ever written,  
with a violin worth 3.5 million dollars.  
Two days before his playing in the subway,  
Joshua Bell sold out at a theater in Boston with seats averaging $100 each.  
This is a real story.  
Joshua Bell playing incognito in the metro station  
was organized by the Washington Post  
as part of a social experiment  
to see whether in a commonplace environment at an inappropriate hour:  
Do we perceive beauty? Do we stop to appreciate it?  
            
Hashem provides us with everything, so much goodness, so many gifts,  
without which our life would be greatly hampered. 
How much would a blind person pay to be able to see, a deaf person to hear? 
A paralyzed individual to be able to walk and bend down? 
And so so much more. 
Do we stop to appreciate all the millions of gifts He gives us,  
or do we just ‘walk on by’?

ד"סב 
A Cat Tale 
One day in Yeshiva Knesses Chizkiyahu of Kfar Chassidim,  
the widow of the late Dean, Rabbi Noach Shimanowitz,  
went down to the storage room together with one of the students  
to bring up some supplies for the kitchen.  
While they were in the storage room, they heard suspicious noises.  
When Rebbetzin Shimanowitz asked what the source of the noises were,  
she was told that there were mice in the area.  
Rebberzin Shimanowitz was aghast at this news 
and asked the talmidim to find a solution to the problem.  
A cat was duly obtained, with the hope that it would eliminate the problem 
A few weeks later, Rabbi Eliyahu Lopian, the Mashgiach of the yeshiva,  
was walking in the yeshivah with a talmid, when they ran into the cat.  
Rabbi Lopian was taken aback - he had been unaware of the cat's presence,  
and this was the first time he had seen it on the yeshivah grounds. 
"Whose cat is this?" inquired Rabbi Lopian. The talmid explained that the cat had 
been brought to the yeshivah to curtail the mice problem. 
Rav Lopian inquired further, "And who feeds her?" 
The talmid answered, "She has plenty of food - she eats the mice.  
We don't need to bring her food."  
Rav Lopian answered, "I don't understand.  
If she does her job and scares off the mice, she won't have anything left to eat!  
We brought her here to perform a service, so we have to feed her!" 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   25 
ד"סב 
Rav Lopian then entered his apartment and brought out a bowl of milk.  
At that moment, as he knelt to place the bowl before the cat, 
someone snapped a photo of the event -  
and that photo is today, one of the most famous photos in the Yeshiva world.  
A photo of a great tzaddik expressing his gratitude and concern for a cat!

ד"סב 
A Food Package From Afar 
R' Yaakov Orenstein of London, would frequently tell his students  
the following story, which happened with his rebbi, the Tchebiner Rav. 
"During the war, the Red Cross circulated a long list  
of names of Jewish people who were in Russia.  
I noticed the name "Weidenfeld' on this list.  
I was not sure that this was my rebbi, but on the chance that it was,  
I hurried to send a package of food to him through the Red Cross.  
I thought to myself, if it is the Tchebiner Rav, good.  
If not, another Jew will enjoy the food. 
"The war ended several years later,  
and the Tchebiner Rav managed to reach Eretz Yisrael.  
One day, a telegram arrived at my home in London, containing the words:  
'I arrived in Chaifa today. Thank you for the food you sent. Weidenfeld." 
So deep was the Tchebiner Rav's gratitude  
that the first thing he did after arriving in Eretz Yisrael, after years of suffering,  
was to send a note of thanks  
to a person who had sent him a package of food several years earlier. 
Each time R' Yaakov Orenstein told this story,  
tears would stream from his eyes, as he recalled the sterling middos of his rebbi.

ד"סב 
The Essence of a Yid 
The name Yehuda means “Thanks”,  
as האל, his mother, gave thanks to 'ה for her having a 4th one of the םיטבש. 
We are known as “Yehudim”, after Yehuda, the leader of the םיטבש,  
for being appreciative is the core of our essence. 
We therefore start off our day with the words  ינא הדומ - “Thank you!”,  
building within ourselves the essential and satisfying הדמ of בוטה תרכה, 
appreciating the good that comes our way. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   30 
ד"סב 
The Six Sentence Thank You 
After the fruit of the new season is harvested,  
the owner brings up the first of those fruits to the Bais Hamikdash  
to fulfill the mitzvah of םירוכב.  
The הרות tells us that when the owner brings it,  
there is a lengthy “thank you” - 6 םיקוספ worth!! – that he must say,  
outlining the different elements Hashem has done for us throughout our history,  
until the day of bringing these םירוכב. 
It is important to say “thank you”,  
but these  םיקוספ are telling us a fundamental lesson in how to say thank you. 
Our thank you shouldn’t just be a roll off the tongue “Thank you”  
rather we should aim to specify what we are saying thank you for. 
“Thank you for making supper” 
“Thank you for your gift” 
“Thank you for fixing the light” 
Etc. 
When we do so, it makes the thank you more meaningful for ourselves,  
And more meaningful for the one whom we are thanking. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   31 
ד"סב 
Good morning, Herr Mueller 
Near the city of Danzig, Poland, lived a prominent Rav.  
Every morning the Rov would take a walk, accompanied by his son-in-law. 
During his morning walk, the Rav would greet every man, woman, and child  
whom he met on his way, with a warm smile and a cordial "Good morning."  
Over the years, the Rav became acquainted with many of the townspeople this way 
and would always greet them by their proper title and name. 
Near the outskirts of town, he would exchange greetings with Herr Mueller,  
a Pole, originally from Germany.  
"Good morning, Herr Mueller!"  
the Rav would hasten to greet the man who worked in the fields.  
"Good morning, Herr Rabbiner!"  
would come the response with a good-natured smile. 
Then the war began. The Rav’s strolls stopped abruptly.  
Herr Mueller donned an S.S. uniform and disappeared from the fields. 
The fate of the Rav was like that of much of the rest of Polish Jewry.  
He lost his family in the death camp of Treblinka,  
and after great suffering, was deported to Auschwitz. 
One day, during a selection at Auschwitz,  
the Rav stood on line with hundreds of other Jews awaiting the moment  
when their fates would be decided, for life or death.  
Dressed in a striped camp uniform,  
head and beard shaven and eyes feverish from starvation and disease,  
the Rav looked like a walking skeleton. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   32 
ד"סב 
"Right! Left, left, left!"  
The voice in the distance drew nearer.  
Suddenly the Rav had a great urge to see the face of the man  
with the white gloves, small baton, and steely voice, 
who played God and decided who should live and who should die.  
His lifted his eyes and heard his own voice speaking: 
"Good morning, Herr Mueller!" 
"Good morning, Herr Rabbiner!"  
responded a human voice beneath the S.S. cap adorned with skull and bones.  
"What are you doing here?"  
A faint smile appeared on the Rav’s lips.  
The baton moved to the right – to life.  
The following day, the Rav was transferred to a safer camp. 
The Rav, now in his eighties, told me in his gentle voice,  
"This is the power of a good-morning greeting.  
A man must always greet his fellow man."

ד"סב 
The Greeting That Saved A life 
Pedro, the security guard at a major food plant,  
welcomed the sound of jangling keys, as it signaled the end of the workday. 
“Everybody out?” the manager asked. 
“Nope, the rabbi is still inside somewhere.” 
By “the rabbi,” Pedro meant a religious Jew named David, hence his nickname. 
The manager went off to search for David, but shortly later he returned alone. 
“Sir, I am positive he’s in there,” Pedro replied with conviction“ 
“Are you absolutely sure he didn’t leave yet, I didn’t find him?  
Maybe you didn’t notice him slip by as he left for the day?” 
“Sir, I am positive he’s in there somewhere,” Pedro replied with conviction.  
Not too pleased, the manager went off looking for David for the second time.  
After a few minutes he was back, but no David.  
With as much calm as he could muster, he said,  
“Pedro, you must be mistaken. I have searched the entire facility twice.  
There’s no way I’m going back to search again.” 
Meanwhile, inside the factory, in a walk-in freezer locked from the outside,  
David lay semiconscious, literally freezing to death.  
His muted calls for help began to slur until they faded completely.  
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   34 
ד"סב 
As if in a distant dream, he heard what seemed to be the sound of a shouting. 
“I’m locking up now,” the manager yelled, leaving no room for arguments.  
And yet Pedro persisted,  
“Sir, allow me to check myself, maybe the rabbi is in some type of trouble . . .” 
With that, the manager and Pedro jumped and dashed towards the freezers,  
finding David semi-frozen inside.  
They took him out and revived him, saving his life. 
The next day, the manager asked Pedro:  
“I’m really curious. How did you know that David was still inside the plant?” 
“It’s really very simple,” Pedro answered.  
“Every single morning without fail, I am greeted with a solitary ‘good morning.’  
It’s the rabbi who greets me this way.  
Every evening, upon leaving, he wishes me a hearty ‘good night.’  
Yesterday morning I received my usual cheery ‘good morning,’  
but I still hadn’t received my usual ‘good night’,  
so I knew he must still be inside!”

ד"סב 
How Do You Say Thank You in English? 
Heart surgery is an extremely risky procedure for anyone.  
When Rav Yosef Shalom Elyashiv, zt”l,  
at the age of 102, required a heart procedure,  
no doctor in Eretz Yisrael was willing to take on the risk of performing it.  
Instead, Dr. Clair, a world-renowned heart surgeon from America,  
along with his team of doctors and equipment, was flown in,  
despite the considerable expense.  
Boruch Hashem, the surgery was successful. 
Before the doctor and his team returned to the United States,  
he made a final visit to Rav Elyashiv.  
Anticipating this visit, Rav Elyashiv turned to someone and asked,  
“How do you say thank you in English?”  
He practiced saying the words “thank you” multiple times, 
until he could say them fluently.  
When the doctor finished his visit,  
Rav Elyashiv expressed his gratitude in English by saying, “Thank you.”  
People around them were surprised, 
because Rav Elyashiv had been communicating with the doctor  
through an interpreter throughout their interactions. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   36 
ד"סב 
They wondered why it was so important for him to personally say “thank you”  
instead of relying on the interpreter to translate “todah rabba.”  
When Rav Elyashiv was asked this question,  
he explained a fundamental concept, based on the Avdraham (quoted above). 
During Chazaras Hashatz, we can appoint a messenger, the shaliach tzibbur,  
to beseech Hashem on our behalf for health or parnasa.  
The shaliach tzibbur can intercede for us in many requests.  
However, when it comes to thanking Hashem,  
it must be done personally by each individual.  
That is why Chazal instituded Modim D’Rabbonon,  
for we cannot delegate someone else to say thank you on our behalf 
because the depth of gratitude varies based on the individual. 
So too here, I needed to express my appreciation to the doctor, myself, 
and couldn’t rely on an interpreter to do it for me.  
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   37 
ד"סב 
Make Them Feel Tall 
(Yoel Gold video in file) 
Rav Eliezer Geldzahler, the Rosh Yeshiva of Yeshiva Ohr Yisroel,  
was niftar young in a bus crash. 
A few years later, his daughter was driving on the Garden State Parkway.  
She stopped for gas and noticed that the attendant was a midget.  
As he began to wash her windows,  
he caught sight of a large picture of Rav Geldzahler on the passenger seat.  
He became visibly excited as he pointed to it. 
“How do you know that man?” he asked in a state of agitation.  
“I’ve been looking for him for more than two years. Where is he?” 
His daughter gently informed him that the man in the portrait was her father  
and that he had passed away from injuries sustained during a bus accident.  
The fellow stared at her, disbelieving, and then began to cry silently. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   38 
ד"סב 
“You know,” he said,  
“I do this job day after day, morning after freezing morning.  
There aren’t many jobs available to someone like me.  
Cars pull in here every few moments,  
but everyone averts their eyes,  
feeling uncomfortable by my strange appearance. 
“Then, one day, your father pulled in.  
He looked me straight in the eye, like no one else had ever done.  
‘My friend,’ he said to me, ‘you are an inspiration. 
You were born with what might appear to be a great handicap,  
but you refuse to play the role of the victim.  
You get up in the morning, go to work, and earn an honest living.  
You are a role model! 
You teach that circumstances should not dictate  
the terms of a person’s existence. 
“‘I am on my way to New York, where I have a large school.  
Today, I am going to tell my students all about you,  
so that they might learn from your example.'” 
With his eyes glistening, the gas station attendant completed his tale.  
“Of course I so looked forward to seeing your father.  
He made me feel tall.”

ד"סב 
Special Seder Songs
An older couple moved to Lakewood to be next to their children.  
Sadly, the husband was niftar, and the next few years were very difficult for the wife. 
Then came Corona and she would have to quarantine for Pesach.  
Not to have her husband, and now, not to be with any of her children!  
She had never spent a Yom Tov alone. She was inconsolable. 
One of the neighbors found out about her distress.  
They called her up and told her since their units are so close to each other 
the family would happily move their dining room table next to her open window.  
We can have the seder and you will be with us!” 
And so it happened. 
After the first days were over,  
the woman called her children, bubbling over with joy. 
She couldn't stop talking about what wonderful sedorim she had enjoyed.  
The best part was all the songs they sang.  
This family had the same minhagim and exact niggunim of her late husband.  
It brought her great comfort and happiness. 
Little did she know, that this couple called each and every one of her children  
to hear the songs of their late father.  
The children sent recorded messages,  
which the family memorized for the seder night, 
changing their usual songs, just to bring joy to this almanah. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   46 
ד"סב 
The Birth of Hatzalah 
In 1965, 
Rabbi Herschel Weber was walking down a street in Williamsburg, Brooklyn, 
when a man collapsed right in from of him. 
911 was called, but by the time the ambulance got there the person had died.  
The EMT stated that if they had arrived a few minutes earlier, 
the victim would have had a chance. 
He asked the EMT what they would have done and they said they would have 
given the man oxygen among other procedures. 
Motivated by what he just experienced,  
Reb Herchel went immediately to buy an oxygen tank,  
and trained himself and others in emergency life saving methods. 
And so Hatzalah was born, 
the largest volunteer ambulance organization in the world,  
which has since saved countless lives, 
due to the care and concern of just one individual! 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   47 
ד"סב 
Saved by a Yarmulka 
Rabbi Jonathan Sacks and his wife on a trip to the Catskill Mountains in New York,  
were told the following story by their driver: 
One Friday afternoon,  
The driver was on his way to join his family in the Catskills for Shabbos 
when he saw a man wearing a yarmulke, by a car at the side of the road. 
One of the tires was flat, and he was about to change the wheel. 
The driver pulled over to the roadside, went over to the man, 
helped him change the wheel, and wished him “Good Shabbos.” 
The man thanked him, took his yarmulke off and put it in his pocket. 
The driver must have given him a quizzical look, 
because the man turned and explained: 
“Oh, I’m not Jewish. It’s just that I know that if I’m wearing one of these” – he gestured to the yarmulke – 
“someone Jewish will stop and come to help me.” 
(RabbiSacks.org Ki Teitse 5778) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   48 
ד"סב 
The Phone Call 
At a dinner, the guest speaker introduced himself with an interesting request.  
He had just gotten up from shiva for his father,  
and was not in the mood to be a guest speaker at a dinner. 
He tried canceling, but the organizers had insisted that they needed him to speak,  
so he had come. 
Before he spoke, he made a request.  
His mother was, lo aleinu, a fresh almanah,  
and he asked if, assuming they enjoyed his derashah,  
they could take a moment to phone her and tell her so.  
Perhaps the people smiled at the request,  
and no doubt, some of them took it seriously and intended to honor it,  
but by the next morning, it was largely forgotten. 
Out of the several hundred audience members  
one person heard the sincerity in the request and honored it. 
The mother received a call from none other than Rav Shmuel Kamenetsky! 
(A Heart for Another, Rav Yaakov Bender, pg 99) 
         
To be a gadol in Klal Yisroel you may have to have a great mind,  
but you need to have an even greater heart!

The Medrash (Vayikra Rabba 34:3) states: 
When Hillel Hazakein would leave his students, they would accompany him and ask: 
“Rebbi, where are you going?” “To do a mitzvah,” he would reply. 
“Which mitzvah is that?” “To wash in the bathhouse.”  
They would ask. “Is it a mitzvah to wash?” “Yes,” he replied.  
“The kings that stand in public theaters and circuses  
have a special officer whose job is to rinse and scrub them to keep them clean.  
They pay him for this, and he sits with the elite of the kingdom.  
How much more so must I wash and myself, 
for I am made in the form and likeness of the Hashem,  
as it is says in the possuk “For He made man in the likeness of G-d”

Wash ‘em 
“ 
ונ ֹּוק
לי ִב ְ ׁשִ ב ֹּ
םֹּוי
ל
כְ ב ויָלְג ַרְו וי ָדָי ויָנָ פ ם ָדָא ץֵחֹּור ׇ
” 
The gemara (Shabbos 50b) tells us  
‘a person should wash his face, hands, and feet every day,  
in honor of his Maker, Hashem.’ 
Rashi on this gemara comments 
that aside from us being created in Hashem’s image  
and therefore intrinsically there is a requirement to be neat and clean, 
when people see us looking clean and respectable,  
it brings a kiddush Hashem as they will say  
ומלועל ול הככש ךורב -  
“Blessed is the One, Who has such a person in His world”. 
(If this halocho applies in a time when it wasn’t always so easy to get water,  
how much more so today,  
when Hashem bestowed on us the gift of modern plumbing and running water.)

ד"סב 
Why Yehoshua? 
Moshe was told to appoint Yehoshua as his successor. 
The םינקז bemoaned the fact that the face of Moshe  
resembled the sun (in its radiance), while of Yeshoshua (only) resembled the moon.  
“Woe to us for the embarrassment; woe to us for the shame and disgrace." ).הע ב"ב( 
Why did the םינקז refer to it as a 'disgrace'?  
Yehoshua merited leadership not because he could match any of Moshe's attributes,  
rather, because he was Moshe's talmid muvhak and he served him diligently. 
Additionally, Yehoshua spread out and organized the mats and the benches  
in Moshe Rabbeinu's study hall for people to sit on. 
The disgrace was that there was only one Yehoshua bin Nun!  
There were certainly other people capable of cleaning up the Beis Medrash  
and turning the lights on and off as did Yehoshua.  
But although "somebody had to do it", only one person actually did it. 
The םינקז realized that they too had the same attributes that Yehoshua had.  
They could have exerted themselves  
and had the same loyalty and faithfulness to Moshe as did Yehoshua.  
But they did not do so.  
As a result of this fact they now bemoaned,  
"Woe to us for our shame and disgrace, that we did not make this effort." 
(https://torah.org/torah-portion/ravfrand-5765-pinchas/) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   59 
ד"סב 
Set It Up Clean 
In 1860, the neighborhood of Mishkenot Sha’ananim  
was the first city to be built outside the walls of Yerushalyim.  
The bye-laws for this new and poor neighborhood 
contain a number of intriguing clauses governing the obligation  
to preserve the new towns’ cleanliness, in public and even in the private domain: 
• Every inhabitant of the Mishkenot Sha’ananim neighborhood  
shall remove from his house on a daily basis all garbage and any unclean matter.  
• He shall also sprinkle clean water on the floor of the rooms of his house  
at least once a day. 
• Every inhabitant shall not to cast away any garbage in front of his house,  
and shall clean up all the areas around his dwelling,  
and throw away the garbage in the designated area and cover it immediately… 
• The bathhouse and the mikveh shall remain clean from any dirt at all times… 
Similar provisions are contained in the 1889 bye-laws  
for the emerging “Mea Shearim” neighborhood, which was founded in 1874:  
““And your camp shall be holy” – this is a positive mitzvah.  
Therefore, all the members and residents of our society ‘Mea Shearim’  are obligated  
• to take care to keep the place clean,  
both in the public and also in the private domain.  
• The same applies to the cleanliness of the latrines,  
so that no damage should be caused to the purity of the air.  
• Garbage and fetid water shall similarly not be spilled.  
Anyone who transgresses this twice, shall be punished on the third occasion.  
And may it go well with those who take heed of this command.” 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   60 
ד"סב 
Organized In Room = Organized In Thought 
The story is told of a father who traveled long and far  
to see how his son was learning in Yeshiva.  
He finally arrived and asked the Rosh Yeshiva how his son was doing. 
The Rosh Yeshiva told him he was doing well  
and offered to take him to the bais hamedresh to see his son. 
The father refused, as he did not want to disturb his son while he was learning. 
However, the father requested to see his son’s bedroom. 
The father went to the bedroom, saw that the bed was made,  
and everything was neat and clean. 
the father said “now I see that my son is learning well,  
for if he is organized in his room, 
he is organized in his thoughts and is learning well.” 
With that the father asked to send regards to his son, 
and headed back home. 
             
In Telz Cleveland, Rav Gifter zt”l was very makpid  
that talmidim look neat and clean, and that the dorm room is kept organized. 
If a talmid did not make his bed,  
he would find a note on his bed asking him to please make it. 
This was not just a cleanliness tactic,  
but an element of enhancing chashivus for oneself,  
and enabling the talmidim to improve their learning, through being clean and neat. 
(Heard from Rabbi Moshe Leibowitz) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   61 
ד"סב 
The Gemara Lesson  
(a joke about cleanliness)  
A young man knocked on the door of the noted scholar Rabbi Shwartz.  
“My name is Sean Goldstein,” he says. “I’ve come because I wish to study Talmud.” 
“Do you know Aramaic or Hebrew?” the rabbi asks.  
“No,” replies the young man. 
“Have you studied Torah?” asks the Rabbi. 
“No, Rabbi. But don’t worry. I graduated with a doctorate in philosophy.  
I would just like to round out my education with a little study of the Talmud.” 
“I seriously doubt you are ready to study Talmud.  
I am willing to examine you in logic, and if you pass that test, I will teach you Talmud.” 
The young man agreed. 
Rabbi Shwartz holds up two fingers.  
“Two men come down a chimney.  
One comes out with a clean face, the other comes out with a dirty face.  
Which one washes his face?” 
The young man stares at the rabbi. “Is that the test in logic?” 
The rabbi nods. 
”The one with the dirty face washes his face”, he answers easily. 
“Wrong. The one with the clean face washes his face.  
Examine the simple logic. 
The one with the dirty face looks at the one with the clean face 
and thinks his face is clean.  
The one with the clean face looks at the one with the dirty face  
and thinks his face is dirty.  
So the one with the clean face washes his face.” 
“Very clever,” Goldstein says. “Give me another test.” 
The rabbi again holds up two fingers.  
“Two men come down a chimney.  
One comes out with a clean face, the other comes out with a dirty face.  
Which one washes his face?” 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   62 
ד"סב 
“We have already established that the one with the clean face washes his face.” 
“Wrong. Each one washes his face.  
Examine the simple logic.  
The one with the dirty face looks at the one with the clean face  
and thinks his face is clean.  
The one with the clean face looks at the one with the dirty face  
and thinks his face is dirty.  
So the one with the clean face washes his face.  
When the one with the dirty face sees the one with the clean face wash his face,  
he also washes his face. So each one washes his face.” 
“I didn’t think of that,” says Goldstein. “Test me again.” 
The rabbi holds up two fingers. “Two men come down a chimney.  
One comes out with a clean face, the other comes out with a dirty face.  
Which one washes his face?” 
“Each one washes his face.” 
“Wrong. Neither one washes his face.  
Examine the simple logic.  
The one with the dirty face looks at the one with the clean face  
and thinks his face is clean.  
The one with the clean face looks at the one with the dirty face  
and thinks his face is dirty.  
But when the one with the clean face  
sees that the one with the dirty face doesn’t wash his face, he also doesn’t wash his face.  
So neither one washes his face.” 
Goldstein is desperate. “Please give me one more test.” 
He groans, though, when the rabbi lifts two fingers.  
“Two men come down a chimney.  
One comes out with a clean face, the other comes out with a dirty face.  
Which one washes his face?” 
“Neither one washes his face.” 
“Wrong. Tell me, how is it possible for two men to come down the same chimney,  
and for one to come out with a clean face and the other with a dirty face?  
Now to you understand the logic of Gemara???”

ד"סב 
A Kingdom of Royalty 
When Hashem prepare to give us the  הרות He said: 
"
)'ו ,ט"י ,תומש( "
שֹודָק י ֹוגְו
םיִנֲהֹ כ תֶׁכֶׁל ְמַמ י ִל  וי ְה ִ ת םֶׁ תַאְו ׁ
. 
And you will be for Me a kingdom of Kohanim and a holy nation. 
Hashem did not just say that we will be a holy nation  
but we will be a kingdom of kohanim.  
We may not be from Shevet Levi, but we are still a kingdom of Kohanim – royal priests. 
Rabbeinu Avraham ben HaRambam, explains that a Kohein is a leader,  
a person who sets an example for his followers. 
Similarly, the Jewish people are meant to set an example for the rest of the world.  
Every individual Jew is responsible to be part of that example. 
Hashem is charging us with a very important role in the world –  
We are His royal representatives. 
Yes, royalty comes with tremendous perks.  
It also comes with tremendous responsibility. 
Let’s appreciate who we are,  
and carry ourselves and behave with dignity deserving of royalty! 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   68 
ד"סב 
Take Him Down 
Hashem in the Torah commands us (Ki Seitzei 21:23) 
not to leave the body of person executed by Beis Din hanging overnight  
“for it is degrading to Hashem.”  
Rashi explains this by an analogy to a case of two identical twins,  
one of whom becomes a bandit while the other becomes the king.  
If the bandit is captured and hanged,  
anyone who sees the body will think that the king has been executed,  
which is certainly a disgrace to the royal personage.  
Similarly, since Klal Yisrael has been created in Hashem’s image  
it is a disgrace to Hashem if the body is left hanging overnight, 
for we have within us a piece of Hashem. 
Wow!  
We are royalty! 
We must keep this in mind always,  
for we misrepresent Hashem if we fail to act in an appropriate manner.

ד"סב 
Our Father, Our King 
On fast days and throughout the Aseres Yimei Teshuva  
we say the הלפת composed by Rabi Akiva  
of  ונכלמ וניבא – our Father, our King. 
Well, if my Father is a King,  
that means that I am a prince!! 
If I am a prince, that means it is incumbent upon me  
to behave like royalty and realize my special role.

ד"סב 
Speak Like Royalty 
When 'ה commanded  חנ to bring the animals into the הבית, He said: 
ה ָרֹּהְט הָ נֶני ֵא רֶ ׁשֲא הָמֵהְ בַה ן ִמ ו ה ָרֹּוהְ טַה הָמֵהְ בַה ן ִמ 
The gemara (Pesachim 3b) tells us that  
although Hashem is very careful with every letter and nothing is extra in the Torah,  
and it would have been shorter to have written 
הָמֵהְ בַה ן ִמ ו
הָאֵמְטַה 
Hashem specifically told  חנ and recorded in the הרות the words ה ָרֹּהְט הָ נֶני ֵא רֶ ׁשֲא 
to teach us the importance of refined speech. 
         
A young man was discussing a certain topic  
with the Chazon Ish, Rabbi Avrohom Yeshaya Karelitz.  
In the midst of his conversation, he blurted out, “It’s sheker (false)!”   
The Chazon Ish, with his highly sensitive ears, turned to him and said,  
“It’s better to say it’s not emes (true).” 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   71 
ד"סב 
The pen of the heart and the messenger of the mind 
When Yaakov Avinu came in to his father Yitzchok Avinu to receive the brochos  
in place of Eisav he said, "Please arise,". 
Rashi tells us that Yitzchok was perplexed –  
as the use of the word "please" was out of character for Eisav.  
In fact when Eisav came in later, he said roughly to his father, "Arise, my father!" 
This behavior does not seem to correspond with what Chazal tell us  
about Eisav and his tremendous kibbud Av -  
how is it possible that Eisav, garbed in the best clothing to honor his father,  
could have spoken so disrespectfully to him?  
Even if he hadn't revered his father, even the rudest child, would remember to say 
please and to speak in the kindest, sweetest tone possible, to get the brachos? 
The Chovos Halevavos (Shaar HaBitachon Perek 5) says: 
“The tongue is the pen of the heart and the messenger of the mind” 
While it may be possible to pretend with our other limbs,  
our tongues represent our true selves. 
For Eisav, as careful as he was with his Kibud Av, his true essence came out when he 
spoke, and he couldn’t bring himself to talk in a refined and polite manner. 
Conversely, although Yaakov was disguising himself as Eisav, and he knew what was 
at stake, his true essence too came out, and  
he could not force himself to speak impolitely, thereby arousing his father's suspicion. 
(Pinnacle of Creation, Rav Henoch Leibowitz) 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   72 
ד"סב 
Sleeping in His Clothes 
Rabbi Shimon Schwab zt”l held a rabbinical position in Germany in 1933,  
prior to immigrating to the United States.  
He had given a Shabbos drosho that was misunderstood to be criticizing Hitler.  
The Gestapo called Rabbi Schwab in for questioning.  
He explained the misunderstanding and was freed  
but was told that they were going to investigate him.  
Over the next two months, he did not know what the Gestapo would do to him.  
During that entire time, he went to sleep at night wearing his clothes, not his pajamas.  
When asked about it, he explained his unusual behavior.  
Another Rav was recently executed by the Gestapo in the middle of the night. 
He had been wearing his pajamas and was left hanging in a public place.  
Rabbi Schwab was afraid  
that he, too, would be arrested and hung in the middle of the night.  
He felt that it would be a chilul Hashem, a desecration of Hashem’s name,  
for a Rav to be left hanging while wearing his pajamas.  
Therefore, he slept in his clothes  
for the entire two months that his life was in the balance. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   73 
ד"סב 
Crew, Not Passenger 
Rav Elchonon Wasserman Hy”d explained with a mashal 
why we have 613 mitzvos while the rest of the nations received only seven. 
When a ship embarks on a voyage,  
the passengers are expected to follow a few basic rules.  
Don’t damage the ship, don’t stand on the railings,  
along with some other basic rules that ensure the journey proceeds smoothly.  
The crew, however, have a far more complex manual of detailed instructions  
since they are responsible for navigating the ship safely through the vast ocean,  
ensuring it reaches its intended destination. 
We as Yidden are the crew of the world,  
charged with steering the ship of creation toward its ultimate purpose:  
a world filled with Kiddush Hashem, where Hashem is recognized as King. 
We answer not to the passengers, but to the owner of the ship - Hashem Himself, 
with unwavering commitment to the manual He has given us: the Torah. 
As crew members, we bear an immense responsibility.  
We hold a deep sense of achrayus and achdus towards our fellow crew members. 
At the same time, we interact with the passengers with pleasantness and sensitivity.  
We recognize their dignity and role, treating them with respect and kindness.  
As crew members, we must maintain our distinct identity,  
mindful not to be influenced or distracted  
by the desires and entertainment of the passengers form of entertainment.  
We are noticeably set apart in our role  
through the way we dress, speak, and conduct ourselves.  
Remember, when the ship reaches its destination –  
a world perfected in its recognition of Hashem,  
our reward as the crew will be beyond measure!

ד"סב 
Keep Smiling 
The yeshivah bachurim were surprised when an elderly non-Jew approached them,  
but they were even more surprised by what he said.  
“You know, I’ve been meaning to tell you young men  
how much I appreciate certain things about you,” the stranger remarked.  
“Whenever I see you, you are always dressed in such a respectful and dignified way.  
And the most impressive thing of all is that you are always smiling.  
I can see the satisfaction on your faces,  
and it really is such a pleasure for me to behold.” 
The mood and state of mind of a ben Torah  
are an important part of shaping the impression that others will have.  
Rav Avraham Pam zt”l pointed out 
that it is of vital importance  
for a Yid to always to project an air of joy and contentment, 
in order to demonstrate how wonderful a life of Torah truly is.

ד"סב 
Fateful Friendship 
Eretz Yisroel was a haven for thousands of European Jewish refugees  
escaping from the Nazis, yemach shemam.  
Among these refugees was Herbert Froehlich,  
a young man who arrived from Germany in 1935.  
He rented a room in Haifa from a woman who owned a boarding house.  
After Herbert had lived there for many weeks,  
the woman asked him whether he would accept a roommate.  
Realizing that his landlady needed the extra income  
to offset the difficult economic situation, Herbert readily agreed. 
As fate would have it,  
his roommate turned out to be the only non-Jew in the boarding house.  
Being a good-natured fellow, Herbert welcomed his roommate warmly  
and they established a close friendship and a mutually deep respect for one another.  
Herbert learned that his roommate's parents had sent him to Palestine from Sweden 
in order to learn the British banking system.  
After being invited to join in various Jewish trips and group activities,  
including Shabbos meals, Herbert's roommate gained an appreciation  
for the Jewish religion and the Jewish people. 
After several months, Herbert's roommate completed his education in Palestine  
and prepared to return to Europe.  
As the two friends tearfully bid each other farewell,  
Herbert's roommate removed his ski cap from his head, handed it to Herbert, and said,  
"I want you to keep this as a sign of our everlasting friendship." 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   76 
ד"סב 
Just a few years later,  
the world turned a blind eye as six million Jews were slaughtered in Europe.  
Only a small number of non-Jews stood up to save Jewish lives.  
Among these heroes  
was a young secretary at the Swedish Diplomatic Mission in Budapest  
whose name was Raoul Wallenberg.  
He was instrumental in saving thousands of Jews by securing visas for them.  
To this day his whereabouts are unknown,  
as Raoul Wallenberg was arrested by Russian soldiers  
while trying to rescue a group of Hungarian Jews during the last days of the war.  
It is believed that he died in one of Stalin's prisons or in Siberia. 
Many could not understand  
Raoul Wallenberg's unusual sensitivity towards the Jewish people,  
but Herbert Froehlich did.  
You see, Raoul was Herbert's roommate.  
Herbert believed that Raoul's association with his Jewish friends in Palestine  
was instrumental in creating his tremendous desire to help the Jews.  
As for Herbert, he never forgot his friend Raoul and wore his ski cap every night,  
even as it tattered and frayed with age.  
Thousands of lives were saved,  
no doubt in part, because one Jew made a Kiddush Hashem!

ד"סב 
The Heart of The Nation 
In November 22, 1963,  
the United States was shaken to the core  
by the assassination of President John F. Kennedy.  
The president was shot on a Friday morning, and the funeral was held Monday.  
Private and public schools throughout the country were closing on that day.  
Dr. Joseph Kamenetzky, the director of Torah Umesorah,  
asked Rav Yaakov Kamenetzky zt”l whether their schools should follow suit. 
Rav Yaakov advised that the schools should definitely be closed,  
quoting the Kuzari’s teaching  
that the Jewish people’s relationship with the nations of the world  
is analogous to the relationship between the heart and the rest of the body.  
The Jews are supposed to be the “heart” of the world  
and teach morality to all the nations.  
If such a horrific wrong could be committed,  
it meant that the Jewish people had failed, in some measure,  
in carrying out their mission. 
For corrections, questions, or additional material to add, please email bplotzker.ohrreuven@gmail.com   78 
ד"סב 
The Regal Rebbetzin 
A baalas teshuvah who grew up in a completely assimilated family in the Ukraine 
once related how a certain rebbetzin who came to their town 
made a deep impression on her.  
“She had a truly regal appearance.  
She was always well-dressed and adorned with beautiful jewelry.  
Someone said that she was wearing a wig,  
but none of us believed it; it looked so real!” 
But the real shock came when someone said  
that the Rebbetzin had eight children back in America,  
five of whom were already married.  
We were all astounded, not just by the fact that she had so many children,  
but by the fact that she looked so young and vibrant, not at all like a grandmother.  
We had always been told that childrearing was a burdensome task  
and most people could barely manage with one or two children,  
yet this woman had been the mother of eight children  
and always looked better than many other women I knew.”  
The outward appearance of the rebbetzin,  
her aristocratic demeanor and beauty,  
played a tremendous role in bringing a Jewish girl back to Yiddishkeit.
