From Rags to Riches 
Mr. Isaac Friedman was one of the thousands of Holocaust survivors that arrived 
in the United States as refugees in the early 1950's. In order to keep Shabbos, Mr. Friedman 
scraped together just enough money to open a small drapery store on the lower level of 
his apartment building in Philadelphia. Business was slow. Nonetheless, every Friday 
before sunset he would close his store and go upstairs to get ready for Shabbos. 
One Friday, just before closing, three people entered. "My name is Henry Ballod," 
said the leader. "We've come from Wildwood, New Jersey, and we need drapes for our 
motel. It's got eighty windows - can you handle it?" 
Realizing that such a large job would require many hours of material selection, Mr. 
Friedman knew there was no way it could be completed before Shabbos. Politely but 
firmly, he said, "I'm a religious Jew and I don't work past sunset on Friday. Please come 
back after the weekend and I'll be glad to help you." 
"Are you mad?!" shouted Mr. Ballod. "We came from over a hundred miles away. 
We don't have time to wait for you. We'll go elsewhere." And they stormed out. 
"I'll do a cheaper and better job," Mr. Friedman called after them. "Just come back 
after Shabbos." 
Monday morning, to Mr. Friedman's great surprise, the men returned. Without 
mentioning a word about Friday's incident, they chose their drapes and gave him a deposit. 
Mr. Friedman completed the job, and they were satisfied. After that, business really picked 
up. Mr. Friedman was ordering supplies by the truckload. Just weeks ago, he was an 
unknown penniless immigrant; now he had a booming business. 
Months later, Mr. Friedman was in Wildwood, New Jersey, when he heard a voice 
from across the street. "Friedman - I sent them all." 
Mr. Friedman looked up and saw Mr. Ballod beaming at him. He told Mr. Friedman 
that at a recent hotel and motel owner's convention, he took the podium and told the 
whole audience about his experience.  
"I told them how you insisted on closing the store before sunset on Friday, despite 
the fear of losing such a big job. I told everyone how impressed I was by the way you 
respected G-d more than money. Your morals are more important to you than the bottom 
line. And in the end, you did a good job for a good price. Someone like you, I told them, 
can be trusted!"  
10 | P a g e 
Shabbos in Mexico 
The negotiations were taking much longer than expected. Two representatives of a 
real estate conglomerate were trying to complete the purchase of an office building in 
downtown Mexico City, and the lawyers were discussing the details slowly and 
meticulously. Every office had to be inspected, the insurance policies verified, and the 
terms of payment specified to the last penny. Jacobo Sherem, the managing partner of the 
owners' group, was desperate to complete the transaction. With every passing moment, he 
was becoming more impatient and exasperated. 
An architect by profession, Jacobo had been trying to sell this particular office 
building for months, so that he could finally turn a profit on his investment. He had 
designed, bought and sold buildings in the past, but this building on Calle Presa Salinillas 
had been his biggest investment, and so far, it had been a losing proposition for his group. 
Due to the depressed financial climate in Mexico, most of the office space was unoccupied, 
so Jacobo was not making a profit. 
As the hours passed, Jacobo became increasingly nervous. The buyers had told him 
unequivocally that they were leaving Mexico on the first plane out the next morning, which 
was Saturday. They would not delay their trip. If they could complete the purchase that 
day, that would be wonderful; otherwise they would cancel the negotiations and move on 
to prospective deals in other countries. 
It was the late hour of the day, Friday, that was putting pressure on Jacobo. For the 
last year, he had been attending evening study classes in the Aram Tzovah Kollel in the 
Polanco section of Mexico City. He studied Torah a few nights a week, but he was not yet 
Shomer Shabbos. After many discussions, much introspection and inspiration from kollel 
members at Aram Tzovah, Jacobo and his wife Sophia were inching closer to total 
commitment to Shabbos observance. Jacobo had already started going to Shul every 
Friday night and his office was closed on Shabbos, but he and Sophia were not yet 
completely Shomer Shabbos. 
Frustrated at being so close, yet so far, from fulfilling his dream of selling the 
building, Jacobo looked at his watch and saw that there was less than an hour and a half 
to Shabbos. Reluctantly, he told the prospective buyers that the negotiations would have 
to continue Sunday or Monday - he had to leave and close his office. The buyers threatened 
that it was now or never, for they were flying out of the country the next day. But Jacobo 
would not budge. Shabbos was coming. He hadn't missed a Friday night at Shul in weeks 
11 | P a g e 
and he wasn't going to miss tonight. The buyers were incredulous that Jacobo would 
scuttle a deal that could lift him out of the financial doldrums, but he would not be moved. 
The parties to the negotiations bid each other a final farewell, and Jacobo went home to 
prepare for Shabbos. 
He couldn't help but second guess himself. Had he acted correctly? There would 
be other Shabbosos when he would be in Shul, but now he might never be able to sell this 
building that was becoming an albatross around his financial holdings. He tried to enjoy 
that Shabbos, but it was difficult. An internal debate raged in his mind. He was proud of 
his commitment but he wondered if it was worth the price. 
Two weeks later, early Thursday morning, September 19, 1985 (during Selichos of 
the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah), Mexico was struck by the greatest tragedy in its history. In a 
matter of seconds, a monstrous, rumbling earthquake gashed and shook Mexico City, 
toppling buildings, swallowing homes, wreaking havoc and bringing instantaneous death 
to thousands of people! Within 24 hours, as the country staggered to adjust to the shock 
and magnitude of the Thursday tragedy, an aftershock shook the city. In the downtown 
district, there was utter devastation. People searched in vain for relatives and friends, but 
it was mostly for naught. The destruction, mayhem and sorrow were beyond imagination. 
Yet, amidst all the devastation, one building 
remained standing - the one Jacobo couldn't sell. Its 
windows were blown out and some of the facade of the 
building had peeled off, but remarkably, it was structurally 
sound. The Mexican government had to regroup; aid and 
rescue efforts had to be directed and coordinated. People 
needed the assurance that the government was functioning 
and accessible. Thus, within days of the earthquakes, 
Jacobo's building, conveniently located downtown, was 
checked for its strength and stability. When it passed 
inspection, the government bought most of the offices in 
the building and the remaining space was sold to large 
corporations who had lost their offices when other 
buildings collapsed or were deemed unsafe. Jacobo's extraordinary profit was far beyond 
what he would have made had he sold the building weeks before. The deals for his building 
propelled Jacobo into a category of wealth he never imagined. 
The Hashgachah Pratis of the Shabbos not allowing him to sell the building 
propelled him into being a total Shomer Shabbos. Jacobo and Sophia never told the story 
12 | P a g e 
to anyone. Word got out that their building was sold to the government, but no one in the 
community knew about the frantic Friday negotiations two weeks before the earthquakes, 
and Jacobo's decision to close his office for Shabbos. 
Years later, in the palatial lobby of his new magnificent office building, Jacobo and 
Sophia tendered a grand party in honor of his first siyum of a Gemara, which he had 
studied for several years with Rabbi Shea Deutsch at the Aram Tzovah Kollel. There, in 
the presence of Rabbis, community leaders and friends, Jacobo told the story of that 
Shabbos that changed his life.

A Candy for Shabbos 
Towards the end of his life, Rav Aharon Kotler was unable to swallow 
anything – no solids, no liquids, not even a drop of water. All his fluids and food came 
through an intravenous tube. This sad situation resulted 
in a mouth that was so dry it was difficult for the Rosh 
Yeshiva to speak. 
One Wednesday, the doctor allowed Rav Aharon 
to have a candy to suck on to make him feel better. The 
student, who was with Rav Aharon to help take care of 
him, was very happy to tell the Rosh Yeshiva the good 
news. With great effort, Rav Aharon responded, “A 
candy? I’m going to save it for Shabbos!”

Watering the Plants 
When Rav Shimon Yehudah HaKohen Shkop first arrived in Grodno to serve as 
13 | P a g e 
Rosh Yeshiva, he stayed in the home of a very nice family. The family showed the tzaddik 
great respect. All his needs where provided for. 
Yet, despite all the luxuries and attention, there was one thing missing. On Friday, 
there was no job that that the Rosh Yeshiva could do to honor Shabbos. All the Erev 
Shabbos chores were already accounted for. 
Then Rav Shkop discovered the flowerpots that were all over the spacious home. 
He accepted the responsibility of watering the many plants and keeping their green leaves 
clean …. L'kavod Shabbos- in Honor of the Holy Shabbos!

Kabbalas Shabbos - A Source of Merit 
The Ridvaz was the Rabbi of Slutzk in the 16th century and one of the gedolim of 
his generation. One day, he became very sick and his life hung in the balance. His 
neshamah was brought before the Beis Din shel Ma’alah – The Heavenly Court. He was 
informed that it had been decreed that he die before his time and he was asked to state 
whether he possessed any significant reason or merit that might alter the decree and let 
him remain in this world. 
The Ridvaz asked the Beis Din if serving as the Rav of Slutzk, one of the largest 
Jewish communities of the time, was a great enough merit for him to return to this world. 
The Beis Din shel Ma’alah weighed this, and concluded that this was not enough. Someone 
else could be the Rav of Slutzk. 
The Ridvaz then mentioned that he was in the middle of writing a commentary on 
the Talmud Yerushalmi. If he died now, it would not be completed and many people 
would have a hard time learning without it. The Beis Din considered this claim, and then 
rejected it. There were already a number of fine commentaries on the Yerushalmi. 
In a final attempt to save his life, the Ridvaz argued that every Erev Shabbos after 
midday, he would stop all his weekday activities, and simply sit and eagerly await the arrival 
of Shabbos. Surely, the sincere longing that he had shown for Shabbos was a great source 
of merit. 
After deliberation, the Beis Din shel Ma’alah accepted this argument, and in this 
merit, the Ridvaz was granted another 30 years of life! 
14 | P a g e 
"Sha...Boss!" 
Many years ago in New York City there lived a man named Reb Chaim Gelb. Each 
week, Reb Chaim would have to find a new job. On Monday he would begin work, would 
work for a few days, only to be fired on Friday because he refused to work on Shabbos. 
Reb Chaim once found a job working in a men's clothing store that sold things such 
as suits, jackets and coats. Since he was such a good and honest worker, his boss did not 
fire him, even after Reb Chaim told him that he would not come to work on Shabbos. He 
worked the entire week and remained home on Shabbos. 
One Friday afternoon, the boss called over Reb Chaim and said, "Mr. Gelb, I want 
you to come to work tomorrow. Some of my other workers are sick so I need you to do 
some of their work.” The boss insisted that Reb Chaim work on Shabbos – that he be 
mechalel Shabbos! 
Reb Chaim did not argue. He just looked 
straight at the boss and said “Sha…Boss!” 
The boss thought that Reb Chaim was 
saying “Shabbos” so he answered, “I know that 
tomorrow is Shabbos, but I need you here. I do 
not have enough workers. You are going to 
have to come to work tomorrow.” 
Reb Chaim did not argue. He just looked 
straight at the boss and said, “Sha…Boss!” 
The boss started to get upset. “Mr. 
Gelb,” he said, “I know that tomorrow is 
Shabbos, but you still have to come to work!” 
Reb Chaim did not argue. He just looked straight at the boss and said, “Sha…Boss!” 
The boss suddenly realized what Reb Chaim was saying. He was saying, “Sha, Boss - be quiet boss.” He was telling his boss to be quiet and not say that he had to come to 
work on Shabbos. 
The boss got very angry. He said, “Mr. Gelb, you are fired! You cannot work here 
anymore.” Reb Chaim had lost his job. 
That Shabbos, Reb Chaim was very, very happy. He had shown Hashem how much 
he loved Shabbos, and that he would never ever be mechalel Shabbos. 
15 | P a g e 
Not long after that, Reb Chaim found another job in a different store. This entire 
store was closed every Shabbos. From then on, Reb Chaim never had to worry about 
having to work on Shabbos. 
o
Splurging on Shabbos Wine 
The mother of Rebbi Zakkai had a special 
beautiful head covering which she wore Shabbos 
night. When she put it on, she felt it raised the 
level of Kedushas Shabbos to new heights. 
One Erev Shabbos, her son, Rebbi 
Zakkai, did not have any money to buy wine. 
Shabbos was getting closer and Rebbi Zakkai 
went to Shul without having bought wine for 
Kiddush. When he returned from Shul, he 
noticed his mother was not wearing her beautiful 
Shabbos head covering that she always wore. Yet, she looked as exquisite as ever, beaming 
with happiness. He then noticed on the table a bottle of wine. Not just any wine, but a 
bottle of very expensive wine standing gallantly on the table waiting for Kiddush.  
Rebbi Zakkai understood what happened and went over to her and gave her a very 
warm brachah for her devotion and great sacrifice L'Kavod Shabbos. The next year, Rebbi 
Zakkai's fortune took a turn upward and he became a very wealthy man. The first thing 
he did was to go out and buy his mother a magnificent head covering shining with precious 
stones. 
When Rebbi Zakkai's mother passed away, she left over for Rebbi Zakkai three 
hundred barrels of wine as an inheritance. When Rebbi Zakkai was niftar, he left over 
three thousand barrels of wine. All this was in the zechus of honoring Shabbos with the 
very best wine.

Shabbos is a Gutte Zach  
(Shabbos saves the Day) 
Shabbos Protection  
Mr. David Solomon was what you would call a 'self-made man.' He lived in 
Manhattan and had built himself up from almost nothing with his ‘own two hands.' He 
started out poor, but was now a multi-millionaire with several factories and substantial 
holdings on Wall Street and he knew exactly how loudly money 'talks'. 
Of course, there was no place in his life for Yiddishkeit, for Shabbos, for Kashrus, 
and no time for anything except business ... and family. 
The most precious of all his possessions 
was his eighteen-year-old daughter. She was the 
apple of his eye. Her picture was on his desk and 
every wall of his office. He dreamed of the day that 
she would marry and he would see grandchildren. 
He even had a special fund saved up to buy her a 
new house and whatever she needed. And that day 
would soon arrive. 
He was sitting in his office when the phone 
rang. “Mr. Solomon?" asked an official sounding 
voice on the other end of the line. 
"Yes." 
"Have you got a daughter by the name of Sarah Solomon?" the caller inquired. 
Again, he answered, "Yes." 
"This is a police officer speaking from County hospital. You'd better get down here 
fast, Mr. Solomon. Your daughter has been in a pretty serious car accident." 
Mr. Solomon asked a few questions to make sure it wasn't a prank, slammed the 
phone down, grabbed his keys and raced out of the office. 
It was a nightmare. She was in critical condition; she laid in bed in a coma. Wires 
and instruments were attached to every part of her body. The doctors said it was 
17 | P a g e 
impossible to operate until her condition stabilized. 
He stood there, weeping. What could he do? His wife arrived and she, too, burst 
out in tears. 
The next few days were practically sleepless. They waited in the hall for some news 
from the doctors. Perhaps she opened her eyes? Perhaps there was some improvement? 
But the only message of hope he received was his father's suggestion to travel to 
Brooklyn and meet with Rabbi Menachem Mendel Schneerson, the Lubavitcher Rebbe. 
"He's the only one who can help," his father said. "I have friends that he did big 
miracles for. If anyone can help you, he can. Just go, get an appointment and try to see 
him." 
David's emotions began flipping. At first, he was excited; there was hope! But then 
he became disappointed...in himself. What? I, David Solomon, a normal, successful 
American businessman going to a religious man, a Rabbi no less, to seek out a miracle? 
Then suddenly, he was afraid. "I don't do any of the commandments. How can I go to 
this Rabbi? I'll be so ashamed." 
But then his confidence returned. He remembered his money. "I'll give a big 
donation and the Rabbi will certainly hear what I have to say." 
David drove to the Rebbe's headquarters in Brooklyn to arrange a private meeting. 
Usually, people had to wait for months to see him, but because of the urgency, that very 
evening he was standing before the Rebbe. 
"Rebbe!" He began to cry. "My daughter had a terrible accident. She is in critical 
condition. Rebbe, can you save her? Here, here is a check for fifty thousand dollars! For 
your institutions." 
The Rebbe just looked at him without seeming to notice the check and said, "If you 
want to save your daughter, you must begin to observe Shabbos." 
"Rebbe," he replied, "I can't promise such a thing. I'm a very busy man and I'm not 
a religious Jew. Here!" He took out his checkbook put it on the Rebbe's desk and began 
writing, "Here. One hundred thousand dollars! Please, Rebbe, please take it, just save my 
daughter." 
The Rebbe looked at him even more intently and said, "Mr. Solomon I am here to 
help you, I'm not thinking of myself. If you want her to be healthy, keep the Shabbos." 
"Rebbe, here!" Solomon signed his name to another check and placed it before the 
Rebbe. "It's an open check. Write what you want. Take what you need, just save her!!" He 
18 | P a g e 
was really crying now. Looking deeply into the Rebbe's eyes for some hope. 
"Hashem is responsible for her healing." the Rebbe replied. "You must appeal to 
Him. I can only help with prayer but you must also do your part. At least keep the Shabbos. 
Then your daughter will be healthy and you will even see grandchildren from her." 
Mr. Solomon gathered up his checks. He said he would think about it, shook the 
Rebbe's hand and left, and closed the door after himself. He waited around for a while 
outside the door, hoping that the Rebbe would call him back. But he didn't. Mr. Solomon 
returned to the hospital empty handed. 
That night he couldn't sleep. The meeting with the Rebbe made a deep impression 
on him. The Rebbe's face danced before his eyes saying, "I am here to help you, not to 
help myself. Keep Shabbos..." It was the first time in his life he met a man that was not 
interested in his own personal profit. 
Meanwhile, Sarah's condition continued to get worse. 
Mr. Solomon turned to his wife and said, “This Shabbos we won't drive or turn on 
any lights. I mean, we'll be staying in the hospital anyway, so we have nowhere to go. I 
think I remember how to make Kiddush; we can at least begin to do what Rabbi 
Schneerson said." 
That Sunday, there was some improvement, and the next Sunday she opened her 
eyes for the first time in a month. 
Mr. Solomon became a Shomer Shabbos Jew and his daughter Sarah not only 
became completely healed, she eventually got married and had several children. Just as the 
Rebbe said. After all, if we do our part, Hashem will do His. 
o
Sir Moshe Montefiore and the Czar 
A long time ago, when Czars ruled Russia, the Czar made a law that Jews could not 
learn Torah, daven in Shul, or do any other mitzvos. The Jews did not like these rules, so 
they tried to continue learning and doing mitzvos secretly. The Yidden were very unhappy 
and wanted to leave Russia, but the Czar was very mean and made another new law 
forbidding any Jews from leaving Russia. 
19 | P a g e 
At that time, faraway in England there lived a very rich Jewish man named Sir 
Moshe Montefiore. When he heard that how hard it was for the Yidden in Russia, he 
decided to try and help. So, Sir Moshe Montefiore sent a letter to the Czar asking to be 
invited to visit Russia because he had something very important that he wished to discuss 
with the Czar. 
When the Czar received Sir Moshe Montefiore’s telegram, he wrote back inviting 
him to come to Russia. Sir Moshe Montefiore packed a suitcase and traveled to Russia, 
and approached the palace to speak with the Czar. “If you let the Jews exit Russia," he 
said, “I will pay you ONE THOUSAND rubles for each Jew that you allow to leave!” The 
Czar liked that idea because it meant he would make a lot of money and the Czar really 
loved money. 
Sir Moshe Montefiore suggested that they write up a contract. So, he took out two 
pieces of paper and on each one he wrote that the Czar promised to let the Yidden leave 
Russia, and that Sir Moshe Montefiore promised to give the Czar 1,000 rubles for each 
Jew that left the country. They both signed their names at the bottom of each piece of 
paper and Sir Moshe Montefiore returned to his hotel with his copy of the contract. 
The next day, the Czar had a meeting and showed all his advisors the contract he 
had signed with Sir Moshe Montefiore. He told them he was very excited because now he 
could let the Jews leave Russia and he would make a lot of money. However, his advisors 
were not happy. “That is not a good idea,” they said, “The Jews pay a lot of money in 
taxes. If they leave Russia, they won't pay you any more money and you will end up losing 
money instead of making it!” 
“But I already signed a contract,” the Czar cried, “I gave my word. If I change my 
mind and don't keep my promise, all the kings throughout the world will make fun of me 
and say that I do not keep my promises.” The Czars advisors were very wicked just like 
the Czar, so they advised him send a soldier to kill Sir Moshe. That way, no one would 
ever know about the contract, and if no one knows about the contract, no one will realize 
that the Czar did not keep his promise. 
“I can't do that,” said the Czar. “Everyone knows that I invited Sir Moshe 
Montefiore to come to Russia to speak with me. If my soldiers kill him, everyone will say 
that I tricked him when I invited him to Russia.” 
Finally, one of the Czar's advisors said, “You must kill Sir Moshe in such a way that 
no one will ever know that it was you who killed him. In our country, there is a special 
poisonous plant. We can take the plant and burn it in a box. The poison from the plant 
20 | P a g e 
will go into the smoke and while the smoke is still in the box, we can seal the box tightly 
before the smoke escapes. Then we can send the box to Sir Moshe. When he opens the 
package, he will breathe in the poisonous smoke and die. No one will ever know that he 
was killed by the poisonous smoke and no one will think that the Czar killed him.” 
The Czar thought this was a great idea so he ordered one of his servants to put the 
poisonous plant into a small iron box and burn it. Before the smoke could get out, he 
sealed the box tightly and wrapped it up like a present. Then the Czar called over a soldier 
and ordered him to deliver it to Sir Moshe right away. Of course, the Czar did not tell the 
soldier that the box contained poisonous smoke; he just told the soldier that it was a very 
important gift for Sir Moshe and that he should get it to him quickly. The soldier took the 
package from the Czar, jumped on his horse, and rode directly to Sir Moshe's hotel. When 
the soldier finally arrived at the hotel, it was Shabbos afternoon. He knocked on the door, 
and when Sir Moshe answered the door, he told him, “I have a package for you from the 
Czar.” However, since it was Shabbos, Sir Moshe told the soldier, “Thank you very much, 
but I cannot open the package now because today is our Shabbos.” 
The soldier replied that the Czar said it was a very important package and that he 
must open it right away. Sir Moshe did not want to be mechalel Shabbos and said he would 
open it as soon as Shabbos was over. The soldier offered to open it for Sir Moshe. When 
he opened the tightly sealed box, the poisonous smoke went into the soldier’s face, right 
in front of Sir Moshe's eyes. The soldier fell on the floor, dead. Sir Moshe realized that the 
Czar had wanted to kill him, not the soldier. He knew that once the Czar would find out 
that he was alive, he would look for another way to kill him, so right after Havdalah, Sir 
Moshe packed a suitcase and left Russia, never to return. Sir Moshe was rescued from this 
evil plan because he refused to be mechalel Shabbos.

The Business of Keeping Shabbos 
Shabbos is a very special time of the week, and we cannot perform any melacha 
(work) on Shabbos. There's a wonderful story about a Yid who gave up everything to keep 
Shabbos, so that he wouldn't have to work on Shabbos. He gave up the opportunity to 
make a lot of money, in exchange for protecting the kedushah of Shabbos. 
21 | P a g e 
Today, it is so easy to keep Shabbos! Seventy years ago in America, however, it was 
not so easy. In those days, if a person did not want to work on Shabbos, he would be fired 
from his job, and would be left without a parnassah. Reb Moshe Diskind went to work to 
make money to support his family. He would walk into a business and say, “Can I work 
for you?” The boss would reply, “Sure, come tomorrow morning and I will give you a job. 
You can earn some money and take it home for your family, and you will be able to buy 
something to eat.”  
He came in the next day, Monday, and worked. After work, his boss gave him that 
day’s payment. He came in Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday and Friday. On Friday, my 
great uncle went over to the boss to get his money from the day’s work. The boss said 
“Here’s your pay. I expect you to be here tomorrow morning, on Saturday.” My great 
uncle said, “I am sorry, I am not allowed to work on our holy Shabbos!” The boss replied, 
“If you don’t come in tomorrow morning, then don't bother coming on Monday morning 
either!” He was fired from that job. He did not feel sad. He went home that Shabbos, and 
enjoyed a wonderful Shabbos with his family. Reb Moshe was smiling and singing zemiros 
the whole time. But after Shabbos, he started to worry about finding work again the 
following week. 
Baruch Hashem, he found another business. He went to this other business and the 
boss said, “No problem. You can work for me.” This boss was a nice man. So, that 
Monday, he started to work. He worked Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday. 
That Friday, his boss told him, “I expect you to be here on Saturday.” Reb Moshe told 
him, “I'm sorry, but I can't work for you on my Shabbos. I am willing to come in and 
work all day Sunday. I am a hard worker, please let me keep my job.” The boss said, “If 
you can work very late on Friday night, maybe until around 10:00 pm, then I won't make 
you come in on Saturday.” But Reb Moshe said, “I'm so sorry, I cannot.” To which the 
boss replied, “I'm sorry, too - you are fired." 
This happened now twice! But Reb Moshe knew in his heart that he was doing the 
right thing, and when you do the right thing, Hashem will help you. 
Again, he found another job and started to work. This new boss was very nice. He 
worked for him on Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday and on Friday he was very 
scared. He didn’t know what was going to happen when he went in to get his money. Reb 
Moshe went in and the boss did not say one word about Shabbos. Reb Moshe was so 
excited! He took his money and walked out of the door. As he started walking down the 
street, suddenly his boss started running down the street after him calling out “Stop, Stop!” 
When his boss caught up to him he said, “I forgot to tell you one thing. That is, we have 
22 | P a g e 
business every day of the week except Sunday.” Reb Moshe said to him, “Please, I am 
such a good worker. Do me a favor, I’m willing to work on Sunday, but please let me not 
come in on Shabbos." And the boss answered, “I feel bad for you, but I can't, and if you 
don't come back tomorrow morning, you can't come back on Monday morning.”  
Again, Moshe was not sad. He went home again and for the third straight Shabbos, 
he barely had enough food to eat; not to mention no nosh or soda. He had barely enough 
food to eat. But, he knew for certain that he was doing the right thing. That Friday night, 
he went to Shul, davened and said, “Hashem please help me find a job somewhere to help 
my family.” And when a Yid davens sincerely, Hashem will listen to his prayers. 
On Monday, Reb Moshe heard about a new business in Manhattan. They were  
making staples and paperclips and pens and all sorts of office supplies. So, Reb Moshe 
called up and asked if he could work there, and the boss said, “Fine, 
come right over, I need another worker.” Reb Moshe worked 
Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday. By Friday morning, he 
was nervous. Would he get fired for the fourth week in a row? Friday 
morning, he knocked on the door to collect his money for the week. 
The door opened and the owner said, “Moshe! You probably came 
to get your money, but I need you to have a seat for a second.” Reb 
Moshe thought to himself, "Oh no, here we go again," and he started 
turning red in the face. The owner looked at Reb Moshe and asked, 
“Why are you getting so nervous? I'm about to give you your money 
for the week.” Reb Moshe replied, “I am very nervous because I know 
what you are going to say to me. You are probably going to tell me 
that I have to work on Shabbos and that if I don’t, I’ll be fired.” 
“Wrong!” said his boss. “I need you to do me a big favor. You know, I’m a big boss 
with many other businesses to run. I don’t want to run this business any more. I want to 
take my money out of this company, but I want to give the company over to someone 
who will run it well. I want YOU to be the new owner of this company.” 
Moshe looks and him in shock and answered, “What?!?! Me? The owner? How?”  
The boss said, “Yes, don’t worry about the details; we can work them out Monday 
morning!” 
Reb Moshe successfully ran that company for 50 years! He was rewarded for one 
simple reason. He never gave up on Shabbos. 
23 | P a g e 
Shabbos in the Herman Home 
Rav Yaakov Yosef Herman and his wife hovered over the hospital bed, awaiting 
some sign of improvement. This week has been a difficult one for Rav Herman. His 
daughter, Esther, had been hospitalized in serious condition and the prognosis was grim. 
On Friday afternoon, Rav Herman and his wife reluctantly left Esther’s bedside to 
prepare for the many Shabbos guests that were always welcomed at their table. Rav 
Herman reassured his wife, “Surely in the merit of oneg Shabbos, our Esther will get 
better.” 
Troubles and sorrows were put aside with the muktzeh items in the Herman home. 
With great joy, Rav Herman recited Kiddush, sliced the challah, and entertained their 
guests. His wife happily served a delicious Shabbos meal. 
The telegram arrived the next morning 
while Rav Herman was at Shul. One of Rav 
Herman daughters received the news: her sick 
sister had passed away. She decided that she 
would not let this distressing news ruin her 
parents’ Shabbos joy! She would keep the 
terrible secret until Shabbos was over.  
After 
Havdalah, the young girl 
approached her parents with a heavy heart with 
the sad news. However, before she could open her mouth to speak, there was a hard knock 
at the door. Another telegram arrived, which read, "ESTHER HERMAN. STOP. OUT 
OF DANGER. STOP.”  
After some investigation, the Hermans solved the mystery. Esther was alive and 
had even taken a turn for the better! Due to some confusion at the hospital, the first 
telegram must have been sent in error! 
“This is the merit of Shabbos!” Rav Herman excitedly told his family, ecstatic. “One 
who honors Shabbos merits everything!”

Onions L’Kavod Shabbos 
Reb Avrom was a farmer, and he was walking home one cool Spring on Erev 
Shabbos. He was always happy this time of year. After months of work, plowing, planting, 
pruning and taking care of his crops, he was close to seeing his hard work pay off. As Reb 
Avrom was walking, he slowly lifted his head and turned his face toward the sky. He 
worriedly realized, “Oh no! It looks like it’s going to rain.”  
After Pesach, in the springtime, it usually doesn't rain, and when it does, it’s not 
good for the crops. He thought to himself, “I have so many patches of onions that I 
planted and worked so hard to care for. I plowed, pulled the weeds, and worked so 
diligently. The whole process took nearly 4 months! Now, if the onions would be left out 
in the rain, they would be ruined. If the rain comes, it will flood my whole field and all 
those weeks of work will be lost. Maybe the rain will wash away my fields and my 
livelihood!” He reassured himself, “No! We all know that Shabbos is the Mekor 
HaBrachah - the source of all blessing.”  
When Reb Avrom walked into his house, 
his wife and children had a worried look on their 
faces. “Father, did you see what we saw? All 
those dark clouds make it look like it's going to 
rain and ruin your crops.” Reb Avrom replied, 
“No! Shabbos is here. We will have a regular 
Shabbos.” He tried to calm them down as he sat 
down to begin the meal. As the whole family sat 
down, they started hearing banging and loud 
noises from outside. The family looked out the 
window to see all the neighbors putting down big sheets of plastic over their fields, to save 
their crops from the rains. “Oh no! The rain is going to come!” The children started to 
scream. “Maybe we should also cover our fields. All of our vegetables and onions, all of 
that hard work may be lost! Father, all of our neighbors are covering their fields to protect 
them.” To which Reb Avrom answered, “No! Shabbos is the Mekor HaBrachah - source 
of all blessing.” With that, he started to sing. Right in the middle of Shalom Aliechem, 
they heard the unmistakable should of thunder crashing, and the rain started to fall. Soon, 
a torrential downpour rained from the clouds. The boys looked out the window and saw 
all those other fields covered with tarps. Our farmer, Avrom, was sitting in his house 
singing zemiros, thinking in the back of his mind, “Shabbos is the Mekor HaBrachah - 
25 | P a g e 
source of all blessing. Nothing bad can happen if I keep Shabbos well.”  
That whole night, it continued to rain. Many of the farmers who had run out to 
cover their fields with plastic tarps were Yidden and were mechalel Shabbos in the process. 
But Avrom refused to be mechalel Shabbos. All Shabbos long, his family was worried, but 
whenever they looked at their father, he was so calm. He sang zemiros at the table, told 
over divrei Torah, davened and relaxed the whole day. Even after Havdalah, Reb Avrom 
didn’t go outside to check what was going on. He ate Melave Malka as if everything was 
fine. 
Sunday morning after Shacharis, our dear Reb Avrom was walking and he saw all 
his neighbors, all those unfortunate people who were mechalel Shabbos, and they were all 
crying. “Look at this! We spent all our time and effort trying to save our fields, and they 
are all ruined.” Reb Avrom asked what exactly had happened. They answered, “You will 
never believe it. Friday night when all the frum Jews were eating their meal, we were 
preparing our fields. We were covering them with these large tarps of plastic to make sure 
they were not ruined by the rain. Do you know what happened? We just went to take them 
off, and all our vegetables were ruined. The plastic ruined them!”  
Avrom ran to check his own fields. To his amazement, all the onions were fresh 
and good. Yes, it rained heavily right on his vegetables, but all the next day, Shabbos, the 
sun was so strong it dried up all his fields. But all the people who broke Shabbos, the sun 
made their vegetables rot under the tarps. 
Reb Avrom walked into his house while his children were eating breakfast. He 
looked at them and said, “Didn't I tell you Shabbos is the source of all blessing?! The 
Ribbono Shel Olam saved all our onions because we kept Shabbos! Know that a Jew must 
keep Shabbos and look, when we keep Shabbos, Shabbos keeps and protects us.

Straus Bank is Closed on Shabbos! 
Jews in Karlsruhe, Germany, were given many rights and lived in relatively peaceful 
conditions in the late 17th century. The Jewish community flourished in this city, located 
along the Rhine River, a short distance from France. 
26 | P a g e 
Shmuel Straus was a banker in Karlsruhe, and he enjoyed a happy life. When he 
wasn’t working, he spent time learning with his children, doing chessed for his friends and 
neighbors, and learning Torah. Shmuel earned just enough to support his family without 
any worries. He was known to be a Yarei Shomayim - Fearing Hashem - and therefore he 
did all his business dealings honestly. 
Shmuel's first job was running a small bank. He got the job from his father-in-law 
right after his marriage. With a permit from the government, Shmuel would mainly 
exchange money for different businessmen who travelled to various countries. He would 
also invest money for people. When he first started working, Shmuel bought a special coat 
with two large pockets, one where he would place 'accounts receivable,' and one for 
currency exchange. 
One Erev Shabbos morning, before going to the Bris of his friend's son, he put on 
a special coat that he would wear on Shabbos, Yamim Tovim, and special occasions. He 
transferred the bundles of money he ordinarily kept in his other coat to this special coat. 
Following the simchah, he continued on his way to work as usual, changing money and 
accepting payments. 
At noon, he left work to help in the preparations for Shabbos Kodesh. After his 
wife lit the Shabbos candles, he put on his Shabbos coat, bid farewell to his wife and small 
children, and headed to Shul. 
Shabbos was a special day for Shmuel, and he'd spend it in Shul, davening and 
learning. He spent precious time with his family. For the Shabbos meal, they would always 
have many guests. That particular Shabbos was no different. As he walked the quiet route 
back from Shul, he took the time to think about the words of Torah he would say at the 
Shabbos table. His guests would soon arrive with their families at his home. Shmuel sat 
on the bench on the side of the road as he gathered his thoughts, when he suddenly 
realized that his pockets were still filled with the bundles of money from that day's 
dealings.  
Shmuel was sweating from the thought of having to carry the money. He could not 
bear the idea of using money that he brought home on Shabbos. Sitting in the deserted 
street, he suddenly thought about the joy he would have knowing that he did the right 
thing, and quickly unbuttoned his coat, dropping the wallets on the ground. A blanket of 
relief swept over him. He knew that he would have to repay many debts, and that his 
financial future was in doubt. However, his trust in Hashem helped him make a decision 
that he knew was right. 
27 | P a g e 
That Shabbos was extra joyous for him. He felt that he passed the great test Hashem 
placed in his way. His extra level of simcha was a mystery to his family and the many guests 
who had eaten at his table before. 
As the sun set and the stars came out, Shmuel said Havdalah. His wife held the 
special candle and the family passed around the bisomim - spices - to soothe the neshamah 
upon the departure of the beautiful Shabbos. When he was finished, Shmuel told his family 
what had happened on Friday night, explaining the reason for his extra joyous Shabbos. 
He also told them that it may be the beginning of a more difficult life. His wife accepted 
the will of Hashem and assured the family that everything will turn out for the best. 
The same night, Shmuel decided to check the route he'd used, hoping to find the 
wallets he'd dropped. Amazingly, he did! As Shmuel opened the door to his home, the 
family breathed a sigh of relief, for the wallets were intact with the full sum of money 
inside them. 
A few days later, the Minister of Finances of the Baden region heard about the 
trustworthy Straus bank, and entrusted Shmuel with a huge sum of money. The investment 
in the bank spread and many well-to-do people invested their money with Shmuel. His 
Shemiras Shabbos was rewarded with great wealth. 
Today, Shmuel's legacy lives on in Yerushalayim, where the Straus Courtyard, a 
place of Torah learning, stands in his name. His children sold Straus & Company in 1938 
when they fled from Germany and relocated to America.

The Miller Who was Mechalel Shabbos 
Many years ago lived a Yid who owned a millstone. People came from the 
surrounding villages with sacks of wheat to grind into flour. However, the Yid was not 
able to keep all the money he earned. He had to pay the poritz (landlord) half of whatever 
he earned from grinding the wheat. 
One Friday night, when the Yid came home from Shul, he was so tired that he had 
to rest for a few minutes. However, instead of resting a few minutes, he fell into a deep 
sleep and didn't wake up until the morning. He had missed Kiddush Friday night and the 
28 | P a g e 
entire seudah! The next morning, he was sad and embarrassed to hear what had happened. 
But the next Friday night it happened again. He came home from Shul, and he was so 
tired that he had to rest for a few minutes, and he soon fell into a deep sleep and didn't 
wake up until morning. He had missed Kiddush Friday night and the entire seudah again! 
The next week it happened again, and again the week after that…  
Week after week, the miller found himself more and more sad about what was 
happening. "Oh, no! Not again!" cried the miller in frustration. "What is happening? 
Nothing helps! I can't go on like this. Tomorrow I'm going to the Rebbe!"  
The next day, he went to Rebbe Shmuel Abba of Zichlin and told him the whole 
story. Bursting into tears, he begged to be rescued from this bizarre situation. 
"I don't know how I can help you," replied the tzaddik, "It must be that you were 
mechalel Shabbos, so measure-for-measure, you are being prevented from honoring her."  
The Yid became even more upset. "I don't understand. I'm so careful with the laws 
of Shabbos. Breaking Shabbos? I don't know how that is possible." 
The Rebbe listened to him, but simply repeated that the uncontrollable sleep was a 
punishment for violating Shabbos. 
Broken-hearted, the Yid returned home. When he told his family all that the Rebbe 
had said, they were astonished. Everyone knew how careful their father was about keeping 
Shabbos. One of his sons spoke up. "Tatte, I have to tell you that the Rebbe's words are 
true. One Friday night, when I was up late, I saw you get up in the middle of the night. 
You were obviously still half asleep and unaware. I saw you light a candle so you could see 
your way to get a drink of water, and then you extinguished it when you were finished. 
The next morning it was clear that you had no memory of what had happened, and not 
wanting to shame you or show disrespect, I never said anything. But now that the Rebbe 
himself hinted to it, I realize that I must tell you what I saw." 
The man thanked his son, and immediately set forth for Zichlin again. He told the 
Rebbe what his son had reported.  
"Please Rebbe," sobbed the man, "tell me how to do Teshuvah!" 
"I told you, I can't do anything. The only 'remedy' is that you have to show Shabbos 
how important she is to you. When you stand up for Shabbos, Shabbos will be appeased 
of your insult." 
A few days later, the Yid was called to his poritz, the nobleman from whom he 
received the right to operate his mill in exchange for annual rent and a percentage of its 
income. The poritz informed him that he wanted to earn more money and therefore it 
29 | P a g e 
would be necessary for the Yid to work the mill on Saturdays as well, starting now. 
"That's impossible." The Jew declared 
firmly. "I only work six days, never on 
Shabbos." 
"Oh, come on," said the nobleman, "I 
know you Jews. You can get around it if you 
want. I heard that a Rabbi can make some kind 
of contract where you can stay home, but the 
mill stays open." 
"That is not how I keep Shabbos!" the 
miller firmly declared.  
The poritz raised his voice. "Fool! I'll give 
you two months to complete the renovations needed to keep the mill open on Shabbos. 
If you don't, I'll throw you out." 
When the two months were up, the poritz sent for him again and asked for his 
decision. Realizing that the moment the Rebbe had spoken of had come, the Yid 
responded quickly that he will never work on Shabbos in any way no matter what happens, 
and just as quickly, the nobleman threw him out! 
With no mill to work, there was no money. The miller and his family soon fell into 
hard times. Even basic food for the children became hard to supply.  
"Stop worrying," said the Rebbe. "Know that your cure and your salvation will soon 
happen. You passed your test, Shabbos is appeased, and your landlord will soon ask you 
to come back to the mill." 
The miller went home, fully believing in his Rebbe's blessing. 
Meanwhile, the mill's owner had completed the work remodeling the mill, 
and he had found a manager who was willing to work a seven-day week to take it over. 
But right from the beginning, things didn't work out. Unusual accidents kept occurring, 
and all sorts of problems arose. Huge losses piled up. The situation was bizarre, and 
everyone realized it.  
The nobleman was forced to admit to himself that his problems must be connected 
to his having ruined the livelihood of the Jewish miller. So, he called the man to come 
back to and work the mill. 
"And what about my Shabbos?" the Yid asked. 
30 | P a g e 
"Look," he answered, "after all the unusual problems and sharp monetary losses, I 
realize that your G-d is with you. You may run the mill however you see fit." 
So, the Jew returned to the mill, and was soon blessed with more success than he 
had ever had. He became prosperous. Also, his sleep problem disappeared at the same 
time, just as the Rebbe had said. Now, even with his new wealth, reciting Kiddush on 
Shabbos night remained his greatest pleasure.

“Don’t Forget Who You Are!” 
Young Rose stood on the docks of the Polish harbor, a steamer trunk at her feet. 
Out of her nine siblings, twelve-year-old Rose was the child chosen by her parents to be 
sent to the "golden land," America. Life in Poland was hard, and hunger was a constant 
visitor in her home. After much scraping and pinching, her family had saved enough 
money for a single ticket to the United States. Rose, the youngest of the nine, was the 
lucky one chosen to go. 
With a deep sigh, her father dropped the trunk on the deck and turned to his 
daughter. He gazed deep into his daughter's unclouded eyes. "Rose, mein kind (my child), 
remember: Hashem is watching over you every step of the way. Remember His Torah and 
keep it well. Never forget that more than the Yidden have kept the Shabbos, the Shabbos 
has kept the Yidden. It will be hard in the new land. Don't forget who you are. Keep the 
Shabbos - no matter what sacrifice you must make." 
"Tatte! Tatte!" (Father! Father!) Rose cried as she hugged her father and he hugged 
her, squeezing the breath out of her in a hug meant to last a lifetime. Then he turned and 
walked down the gangplank. As the ship steamed away from the shtetl life of Poland, a 
fresh sea wind blew on the passengers preparing to start their life anew. 
After weeks at sea, the ship finally made its entrance into New York harbor. While 
the other passengers stood against the railing, shouting and clapping as they saw the "new 
land," Rose stood there alone, shy and unsure. Would the new land fulfill its promise of 
hope, freedom, and riches? Would her relatives meet her there - or was she now homeless? 
Rose did not have long to worry. Her relatives were indeed waiting for her and she 
31 | P a g e 
was soon safe in their home. With her mature appearance and demeanor, it was not long 
before Rose found a job as a sewing machine operator. 
Life in America was new and strange. Polish mannerisms were quickly shed, along 
with religion. Modesty, Kashrus, and Torah were slowly abandoned, together with the 
“outmoded” clothing and accent. Rose's relatives insisted religion was "old-fashioned," 
that it was an unnecessary “accessory” in America. Rose, however, never forgot her 
father's parting words. She put on the new clothes her relatives gave her, cut her hair to 
suit the fashion, but never gave up on the Shabbos. 
Every week without fail, Rose devised a new excuse for her boss to explain why she 
did not come to work on Saturday. One week, she had a toothache; another week, her 
stomach bothered her. After three weeks, the foreman grew wise. He called her over. 
"Rose," he said, "I like your work, and I like you. But this ‘Shabbos business’ has got to 
stop. Either you come in this Saturday, or you can look for a new job." 
Upon hearing of this, Rose's relatives were adamant. Work on Shabbos, she must. 
They applied pressure; they begged, and bribed. Rose felt like a leaf caught between heavy 
gusts of wind, pushed and pulled with no weight or life of its own. She was so young and 
vulnerable. She wanted to please her relatives. But her father's words kept echoing in her 
head. What should she do? 
The week passed in a daze for Rose. Her emotions were in turmoil. "On the one 
hand," she thought, "Tatte is not here to help me be strong. I do want to please my new 
friends. I want to fit into this new land,” she reasoned. And then just as quickly came 
another thought: “On the other hand, how can I forget Shabbos? How can I give up the 
beauty Tatte taught me?” 
"Rose, sweetheart, listen to us. It's for your own good." On and on went her 
relatives, until Rose's determination wavered. 
That Friday, Rose walked to work, lunch bag in hand and head stooped in thought. 
She sat at her machine throughout the day. Would it be so awful to do this tomorrow as 
well? Whirr, buzz...whirr, bzzz. The machine kept tune to Rose's troubled thoughts. What 
should she do - or was the question: what could she do? Rose knew there was really no 
question. She was Jewish, and she would keep the Shabbos. 
32 | P a g e 
Shabbos in America was not like the warm day Rose had 
known at home. This week was the worst yet. She didn’t have 
the courage to face her relatives and tell them of her resolve. 
Instead, she left the house that Shabbos morning, pretending to 
be headed for work. She spent the morning walking the streets 
of Manhattan. Together with the city pigeons, she rested in 
Tompkin's Square Park. "Tatte, this song is for you," she 
whispered. The pigeons ruffled their feathers. "Yonah matzah 
bo manoach.” There she sat among the pigeons, singing 
Shabbos songs, with tears in her eyes. Finally, three stars peeked 
out from the black sky, announcing the end of Shabbos. Rose 
had triumphed, but her victory would cost her dearly. She had 
no job and had alienated her family. 
"Baruch HaMavdil..." It was time to face the hardness of the world. Rose trudged 
homeward, dreading the nasty scene when her relatives learned that she hadn't been to 
work. As she neared home, a shout broke into her reverie. "Rose! What ... wait...what ... I 
mean, how are you here? Where were you?" 
Rose looked up at her cousin Joe. 
"Joe, what will become of me? I kept Shabbos and lost my job. Now everyone will 
be angry and disappointed with me, and oh, Joe, what will I do?" The words tumbled out 
together with her tears. 
Joe looked at her strangely. "Rose, didn't you hear?" he asked gently. 
"Hear what?" 
"There was an awful fire in the factory. Only forty people survived. There 
was no way to exit the building." Joe's voice was hushed, and he was crying openly. "Rosie, 
don't you see? Because you kept Shabbos, you are alive. Because of your Shemiras 
Shabbos, you survived." 
Out of 190 workers, Rose Goldstein was among the minority of those who survived 
the infamous Triangle Shirtwaist Factory fire on Saturday, March 25, 1911. Because it had 
been Shabbos, Rose Goldstein was not there. As her father had said, more than the Jews 
keep the Shabbos, the Shabbos keeps the Jews.

33 | P a g e 
Shabbos in Pajamas 
This story takes place during the 1920’s. 
Once a year, Mr. Sam Golding, who was not very religious, would go to Shul to say 
Kaddish for his father’s yahrtzeit. 
That year, on a cold winter morning, Sam came into Shul to join the 7am minyan, 
when he saw two little boys running down the Shul hallway wearing pajamas.  
Curious, Sam followed them down the steps and saw them run into a room in the 
basement. So, Sam walked over and knocked on the door, which was answered by a nice 
lady. He asked the lady why the boys were in the Shul and why they were in their pajamas.  
“We live here,” replied the lady.  
“I didn't know there was an apartment in the Shul basement,” said a surprised Sam. 
“It's not an apartment. We were thrown out of our apartment for not paying the 
rent. My husband refuses to work on Shabbos, so each week he is fired from his job. When 
we ran out of money, we had no place else to go, so the Shul is letting us stay here for 
now.” 
“How sad,” thought Mr. Golding as he went to daven. After davening and saying 
Kaddish for his father, Sam went home to talk to his wife about what he had seen. They 
decided to try and help this family. 
Mr. Golding returned to the basement and knocked on the door. When the mother 
of the house opened the door, he said, “I would like to help you out. Here is a check for 
$5000 to help you find a better place to live.” 
To his surprise, the mother answered, “We appreciate your kind offer, but we will 
not take money from someone who does not keep Shabbos.”  
Sam was shocked! “Don't be silly,” he said, “I am willing to give you all this money - just take it.” But nothing could change their mind. The family was being moser nefesh 
for Shabbos, by losing job after job; they wouldn't think of taking money from Mr. 
Golding, who had his business open on Shabbos. 
Sam returned home, and sat down for breakfast with his wife. He told her 
everything that happened. They were extremely impressed with the mesiras nefesh of the 
family. For a while, they both just sat there and ate quietly. Finally, his wife said, “You 
know, after hearing that someone would be willing to live in a tiny apartment to avoid 
being mechalel Shabbos, maybe we should start keeping Shabbos too?” Mr. Golding look 
34 | P a g e 
at his wife and said “Do you mean it? Because I feel the same way. If someone is willing 
to live like that for the sake of Shabbos, Shabbos must be very, very important.” 
On the Sunday morning after keeping their first Shabbos in 20 years, Mr. and Mrs. 
Golding knocked on the that family’s door. When the husband and wife opened the door, 
they said, “I hope you're not back to try and give us money again. We have not changed 
our minds about taking money that was earned by working on Shabbos.” 
Mr. and Mrs. Golding responded, “Yes, it is true that we have come back once 
again. This time to offer you $10,000! But this time you can accept the money, because it 
is coming from someone who keeps Shabbos. Yesterday was the first Shabbos that we 
kept in a long time.” 
This time, the family took the check and were now able to buy an apartment for 
themselves. They guarded the Shabbos and the Shabbos and helped them.

He Never Sleeps on Shabbos 
Many years ago, a man named Rabbi Zalman Grossman lived in Yerushalayim. He 
loved to do mitzvos, especially to honor the Shabbos. Rabbi Grossman did not go to sleep 
on Shabbos at all. All Friday night, he would stay awake, singing Shabbos songs and 
learning Torah, trying to enjoy Shabbos to its fullest.  
Rabbi Grossman was very poor, and he could not make a living in Yerushalayim, 
so he came to America and opened an office in Manhattan. He worked very hard and sent 
money to his wife and children in Yerushalayim. Rabbi Grossman lived in a small room 
behind his office. He didn’t have much, but he was happy with what he had.  
One Friday night after Shul, Rabbi Grossman came to his room and began to make 
Kiddush. When he lifted the cup of wine, he suddenly felt a sharp pain in his side. It hurt 
so much that he dropped the cup and fell to the floor. Rabbi Grossman cried for help, but 
there was no one around to help him. He was in too much pain to get to a phone to call a 
doctor.  
Rabbi Grossman had a friend named Reb Nachum. He had also come to America 
to earn some money for his family in Yerushalayim. After Reb Nachum finished his 
35 | P a g e 
Shabbos meal, he went for a walk. He walked and walked. He thought about what 
happened to him that week… He thought about his family back in Yerushalayim, whom 
he missed very much… Then Reb Nachum looked around. “Where am I?” he thought. 
He was lost. Lost in a foreign land that was unfamiliar to him. What should he do now? It 
was Shabbos, so he had no phone to use, and it was late at night with no one around to 
ask. 
Reb Nachum saw that he was very close to his friend Rabbi Grossman’s office. 
“Rabbi Grossman never sleeps on Shabbos,” thought Reb Nachum. “Even though it is 
very late, I know he is still awake. I will go to his office and spend the night there.” Reb 
Nachum walked to Reb Zalman’s office and knocked on the door. There was no answer. 
“That’s strange,” he thought. “I know Rabbi Grossman is inside and I am sure he is not 
sleeping.” He knocked again. He knocked louder. He called Rabbi Grossman’s name. Still, 
there was no answer. Now Reb Nachum was worried. He put his ear to the door and 
listened. Was that the sound of someone moaning in pain? Reb Nachum ran to the street 
to try to get help.  
A police car was passing by at that moment! He waved at the police car to stop. He 
told the two policemen that his friend seemed to be very sick. The officers broke the door 
open and sure enough, there was Rabbi Grossman lying on the floor in pain, unable to 
move. The police rushed him to the hospital, where the doctors took care of him at once. 
The doctors told Reb Nachum, “It’s a good thing that you found your friend when you 
did, and not an hour later. You really saved his life!”  
Reb Nachum knew that he had not saved Rabbi Grossman's life. Hashem saved his 
life because he always kept Shabbos. As we sing in the zemiros, “Ki Eshmiras Shabbos, 
Keil Yishmireini– If I keep Shabbos, Hashem will watch over me.” 
Rabbi Zalman Grossman kept Shabbos in a very special way, so Hashem 
watched over him and brought Reb Nachum to save his life.

Reb Mattisyahu and the Wheat 
Reb Yitzchak Mattisyahu was a wheat and grain merchant. His income was 
36 | P a g e 
dependent on buying and selling wheat and other grains. Reb Mattisyahu lived from 
business deal to business deal, and every extra coin he earned went to support his growing 
family.  
One Shabbos afternoon, a group of soldiers stormed into his home. Their 
commanding officer said, “We are in desperate need of wheat to feed the soldiers stationed 
in this area. We are ready to buy all of the wheat you can give us.”  
“It is my day of rest today, my holy Shabbos,” was Reb Mattisyahu’s unruffled reply. 
“It is forbidden for me to trade, buy or sell today. I would love to do business with you, 
but I must decline.” 
“But we need that wheat!” came the angry response, “We need to feed our soldiers. 
We cannot take no for an answer. Fear not, we will pay you handsomely for your grain.” 
“I don't sell today.” Reb Mattisyahu 
stubbornly repeated. Nothing would come 
between him and the heilige Shabbos. Not even 
a windfall of this magnitude. He then took his 
key ring out from his desk and hastily threw it 
in their direction. “Here are the keys to my 
wheat silo. Help yourself to whatever you want. 
I cannot do business today!”  
The soldiers went to the silo, opened it 
with the keys and left some money at the entrance, equivalent to what they figured the 
grain was worth. They took all the wheat they could carry and went off. 
As soon as Shabbos was over, Reb Mattisyahu hurried to his silo to salvage what 
was left, but no one could prepare him for what he found - an empty silo! No wheat 
whatsoever! No grain meant no money. What could he do? How would he feed his family? 
How could he resume his livelihood if there was nothing left to trade? Reb Mattisyahu 
would not let those thoughts bother him. He knew that he had done the right thing. 
Shabbos was Shabbos, and Shabbos was holy. It was more important than anything else. 
Who cares about the lost income when he still had his Shabbos?! 
A moment later, he noticed the pile of gold coins left behind by the soldiers, but it 
did not change the situation. Reb Mattisyahu would not benefit from business dealings 
done on Shabbos. He didn't consider the money his. He discreetly distributed the money 
to the poor of his town. Money earned on Shabbos was not for keeping.  
Reb Mattisyahu’s family lived in poverty for quite some time, but they did so with 
37 | P a g e 
a clear conscience.  
Eventually, Reb Mattisyahu was offered the position of a Dayan, a judge, in the city 
of Pinsk and he accepted it. In Pinsk, Reb Mattisyahu became known as a fair and just 
judge who was sought after for his opinion. After one Din Torah between two very rich 
men, Reb Mattisyahu received a substantial amount of money as payment for his ruling. 
But here again, Reb Mattisyahu knew that the money was not rightfully his. He gave the 
money to the Stoliner Rebbe, Reb Usher ztz”l, to once again distribute to the poor. 
When Reb Mattisyahu approached the Rebbe and told him the two stories, the 
Rebbe was astonished by Reb Mattisyahu’s strength of character and blessed him saying, 
“In the merit of your astounding piety and tzidkus, you will be zoche (merit) to have a son 
who will be a Rebbe’s Rebbe." 
In 5564 (1804), Reb Mattisyahu was blessed with a son who grew up to be a true 
Rebbe’s Rebbe, the Tzaddik HaRav Hakodosh, Reb Avraham of Slonim ztz”l, the author 
of the seforim Yesod  HaAvodah and Be’er Avraham.

Saved By The Queen 
The following story happened over a hundred and fifty years ago, in the year 1831. 
It was the year of the Polish uprising, when Polish patriots organized a rebellion against 
their Russian overlords. They drove the Russians out of Warsaw and proclaimed 
independence (January, 1831). But later that year, the Russians recaptured Warsaw and 
crushed the revolt. In a small Polish town near Kovno, there lived at that time a Jewish 
innkeeper. We will call him Yosef. He was well known in the surrounding countryside as 
an honest and G-d fearing Jew, whose wife would prepare delicious kosher dishes. 
Members of the Polish nobility frequented Yosef’s inn, where there was never a shortage 
of good food and wine.  
One day, on a late Friday afternoon, a Russian General and his troops arrived in 
town. They were returning from the fighting around Warsaw, and settled into the town 
for a rest after a long march. The sun had already set when the General sent his assistant 
to fetch some wine. The General had been told about the good reputation of the local 
38 | P a g e 
Jewish innkeeper, and to him he directed his assistant. From every Jewish house, Shabbos 
candles were shining forth, which cast an air of festivity and holiness in the otherwise dark 
and deserted streets, through which the General’s assistant made his way. He finally found 
the inn, but it was closed. He went around to the private entrance and knocked on the 
door. The innkeeper, dressed in his Shabbos clothes, welcomed the assistant into his 
house.  
“The General sent me to buy some of your best wine,” the assistant said, taking out 
a roll of money.  
“I am sorry indeed,” Yosef replied. “We are now celebrating our Shabbos. I do not 
do business on our holy day of Shabbos.”  
Nothing the assistant said could make the Jew change his mind. The assistant 
returned to his General and told him that the Jew had refused to sell him wine because of 
the Shabbos. The General flew into a rage. He immediately dispatched two soldiers to the 
innkeeper to warn him that if he still refused to sell them wine for the General, he would 
face the most serious consequences. Some time later, the soldiers returned to the thirsty 
General – without wine.  
“Why didn’t you bring me wine?!” the General roared.  
“The Jew said, he could not sell any wine to anybody on his Shabbos. However, he 
sent the key to his wine cellar, and suggested that perhaps the General might wish to help 
himself to any of the wines there, as his guest,” the soldiers reported. The fury of the 
General began to evaporate as he contemplated the strange situation.  
“How strange that Jew is!” the General thought. “He would not sell me a bottle of 
wine because of the Shabbos, yet he is prepared to give away his entire wine cellar. That 
little Jew has a great deal of brazenness, or perhaps courage would be more accurate.” 
Such were the thoughts that crossed the General’s mind at that moment, and he decided 
to meet the Jew in person!  
When the General entered Yosef’s house, he remained standing at the door as he 
absorbed the wonderful scene that met his eyes. The table was covered with a white cloth 
and laden with tasty dishes. The Shabbos candles shone brightly. Yosef and his wife and 
children were dressed in their Shabbos clothes; faces aglow with delight. The General 
almost felt sorry to have disturbed this beautiful atmosphere and to have frightened the 
children. Yosef rose to meet the General and respectfully invited him to join in the feast.  
The General, who had burst into the house with the intention of teaching the Jew 
a lesson, felt his anger melt away. He sounded quite human, even polite, when he asked 
39 | P a g e 
Yosef why he had refused to sell him some wine. “Don’t you know that refusing to sell 
provisions to the army in times of war is tantamount to rebellion?”  
“Your gracious Highness surely knows that we Jews are forbidden to do business 
on our holy Shabbos day,” Yosef replied. “To keep the Shabbos day holy is one of the 
Ten Commandments given to us by Hashem, the Supreme King of Kings. We must obey 
His command over any command by human kings and princes. However, now that your 
Highness has been so gracious as to honor our humble house with your presence, allow 
us the opportunity of fulfilling another great commandment – that of hospitality. We shall 
indeed consider it a privilege if you and your assistant would join us at the table. Please, 
be our guests.”  
The General was greatly impressed. He sat down at the table and motioned for his 
aide to do the same. Never in their lives had they enjoyed such delicious dishes before – 
gefilte fish with horseradish, roast chicken with tzimmess, kugel and kishka, with plenty 
of excellent wine to wash down each course. It was a feast fit for royalty. 
Before leaving, the General took a handful of golden pieces from his pocket and 
offered them to Yosef. Politely but firmly, Yosef refused to accept any money. “Have I 
not told your Highness that we consider it a special mitzvah to offer hospitality? You were 
our guests, not our clients. We are grateful to you for the privilege.” The General warmly 
shook hands with Yosef and departed in a happy mood.  
Several years later, some soldiers suddenly appeared in Yosef’s inn and arrested him. 
Together with other dangerous rebels and criminals, Yosef was brought to Vilna in chains. 
During the long investigation that followed, Yosef learned that he was accused of taking 
part in a new Polish conspiracy to overthrow the Russians and drive them out of Poland. 
It so happened that the leader of the local rebels, Pan Kanarski, was captured, and in his 
documents, Yosef’s inn was mentioned as the place where members of the Polish nobility 
frequently met to plan their revolt. This was proof enough that Yosef, too, was part of the 
conspiracy, even though it was not true.  
Yosef sat in prison awaiting trial, fully aware of the serious sentence that would be 
meted out to him – lifelong deportation to Siberia with hard labor, from which very few 
ever returned alive; or perhaps more mercifully – a quick death by a firing squad. In 
addition to his own plight, Yosef knew that if he were found guilty, it would cast a shadow 
on all the Jews of his town, with endless repression and persecutions. While he knew that 
all efforts would be made by his fellow Jews to establish his innocence, he did not feel 
very confident about the outcome. His only hope was to trust in Hashem. There was 
nothing for Yosef to do but daven with all his heart and recite Tehillim, which he did 
40 | P a g e 
constantly, tearfully, and with a broken heart.  
One day, as he was in the midst of such davening, the heavy door of his solitary jail 
cell opened, and a high official appeared. He was the Chief Inspector of prisons, on a 
routine check-up of the prison cells. The tears which filled Yosef’s eyes blurred his vision, 
and he could not see the Inspector very well. But the Inspector gazed at him intently, and 
then exclaimed, “Why, this is my good friend, Yosef the innkeeper! Good Heavens, what 
are you doing here?” Yosef wiped his tears and looked in astonishment at the Inspector. 
It suddenly dawned on him that this was none other than the General whom he had 
entertained in his house that Friday night many years before!  
“Believe me, your Highness, I have done no wrong. I have always minded my own 
business, and have taken no part in any politics. I am as innocent as a baby,” Yosef cried.  
“I have no doubt about it, Yosef,” the Chief Inspector assured him. “Rest assured 
that I shall leave no stone unturned to get you out of here. At last, I shall be able to repay 
you for the friendship and hospitality you showed me that Friday night. I have never 
forgotten the experience of that evening.”  
That General, now the Chief Inspector, personally appeared before the 
investigating committee and vouched for the innocence of the Jewish innkeeper. He told 
them in detail of his experience with the innkeeper, and assured them that from his 
personal knowledge, he had not the slightest doubt that Yosef had no part in any 
conspiracy.  
“He is nothing more than an innkeeper, whose inn is open to all. In between serving 
his customers, he was always busy with his sacred books. How can he be held responsible 
for the actions of customers who found his inn a very attractive place to have a good 
meal?” The Chief Inspector’s words, and his great influence in the highest spheres of the 
Russian government, dispelled all the suspicion directed against Yosef, and he was 
promptly released and sent home. Great was the joy of Yosef’s family when he suddenly 
returned home, a free man.  
“How did this wonderful thing happen?” his wife asked.  
“The Queen has intervened in my behalf,” said Yosef.  
“Which Queen?” she wondered. 
“The Shabbos Queen, of course,” replied Yosef with a smile.

From Kiddush To Havdalah 
Baron Anshel Rothschild, the leading banker in Europe, was a proud Jew and his 
emunah was legendary. Despite his many business dealings, on Shabbos everything came 
grinding to a halt as if nothing else existed and he was simple Jew attached to his Shabbos. 
One time, a large financial institution in England was on the verge of collapse. It 
needed to be rescued by a person or group with great financial means. The directors 
decided that Baron Rothschild would be the perfect candidate. They drafted a proposal 
offering him the company for 2.5 million Pound-Sterling (the money used in England). 
This price was considerably lower than the true value of the company, but still not a 'steal.'   
The telegram was sent on Friday night, and Baron Rothschild completely ignored 
it. The men of the company were nervous and awaited a reply. When no reply was 
forthcoming, the directors understood this as a lack of interest and the next morning sent 
a new proposal for 2.2 million. Of course, Reb Anshel did not pay attention to this 
telegram either because it was Shabbos. Later in the day, out of desperation, the directors 
sent an offer of 1.7 million. This one arrived on Motzei Shabbos and Reb Anshel opened 
it together with the other two, and sent back a positive response to the third offer. 
As soon as he sent off his reply, he gathered his people together to update them on 
the transaction, and more importantly, to teach them a lesson on the koach of 
Shabbos. Shabbos is not only profitable in the next world, but in this one as well for those 
who watch over it. 
Hashem rewards those who keep Shabbos.

No Weekday Talk  
Many years ago, in Eretz Yisroel, there lived a Jew who owned a vineyard.  His 
vineyard was protected on all sides by fences.  One Shabbos afternoon, when the man was 
taking a little walk, he noticed that the fence was broken in one spot. He became very 
worried.   
42 | P a g e 
“If wild animals get into the vineyard, they will eat up my grapes,” he thought, “I 
will no longer earn money from selling grapes. I will have to fix the fence immediately 
after Shabbos.”   
In order to save time, he decided, “I will 
go on Shabbos to someone who knows about 
fence building to find out exactly how to fix the 
fence. The sooner my fence will be repaired, the 
safer my grapes will be. If I know exactly how 
to do it, I will be able to start as soon as Shabbos 
is over.” 
When he came home after talking to the fence expert, he was calm at first, but soon 
he began feeling sorry for his actions. 
“What have I done?” he exclaimed. “I made a terrible mistake! I’m afraid I was 
mechalel Shabbos. During Shabbos, we are not even allowed to talk about forbidden work 
that we plan to do after Shabbos. Hashem wants us to imagine that all work is done. We 
should not worry about future work at all.” He was so sorry that he had discussed the 
fence repair on Shabbos that he decided, “I must do teshuvah. But how?” 
The man thought, “I want to show Hashem I am really sorry that I talked about the 
repair on Shabbos. Therefore, I won’t fix my fence even after Shabbos is over! I will always 
leave it broken, just as it is now.” 
He was, in fact, allowed to repair the fence after Shabbos, but since he was such a 
tzaddik, he didn’t want to benefit from words that were forbidden on Shabbos. 
Hashem rewarded the man by performing a nes, a miracle, for him. A huge tree 
began to grow on the exact spot where the fence was broken and it completely blocked 
the hole in the fence. This tree was a special type called a Tzlaf Tree. It grows delicious 
edible little fruits similar to dates and the leaves can also be eaten. There are always plenty 
of fruits on the tree because the fruits of the Tzlaf Tree grow and regrow very quickly.  
This amazing tree also provided the righteous man with a good income. He was 
able to sell its fruits, which kept growing all the time. From then on, he could learn Torah 
without worrying about money. This was his reward for treating Shabbos properly and 
doing sincere teshuvah.
