43 | P a g e 
Reach for the Gold 
Sam Zeitlin, also known as "Brooklyn Lightning," raised his arms in victory as he 
sped through the finish-line fifty meters ahead of the competition at the 1967 U.S. 
Olympic cycling pre-trials. A wave of happiness washed over him as the crowd lifted his 
exhausted body high in the air. Meanwhile, a grim-faced coach fought his way through the 
mob, and whispered into his trainee's ear. Sam had just been officially disqualified from 
the race because he raised his arms a fraction of a second before crossing the finish-line. 
From the time Sam Zeitlin's father bought him a gleaming red Schwinn 
bicycle on his fifth birthday, Sam dreamed of racing in the Olympics. As an adolescent, 
Sam saved up money and purchased a custom-made track bike. 
Sam wasted no time in entering competitions throughout the New York 
metropolitan area, and won race after race. Scouts soon took notice of Sam's talent and 
perseverance, and he was asked to join the New York Cycling Team. Thereafter, he made 
the American National Cycling Team where he won medals in Israel, across South and 
Central America, the USA and Canada, and Europe. He rode his bike through all weather 
conditions, in an effort to win the Olympic gold medal he dreamed of as a five-year-old. 
A few days after being rejected, Sam went for a bike ride through Long 
Island. As he turned a corner, he heard the frightening screech of a car approaching him. 
Sam's swift reflexes allowed him to swerve onto the sidewalk, thus avoiding the car, 
preventing catastrophic injuries and even death. Stunned, Sam looked over his shoulder, 
and saw his chief cycling rival, Doug, sitting in the driver's seat of a black Corvette. Doug's 
voice rang with venom, "We'll get you next time, Jew!" 
Realizing that he was singled out because of his Jewish religion, Sam decided 
to join the Israeli cycling team. He was determined not to give up on his dream. Sam 
moved to Israel, where his professionalism and upbeat attitude made him a valuable 
member of the Hapoal Tel Aviv Sports Club. He designed a rigorous cycling routine for 
the Israeli athletes, and under his tutelage, his vision of sending an Israeli cycling team to 
the Olympics began to take form. 
When he wasn't training, Sam explored the Israeli cities and countryside. 
Although Sam had basic knowledge of Judaism, he was unaware of the beauty of his 
heritage. Once, while visiting the Western Wall, Sam noticed two teenage boys wearing 
tzitzis and yarmulkas. Sam introduced himself to the boys. After a long discussion, he was 
impressed by their straightforward attitude and ideals. Sam and the boys became good 
44 | P a g e 
friends, and they suggested that he enroll in an English-speaking Yeshiva. 
One day, Sam rode his bike to a Yeshiva in Bnei Brak, parked his bike in front of 
the brick Yeshiva building, and entered. Sam approached a young Rabbi whose gentle face 
was framed by peyos and a thick beard. The Rabbi introduced himself as Rabbi Gershon 
Rabinowitz, and Sam was grateful when the kind man took him under his wing. Sam began 
to warm up to the Yeshiva's atmosphere, and began spending much of his free time there. 
Shabbos became the highlight of his week, as he would join Reb Gershon 
and his family for the Shabbos meals. The Shabbos table set with a sparkling white 
tablecloth, glowing candles, and mouthwatering food filled Sam with warmth and a sense 
of belonging. Reb Gershon discussed words of Torah, and sang zemiros that lifted the 
spirit. Sam especially loved the fast-paced song "Shabbos Hayom L’Hashem - Shabbos is 
the day for Hashem," and it became his mantra. The beat would remain in his mind all 
week long. While Sam practiced racing tactics biking along the coast roads near Caesarea, 
he sang the song to himself, matching its quick tempo to the rhythm of his cycling. 
As Sam's appreciation for Judaism grew, he made changes in his life toward 
becoming a practicing Jew. Sam, by then known by his Jewish name, Shimon, moved from 
the rocky hills of Jerusalem to the even terrain of Bnei Brak. Bnei Brak put Shimon 
adjacent to his beloved Yeshiva, and the town's smooth roads aided his cycling practice. 
As Shimon became more observant, he became increasingly troubled by the 
need to train on Shabbos. Shimon wanted to fully observe the holy Shabbos by abstaining 
from riding his bike, but how could he avoid bike-riding on Saturday when it was 
designated as the official day of cycling practice and trials in Israel? 
Shimon sent a heartfelt petition to the Israeli Sports Federation explaining 
his dilemma. He explained that as an observant Jew, he did not want to desecrate the day 
of Shabbos, and that he was willing to put in extra hours of training every other day of the 
week. The Israeli Sports Federation was unmoved. They sent him a letter stating: "All 
official practices and trials will be held on Saturday, no exceptions." 
Shimon did not give up. Instead, he reminded the Federation that he was the 
only Israeli at world-class cycling standards, and asked again that they make an exception 
for him. The Federation's abrupt reply: "No exceptions will be made." 
Shimon had gone through months of brutal practice in order to compete in the '72 
Olympics in Munich, Germany, and he had already purchased his plane ticket. After 
serious deliberation, Shimon resolved that he would not violate Shabbos, even if it meant 
giving up on his dream of competing in the World Olympics. Israel sent representatives 
45 | P a g e 
in weightlifting, wrestling, fencing, and rifle shooting, but without Shimon, there was no 
Israeli cycling team. 
On the tenth day of the Munich Olympics, the Black September terror group burst 
into the Israeli sleeping quarters and seized eleven athletes. The world watched in horror 
as every one of the hostages was brutally killed. 
The State of Israel entered a state of mourning. In the midst of his grief and anger, 
Shimon trembled when he considered how close he came to certain death. In the days and 
years that followed the massacre, Shimon did not cease to speak of his personal miracle, 
always giving credit to his keeping the holy day of Shabbos.

The Lost Sister  
It was a cool morning in the early spring. Soroh and Ella looked out their window 
at the blue sea. The sun was shining making the water look even more beautiful. “Hurry,” 
called Ella, “or we’ll be late for school.” Soroh and Ella lived in a small town in Holland, 
not far from the sea. A short time later, the two girls were off, and hand in hand they made 
their way toward the little schoolhouse.  
Ella, the elder of the two, was a kind and quiet child with deep blue eyes. But seven 
year old Soroh was quite the opposite. Her merry black eyes twinkled mischievously as 
though she were always contemplating some new prank, or laughing at some mischief she 
had already done. Although the two little girls were so different, they never quarreled. 
Rather, they were greatly attached to each other, and could not bear to be separated. That 
winter, when Soroh had slipped on the ice and injured her foot, Ella did not go to school 
for many days. She sat at her sister’s bedside all the time. When Soroh grew tired of one 
game, Ella would play another, and when Soroh grew tired of playing, Ella would tell her 
stories, or read to her. Although Ella could see the boys and girls frolicking on the ice, and 
could hear their merry shouts, she never left Soroh’s bedside, until Soroh could walk again.  
Finally, Soroh was well enough to return to school. On her first day back, she 
listened intently to the teacher’s lesson. Suddenly, the door was flung open. An excited 
and terrified-looking youth stood in the doorway. In a voice hoarse with terror, he cried, 
46 | P a g e 
“Flee, flee for your lives. The dike has broken! The water is coming up. Run to the plain!” 
A wild panic gripped the children. Everyone stampeded to the door. In the street hundreds 
of people were pushing, running, fleeing for their lives! The mob surged forward at 
breakneck speed, racing with the water. When the plain was sighted, a cry of relief broke 
from the panting people.  
At last they were safe. Everybody was there but little Soroh. She was not able to 
keep up with the mob. Her old wound had reopened and she fell behind. Exhausted, she 
threw herself on the ground, and in a few minutes she was fast asleep. She was blissfully 
unaware that the surging water was coming closer and closer, and in another minute she 
would be devoured by the foaming sea.  
The next morning, Soroh awoke and rubbed her eyes sleepily. “Oh,” she cried 
looking about her. “Where am I?” Soroh lay upon a bed in a little room. “How did I ever 
get here?” she thought to herself. The door opened, and in walked an old woman with 
hard and cruel eyes. “Well, little girl,” she said in a harsh voice, “you have me to thank for 
this. If I hadn’t found you and carried you up the hill to my cottage, you might have 
drowned.” Soroh looked about her in bewilderment. At last she was beginning to 
understand what had occurred. But it was difficult indeed to thank the ferocious-looking 
lady for saving her life. “Five long miles I carried you,” the woman muttered. “You’ll repay 
me for my kindness, though.”  
“But where are my mother and my father?” cried Soroh. “Where is my sister Ella?”  
“Don’t worry about them,” said the old woman. “You’ll be happy enough here 
without them.”  
“But I want to go home,” cried Soroh again. “You have no home any more except 
this home here!” replied the woman. “You have no one left in the world but me. You 
might as well get used to the idea.” The old woman forbade Soroh to mention her parents 
or her sister, and besides, she made Soroh work so hard that she had no time to think of 
them at all. As the days passed, Soroh began to forget her parents, and her home, and her 
dear sister Ella. Saddest of all, Soroh began to forget that she was a Jewish girl. For the 
old woman never lit the Shabbos candles, or made a blessing, or kept Shabbos. So, Soroh, 
too, forgot how to do these things. The old woman’s appearance did not disguise her 
character. She was as mean and cruel as she looked.  
For the three years that she kept Soroh with her, she never had a moment of rest 
from morning till night. With the first light of day, she rose to milk the cows. Then, she 
would lead the cows to pasture and bring them home again. She had to fetch water from 
47 | P a g e 
the well, and keep the house tidy and clean. And not one little speck of dust escaped the 
woman’s eyes. Mercilessly, she worked the little girl until she grew wan and lean. Her 
cheeks lost their rosy look, and her eyes no longer twinkled. Her little hands were rough 
and thin. At night, her little bones ached from the long hours of drudgery and 
backbreaking toil. Never was a little girl as unhappy as Soroh was!  
It was Thursday evening. Soroh was sitting in the kitchen mending some clothes. 
“Soroh,” called the old woman, “come in here.” Obediently, Soroh went into the next 
room and the old woman said to her, “Tomorrow, there will be a fair near the village of 
Yondam. You will take the butter and cheese that you have prepared this afternoon to the 
fair. Be sure that you get a high price, and don’t let anyone fool you. You will start at dawn, 
but you must be back before nightfall, if you don’t want a beating. Now get back to your 
sewing.” As she sewed, Soroh mused about the task that she had to perform the following 
day for the first time. “I hope there will be other people from the neighborhood going to 
the fair. I will surely be afraid to go home all by myself, and Yondam is so far away.”  
The next day, Soroh started for the fair at dawn. She had plodded a good many 
miles along the dirty road before she came to Yondam. But she soon forgot her fatigue. 
She had never seen such a colorful and exciting event. Vendors were calling off their wares, 
farmers and merchants bargaining and talking together. After Soroh had sold the dairy 
products and made some purchases for the old woman, it was nearly dusk, and she started 
reluctantly on her way back. Wearily, she trudged through streets that were deserted, and 
dark.  
Suddenly, through the shutters of a little window she saw the gleam of candles. 
They struck a chord in her memory that had long been forgotten. Somewhere, long ago, 
she had seen these same candles. But when? Where? Soroh could not tear herself away 
from the candles. They held her like a magic spell. She had stopped in front of the little 
house, and was watching the candles flicker and play on the shutters. They stirred a 
wonderful feeling in Soroh’s heart, and suddenly familiar scenes of her childhood came 
back to her. She remembered her mother lighting the candles on Friday night. She 
remembered the Shabbos table, laden with delicious food, everyone sitting about with 
shining eyes, and smiling faces; and she could not resist an impulse to enter the little house 
where the candles shone in the window. “At least, once again, I will see the Shabbos table 
as it was in my own home many years ago,” thought Soroh, and she knocked timidly at 
the door. The next moment, the door opened. “Soroh!” cried Ella. “Is it really you?” she 
cried again, embracing her long-lost sister. “Oh, Ella!” Soroh cried, and fainted. A little 
while later, Soroh was sitting by the table surrounded by her parents and sister. “At last, 
48 | P a g e 
Soroh, you have come home again! We thought you had drowned on that fateful day when 
the dike broke. Where have you been? What brought you back to us?” Soroh told them 
what had happened to her, and with tears of joy gleaming in her eyes, she concluded: “The 
candles, mother! The Shabbos candles, burning in the window, brought me back home!”

The Best Medicine 
Several years ago, Rabbi Kalman Fogel, Principal of the Hebrew Academy of 
Nassau County (HANC) underwent a minor, yet painful, operation. When he was leaving 
the hospital, his doctor prescribed Tylenol with Codeine in order to help manage the pain. 
As you may know, Tylenol does two things: it helps limits pain and it also helps lower a 
fever. Fever can be the body's way of fighting off infection. 
While at home recuperating, Rabbi Fogel took the Tylenol that whole first week. 
By Shabbos, the pain began to recede. Since he was not in much pain by that point, Rabbi 
Fogel knew that Hilchos Shabbos dictate that he should try not to take the pills on 
Shabbos. 
In a strange and scary occurrence, that following Sunday, his fever shot up and 
Rabbi Fogel fainted. His family called the doctor who had operated on him. The surgeon 
told his family to rush Rabbi Fogel straight to the emergency room. 
When he arrived, the doctor did some tests and realized that somehow, a small cut 
had accidentally been made during the operation. That small cut became infected and the 
infection had been spreading into Rabbi Fogel’s bloodstream all week. However, because 
he had been taking the Tylenol, he didn't know, since the medicine had prevented any 
fever. And remember, fever is really Hashem's warning that we may have an infection in 
our bodies. 
The doctor told Rabbi Fogel that he should say Birchas Hagomel in Shul in 
appreciation of how Hashem saved his life. Because of Shabbos, he refrained from taking 
Tylenol for the pain he was in, and as the Tylenol wore off, it stopped ‘hiding’ the fever, 
which was there the whole time, getting worse and worse! By the end of Shabbos when 
the fever spiked, Rabbi Fogel had just enough time to reach the hospital and start serious 
IV antibiotics. If not for Shabbos, it's quite possible that he would not be alive to share 
this amazing story. 
49 | P a g e 
Reb Ariel and the Lion 
Many years ago, a certain Rabbi decided to travel to the Eretz Yisroel. He joined a 
caravan of traders, travelers, and camels, which was leaving for the East and Eretz Yisroel. 
After traveling many days through Morocco in the 
desert, the Rabbi realized that the day was Friday, Erev 
Shabbos, and the sun was descending on the horizon. As 
Shabbos approached, still distant from their next town, the 
man realized that he would have to stop. So he asked the 
non-Jewish caravan leader to stop and rest for Shabbos. 
“I am sorry,” the leader replied, “but I cannot delay 
everyone else for the sake of just one traveler. We must go 
on!” Of course, the Rabbi could not travel on Shabbos, so 
he got down from his camel and took his bag with him and 
announced that he would remain in the desert for the next 
day. 
"What?? Are you insane?" cried his fellow travelers. "The desert is no place for you 
to remain alone. The sun is hot, the wind is harsh, the land is dry, and there are wild 
animals that will tear you apart. Not even a camel would want to stay." The caravan 
members argued with him for an hour, but he would not budge, so regretfully, they 
continued on their way to Tunis, leaving the Rabbi to spend Shabbos alone in the desert. 
As the afternoon drew to a close, the Rabbi took a stick and drew a circle in the 
sand, and there he began to daven Mincha followed by Kabbalas Shabbos and Maariv. 
Then he sat himself down to the Shabbos meal and sang some beautiful zemiros. 
As he was singing, the Rabbi saw a lion coming toward him from a distance. But 
the man was not afraid. He began to say Birkas Hamazon (bentching) and as the lion 
approached the circle drawn around the man, it merely stopped, crouched, and sat down. 
It seemed that this lion did not arrive to eat the man. Rather, he arrived to protect him. 
In the morning, the man awoke, davened Shacharis, and opened a sefer to learn. 
The lion kept watch throughout the day. 
When evening came and the stars twinkled in the sky, the lion got up and stretched. 
Then it came closer to the Rabbi, wagging its tail and motioning for the Rabbi to climb on 
its back. The Rabbi took his bag and climbed up onto the lion’s back, and suddenly the 
animal was off running. All night, the lion hurried over the desert sands, and in the 
50 | P a g e 
morning light, the Rabbi could see his caravan a short distance ahead. 
The other travelers were shocked when they saw the Rabbi riding on the back of a 
lion. They were even more surprised when the lion bent its knees and carefully let the 
Rabbi off its back. Then the lion got up, roared fiercely, shook its mane, and vanished into 
the desert. 
Now the travelers realized that even a lion knew that the Rabbi was a very holy 
man. The leader of the caravan bowed down on his knees before the Rabbi and begged 
for his forgiveness. 
From that day on, the people called the Rabbi Ariel, meaning "the Lion (Ari) of 
Hashem.” 
The Value of Keeping Shabbos 
The Golden Coin 
One Shabbos afternoon in Yerushalayim, young Yitzchak Eisenbach was walking 
and he spotted a very valuable gold coin shining on the ground in the distance. The value 
of the coin was enough to support his entire family for two weeks! But it was Shabbos, 
and young Yitzchak knew that the coin was muktzeh on the Shabbos. 
He decided to put his foot on top of the coin and guard it until Shabbos was over - a good number of hours later - but worth every moment of the wait. 
An Arab boy saw Yitzchak with his foot strangely and obviously strategically placed, 
and decided to investigate further. 
"What's that you're hiding?" the Arab asked. 
"Nothing," Yitzchak replied, as he shifted his body to hide the fact that he was 
guarding a golden treasure. 
It was too late. The Arab boy pushed him, saw the coin, quickly grabbed it 
51 | P a g e 
and ran away. All Yitzchak could do was watch in horror as his attacker disappeared into 
the Arab marketplace. 
Dejected, Yitzchak sulked to the nearby Tzcernobel Bais Medrash and sat in a 
corner. Normally, Yitzchak would help prepare the final Shabbos meal, but today he sat, 
dejected and depressed, until Shabbos was over. The Rebbe asked young Yitzchok why 
he was so sad and upset and Yitzchak told the story. 
The Tzcernobel Rebbe (1840-1936) asked Yitzchak to come visit him after 
Shabbos. When Yitzchok came into his study he saw that the Rebbe held a gold coin, 
exactly the same size as the one Yitzchak had almost secured earlier that day. 
"I am very proud of you," said the Rebbe. You did not desecrate Shabbos even for 
a tremendous monetary gain. In fact," he continued, "I am so proud of you that I am 
willing to give you this same coin." The Rebbe halted. "On one condition. I want you to 
give me the reward for the mitzvah you did." 
The boy looked at the Rebbe in utter disbelief. "You want to trade the coin for the 
mitzvah?" 
The Rebbe nodded, slowly. 
"If that's the case, keep the coin. I'll keep the mitzvah." Yitzchak replied. 
The Rebbe leaned over and kissed the child. He saw that Yitzchak understood the 
greatness of Shabbos.

Yosef the Spy 
It was in the middle of the Second World War, and Yosef was fleeing Europe and 
trying to enter Canada. As he reached the border, the border patrol agent asked him his 
name. 
“Yosef,” he replied. 
The agent looked at his papers and asked, “Are you sure your name is Yosef?” 
“Yes, that is my name,” he answered. 
He took a look at his papers again, and stared him straight in the eye and said, 
52 | P a g e 
“Yosef? Come with me. You’re not going anywhere.” 
“What did I do?” asked Yosef. 
“We know what you’re up to” the guard said to him. “We know why you’re coming 
here. You’re a spy. You’re coming into our country to spy on us. You want to tell our 
enemies all of our secrets. We can’t let you into our country. Do you know where you’re 
going to go? You’re going straight to prison.” And they took him and locked him up in 
prison. 
Yosef sat in his cell, broken and sad. He had done nothing wrong. They made up a 
story about him! He wasn’t a spy! He was sitting there in the tiny cell all by himself, with 
nothing but a small sink and a tiny hard bench to lay down on. So he stayed there all alone. 
Day after day, week after week. 
Soon, however, other Yidden heard about Yosef. They started working right away 
to get him out of jail. 
“We know who Yosef is!” they said. “We know that he’s not a spy. We have to help 
him. We have to get him out of jail.” Jewish activists started calling and contacting 
everyone they could in the Canadian government. It took some time but they were finally 
able to convince the Canadian Government that Yosef was not a spy. So, the government 
sent a soldier to the jail with a special message that Yosef was allowed to leave the jail!  
The soldier headed to the prison on Friday. By the time he arrived at the jail, 
it was already close to Shabbos. Meanwhile, Yosef was sitting in his jail cell, davening to 
Hashem, hoping that He would help him.  
The soldier asked, “Are you Yosef?” 
“Yes,” answered Yosef. 
“I’m here to release you from this prison. 
You just need to sign these forms. When you sign 
these papers, you are free to leave." The soldier 
informed him. 
Yosef replied, “I’m sorry, but I cannot do 
that.” 
“You WHAT?” asked the soldier, 
surprised. “If you don’t sign these papers, you are 
going to stay here in jail.” 
“I’m sorry,” said Yosef. “But the sun has 
53 | P a g e 
set and it is now Shabbos. And on Shabbos, Jews are not allowed to write. I cannot sign 
those papers.” 
The soldier said, “I understand, but I will give you one more chance. Sign the papers 
or you aren’t going anywhere. You are going to stay here.” 
“I’m sorry,” said Yosef. “You’re not going to get me to sign those papers on 
Shabbos. Even if it means staying here.” The soldier left, and Yosef was once again left 
alone in his jail cell. 
It took several more weeks for the people to speak to the government again and to 
plead with them to grant Yosef another pardon. Finally, another soldier was sent to free 
him, this time not on Shabbos.  
Yosef was willing to spend more time in jail, so that he wouldn’t be mechalel 
Shabbos.  
The Aroma of Shabbos 
o
Years ago, the tzaddik, Rav Simcha Wasserman, lived in Los Angeles. A family who 
hadn’t been religious for long, who had become baalei teshuva because of Rav Simcha's 
influence, were planning on making a Bar Mitzvah for their oldest son. They consulted 
with Rav Simcha about every detail of the affair. Since most of their family was not yet 
religious, to prevent chillul Shabbos, they offered to host their extended family and guests 
in a hotel for the entire Shabbos. They also asked the guests who couldn’t stay for the 
whole Shabbos not to drive there on Shabbos.  
A short while before the Bar Mitzvah, Rav Simcha called to wish them mazal tov 
and to tell them that regrettably, he would be unable to attend their simcha. During the 
conversation, Rav Simcha asked the mother if she had made cholent for their guests. She 
replied “No,” her guests were not used to Shabbos, and the family was a bit hesitant to 
serve such ‘simple fare’ to their elegant guests. Rav Simcha asked her to prepare a cholent 
anyway. 
The morning of the Bar Mitzvah, a car stopped by the hotel and the Bar Mitzvah 
boy's father's elderly aunt and uncle got out and entered the hall where the Bar Mitzvah 
54 | P a g e 
lunch was being held.  
As the entire family was sitting around the table enjoying each other's company, the 
waiter entered the dining room and set a huge tureen of piping hot cholent on the table. 
At just that moment, the elderly aunt jumped up, quickly excused herself from the table 
and raced out of the room. Her husband ran after her. 
After Shabbos, the aunt called her nephew and his wife to explain her sudden and 
hasty departure. 
"As you all know," the elderly aunt began, "I remember little of my life before the 
war. I had been taken from my home as a young girl of six one Shabbos morning. I spent 
the war in concentration camps and somehow, I ended up here in America. I remembered 
nothing of my early life in Poland, or of my family traditions. I grew up and lived a non
religious life. Today, in the hotel, the smell of the cholent triggered memories of my 
youth.” 
The family sat in stunned silence, waiting for her to continue. 
"It was the smell of that cholent that did it. When they placed that bowl of hot 
cholent on the table, I felt myself propelled back in time. Suddenly, I remembered myself 
as a young child, sitting at the Shabbos table with my parents, my older sister and my two 
younger brothers. My mother had just placed the tureen of cholent on the table when the 
soldiers entered and took us away. I was the only one to survive.  
Now, all of a sudden, I remember them. I remember my home, my family, my roots. 
Thanks to the smell of that amazing cholent." She looked directly at the mother of the Bar 
Mitzvah boy, "I now have a past."  
The elderly aunt and her husband eventually became Torah observant Jews.

The Chofetz Chaim's “Shabbos” 
About 35 years ago, a certain Rav was visiting Miami, Florida. While he was there, 
he gave a speech about the life of the famed Chofetz Chaim. He described the life of the 
great tzaddik, who lived a humble life as a shopkeeper in the village of Radin, Poland, yet 
was recognized throughout the Jewish world as one of the Gedolei Yisroel, a great Talmid 
55 | P a g e 
Chochom, tzaddik and leader. 
The Rav wanted to tell another story about the Chofetz Chaim, but he hesitated, 
for he only knew part of the story. As he stood there at the front of the Shul, he thought 
for a moment and then decided that he would tell it anyway. He thought to himself that 
even an unfinished story about the Chofetz Chaim would have a meaningful message. 
He began to tell about a teenage boy in the Chofetz Chaim's Yeshiva who was 
found smoking a cigarette on Shabbos! The Hanhalah (faculty) and the other students 
were shocked, and some of the faculty felt that the boy should be sent out of the Yeshiva. 
However, when the Chofetz Chaim heard the story, he asked that the boy be brought to 
his home. 
At this point, the Rav interrupted the story and said, "I don't know what the Chofetz 
Chaim said to the boy. I only know that they were together for only a few minutes, and I 
am told that the boy never desecrated the Shabbos again. I would give anything to know 
what he said to this student. How wonderful it would be if we could relay that message - 
whatever it was - to others, in order to encourage them in their observance of Shabbos." 
The Rav then continued with his speech. 
After his talk, the hall emptied of everyone except for one elderly man, who 
remained in his seat, deep in thought. From a distance, it seemed he was trembling, as if 
he were either crying or suffering from chills. The Rav walked over to the elderly man and 
asked him, "Is anything wrong?" 
The man responded, "Where did you hear that story of the cigarette on Shabbos?" 
He did not look up and was still shaken.  
"I really don't know," answered the Rav. "I heard it a while ago and I don't even 
remember who told it to me."  
The man looked up at the Rav and said softly, "I was that boy!" He then asked the 
Rav if they could go outside, and as the two walked together, he told the Rav the following 
story: 
"This story happened in the 1920's when the Chofetz Chaim was in his 
eighties. I was terrified to have to go into his house and face him. But when I 
did go into his home, I looked around with disbelief at the poverty in which he 
lived. It was unimaginable to me that a man of his stature would be satisfied to 
live in such surroundings. 
"Suddenly, he was in the room where I was waiting. He was remarkably 
short. At that time I was a teenager and he only came up to my shoulders. He 
56 | P a g e 
took my hand and clasped it tenderly in both of his. He brought my hand in his 
own up to his face, and when I looked into his soft face, his eyes were closed 
for a moment. 
"When he opened them, they were filled with tears. He then said to me 
in a hushed voice full of pain and astonishment, ‘Shabbos!' And he started to 
cry. He was still holding both of my hands in his, and while he was crying, he 
repeated with astonishment, ‘Shabbos, the heilige Shabbos!' 
"My heart started pounding and I became more frightened than I had 
ever felt before. Tears streamed down his face; one of them rolled onto my 
hand. I thought it would bore a hole right through my skin. When I think of 
that tear today, I can still feel its heat. I can't describe how awful it felt to know 
that I had made the great tzaddik weep. But in his rebuke - which consisted 
only of those few words - I felt that he was not angry, but rather sad and fearful. 
He seemed frightened at the consequences of my actions." 
The elderly man then caressed the hand that bore the invisible scar of a precious 
tear. It had become his permanent reminder to observe the "Holy Shabbos" for the rest 
of his life. 
o
The Write Thing To Do  
During World War II, Harav Gedaliah Moshe Goldman, the Z'viler Rebbe, was 
sent to a Siberian labor camp. It was a miserable, back-breaking experience, but at least it 
was not a Nazi extermination camp. One Shabbos, the commander of the camp 
summoned both the Rebbe and another Jew, a frail, old man, to his office. "You are both 
free to go. All you have to do is sign these papers and go," he said. 
Rav Gedaliah Moshe reached for the papers, and stopped. ‘How could I write on 
Shabbos?’ he thought, ‘True, it was a release, but can I desecrate the holy Shabbos? After 
all, as bad as it is here, it is not life-threatening. I am young and strong. Even if I would be 
detained here for a few more years, I would probably survive.’ 
"No, I am sorry, sir. While I appreciate your kind gesture, I cannot desecrate my 
57 | P a g e 
Holy Shabbos," the Rebbe replied. 
"Are you insane?" the commander screamed. "I am granting you freedom. How 
can you waste such an opportunity?" 
"I understand and appreciate your kindness, but today is Shabbos. I may not write," 
the Rebbe reiterated. 
"If you do not sign these papers right now, you will stay in this place," the 
commander responded with disgust. He then pushed the papers to the old Jew and said, 
"Okay, now, you sign the release papers." 
"I am afraid that I cannot sign either. The same law applies to me," the old man 
said. 
"You two are both insane!" the commander said in disgust as he retrieved the 
papers. 
"Wait!" said Rav Gedaliah Moshe. "I will sign his papers. Let him go free." 
"I do not understand. You just told me that you cannot write on Shabbos. Yet, you 
are willing to sign his papers. Have you taken total leave of your senses? Why are his papers 
different from yours?" the commander asked in shock. 
"There is a major difference," the Rebbe explained. "I am young and strong. I can 
survive here. He, on the other hand, is old and weak. He will not make it. Therefore, if he 
is not prepared to sign, I will sign for him." 
The commander was so impressed by this act of selflessness that he allowed them 
both to leave, without demanding their signatures at all.

The Cow That Kept Shabbos 
There once lived a very righteous Yid in Eretz Yisroel. Every weekday, he led his 
cow into the field where she plowed the ground for him. When Shabbos arrived, the Yid, 
of course, did not work. He rested as Hashem commanded. His cow also rested every 
Shabbos, because the Torah forbids a person to have their animals work on Shabbos. 
Time passed, and the man became so poor that he was forced to sell his cow in 
order to have money for food. He found a non-Jew who was willing to buy his cow. The 
58 | P a g e 
non-Jew paid for the cow and led her to his barn. Bright and early each morning, the non
Jew took the cow out to the field to plow, from morning till evening. 
That Saturday morning, the non-Jew woke up early as usual. He went to the barn 
and led his cow out to the field, but today the cow was acting strangely. She laid down and 
refused to move. “Perhaps she is a little lazy. I will have to teach her a lesson,” he thought. 
He began to hit the cow with a stick but the cow still refused to budge. What use was there 
for a cow that would not work? He would have to return it to the man who had sold the 
animal to him. 
The non-Jew hurried to the house of the Jew who had sold him the cow. “I have a 
terrible problem with the cow you sold me,” he complained, “all week long, she worked 
just fine, but today she became lazy and all of a sudden she is refusing to move. I hit her, 
but it did not help at all.”  
The Jewish man understood everything. His cow had never worked on Shabbos. 
She was used to resting on the seventh day. “Let's go to your barn,” he told the non-Jew. 
“I will try to take care of your problem.” The two men walk over to the non-Jew's farm. 
There they saw the cow resting on the ground, motionless. The Jewish man bent over the 
cow and began to whisper in her ear, “My dear cow,” he said, “As long as you were mine, 
we both rested on Shabbos, but I unfortunately I became poor and I was forced to sell 
you to this man. He is your new owner. He does not have Shabbos. You are allowed to 
work for him every single day of the week.”  
When the cow heard these words, she immediately stood up on her feet, ready to 
begin work. The non-Jew stared in surprise. “Well, I must say, I've never seen anything 
like this. What magic have you done?” he asked in amazement. “What did you tell her that 
made her willing to work? You must tell me the secret so I'll know what to do if it ever 
happens again.” 
The Jew explained about Shabbos. He explained that Hashem created the world in 
six days and rested on the seventh. He commanded the Jews in the Torah to also rest from 
work every Shabbos. “Is that so?” exclaimed the non-Jew in great wonder. He continued 
to think, “This animal has no understanding, yet it knows the difference between Shabbos 
and a weekday. Shouldn't I, a human being, also serve Hashem and keep His 
commandments?” He decided then and there to become a Jew. So the farmer became a 
Ger and began learning Torah until he became one of the great Chachomim whose 
teachings are mentioned in the Gemara. He was known by the name of Rabbi Yochanan 
Ben Torsa. In the Aramaic language, the name Ben Torsa meant ‘son of the cow’ for it 
was a cow that caused him to become a Jew.  
59 | P a g e 
The Carpet Test 
Many years ago, there was a man who lived in the Netherlands. He owned a very 
successful carpet business in The Hague. Among his many customers was the land’s royal 
family.  
One Friday night, the man and his family were seated around the Shabbos table 
singing zemiros when there was a knock at the door. A messenger from Crown Prince 
Hendrick had come to request that a shipment of expensive carpets be sent to the royal 
palace at once. The Jew replied that he did not conduct business on Shabbos. The 
messenger responded that the carpets were needed for a party which the prince was 
hosting that very night! The Jew countered that while he understood the urgency of the 
matter, his religious beliefs made it impossible for him to honor the prince's request. 
The messenger returned twice more that night. The last time, he came with a written 
request penned by the prince himself. The request ended with a threat the Jew would 
suffer greatly were he to ignore the order. Again, the Jew apologized, but stood firm. He 
knew that the current law would not permit the prince to harm him physically or take any 
legal action against him. At worst, he would lose his best customer and suffer a tremendous 
loss of profit. These consequences he willingly accepted for the sake of Shabbos. 
The man's sons, boys in their teens, asked why he did not permit the (non-Jewish) 
messenger to take the key to the store and help himself to whatever he needed. Surely this 
would involve a smaller loss than that which their father would suffer by refusing the 
prince’s request. Hearing this, the man’s aged father told his grandchildren, “Silence 
children! Instead of offering your suggestions, you should rejoice over this great 
opportunity to make a sacrifice for the sake of Hashem and his Shabbos. Listen to your 
father, and do not worry about the consequences.” 
After Shabbos, the Jew was called to Prince Hendrick’s chambers. Fearing the 
worst, he honored the summons and came before the prince. The prince welcomed him 
warmly and asked that he be seated. The prince said, “This past week, I had an argument 
with a friend. I said that a truly religious Jew places his beliefs in Hashem and His Torah 
above all. My friend, however, insisted that to Jews, money comes before anything. We 
decided to test one Jew and see who was right. You were the one whom we chose. 
Obviously, you passed the test and have proven me right.” 
From then on, the Jew was given special privileges in doing business with the royal 
family. And when a member of the royal family visited Eretz Yisroel, he brought back a 
60 | P a g e 
o
gift for this Jew – a pair of Tefillin! 
Army Walk and Talk 
Rabbi Dr. Phillip Zimmerman was the founder of Freeda vitamins. In 1953, he 
served as chaplain in the US Army and was stationed at Fort Gordon in Georgia.  
One Friday night, a serviceman from New York came in and said, “Chaplain, I need 
help. I have three letters here. The first says that I’m supposed to be transferred to Korea 
on Sunday morning. The second and third letters are from my parents’ cardiologist. My 
parents and I went through the Holocaust and I am their only child. If they find out that 
I was transferred to Korea, they will die from the trauma of it, the cardiologist writes.” He 
showed the chaplain the letters. “Please,” the soldier pleaded, “please try to help me!”  
Rabbi Zimmerman had no idea how he could help the young man; chaplains had 
very little say in such matters. But he did not let that stop him.  
The next morning, which was Shabbos, he walked six miles to the military 
commander on the base.  
“Rabbi Zimmerman, what are you doing here so early?” the commander asked.  
“I need a favor. A serviceman came to me last night and told me he’s supposed to 
be transferred to Korea, but his parents, who are Holocaust survivors, are so frail that they 
will literally die of fright if he is transferred. I have letters from cardiologists attesting to 
this.” Rabbi Zimmerman replied, showing the letters.  
“What are you talking about?” the commander snapped angrily without even 
looking at the letters. “The United States is at war!” Then he grabbed the lapel of the 
chaplain’s jacket. The official jacket of the chaplaincy had the Aseres Hadibros on its lapel. 
“You see these tablets? They’re made of stone. Do you know why they’re made of stone? 
Because stone can’t be broken. Well, my laws are like stone: they can’t be broken. There 
are no exceptions!”  
Rabbi Zimmerman felt terrible. It was obvious that there was nothing he could do, 
so he turned around and left. He hadn’t gone far when, all of a sudden, the military 
commander called out, ”Rabbi Zimmerman, where’s your jeep?”  
61 | P a g e 
“I have no jeep, sir.”  
“What do you mean, you have no jeep? How did you get here?”  
“I walked, sir.”  
“You walked six miles from your camp to here?”  
“I walked, sir.”  
“How are you getting back?”  
“I’m going to walk the six miles again.”  
“Why didn’t you take a jeep or have someone drive you?”  
“Because it’s my Sabbath and I can’t drive a car or be driven in one.”  
“You walked six miles for that serviceman and you’re going to walk another six 
miles for him?”  
“Yes, sir.”  
“Come back to my office,” said the commander. The commander looked over the 
letters, picked up his phone and called his second-in-command, ordering that the 
serviceman be transferred to Governor’s Island in NY, with a direct command and explicit 
instructions to visit his parents every night.

By the Port 
Rav Yaakov Yosef Herman and his wife decided that they would live the rest of 
their lives in Eretz Yisroel. After months of packing, they were ready to set sail to begin 
their voyage. And so, near the end of August 1939, the great tzaddik, Rav Yaakov Yosef 
and his wife parted from their family, loaded their earthly belongings aboard a ship, and 
left America to settle in the holy land. 
The original schedule called for their ship to dock at the port of Haifa on a 
Wednesday. They would spend a few days resting in Haifa after which the Hermans would 
continue their journey to Yerushalayim, where they would live.  
On September 1st, 1939, World War Two began. The captain took a circular route 
to avoid possible sea-mines and torpedoes, and the vessel reached the port of Haifa late 
62 | P a g e 
on Friday afternoon, only a short time before sunset. 
As soon as the boat docked, soldiers ordered all passengers to disembark 
immediately and claim their baggage at once. All the passengers would be responsible to 
retrieve their luggage and to pass through customs before being allowed to enter into 
Palestine. 
It was clear to Rav Yaakov Yosef that if they stuck around to claim their belongings, 
they would not make it anywhere before Shabbos. He grabbed the suitcase that held a 
small Sefer Torah, along with his Tallis and Tefillin. His wife took her hand luggage and 
they asked to speak to the officer in charge. 
With tears streaming down his face, Rav Yaakov Yosef turned to the highest
ranking officer and said, “All of my life I have been extra careful and have never once 
been mechalel Shabbos. Now, when I finally reach the Holy Land I will need to desecrate 
it?”  
To which the officer responded, “War has started. There is nothing to be done.”  
“Just stamp our passports and let us through. We’ll pick up our baggage after 
Shabbos,” begged Rav Herman.   
The officer then explained, “That would be impossible. We are removing all the 
baggage from the ship and leaving it on the pier." 
“I don’t care about the luggage,” replied Rav Herman, “please just stamp our 
passports so we can leave.”  
The officer looked at Rav Herman and asked, “How much baggage do you have?”  
“Sixteen crates in the hold and nine suitcases in our cabin,” came the reply.  
“Do you realize,” the officer added, “that once you leave, your baggage will be on 
the pier with no one responsible for it? By tomorrow night, the sailors and local Arabs will 
make short work of your belongings. You will not find a shred of your belongings left.” 
“I have no choice,” Rav Herman explained. “It’s almost time for Shabbos. We must 
get to the city before sunset. Please, please just clear our passports and let us go.”  
The officer, astounded, called to another officer, “Stamp their passports and let 
them through. This rabbi is willing to lose all his belongings to get into the city in time for 
their Shabbos.”  
As soon as the Herman’s were off the boat, they jumped into a taxi which rushed 
them to their Shabbos destination. They arrived just in time for Mrs. Herman to light 
Shabbos candles. Over that Shabbos, Rav Herman was elated, repeating over and over, 
“The Boss (Hashem) does everything for me. What could I ever do for him? Now at least 
I have the merit to give all for the Boss for his mitzvah of Shabbos and to be Mekadesh 
63 | P a g e 
Hashem!" 
After Rav Herman made Havdalah, his host suggested they go back to the port to 
see if any of their belongings remained. The Hermans had both given up hope on seeing 
any of their belongings ever again, but went along anyway. When they got there, everything 
was pitch black except for a small area at the end of the dock. As they approached the 
area, they heard a man with an English accent call out, “Who goes there?”  
Rav Herman answered, “Some passengers from the boat that docked late yesterday 
afternoon.” The guard approached them and asked, “What is your name?”   
“Jacob Josef Herman.”  
“Well, well, Rabbi, it’s about time you put in your appearance. I was assured that 
you would be here the minute the sun set. You are a few hours late. I have been responsible 
for your baggage for more than twenty-four hours. My commanding officer said he would 
have my head if any of your baggage is missing. Kindly check to see that all is in order and 
sign these papers. Please remove it as quickly as possible…I am totally exhausted.”

The Embarrassed Challah 
Reb Shraga Feivel Mendlowitz, one of the great architects of Torah education in 
America, was once a guest at someone’s home for the Shabbos evening meal. Upon 
arriving home from Shul that night, Reb Shraga Feivel and his host found the table not 
completely set. The woman of the house, after an exhausting day of preparing for Shabbos, 
had fallen asleep without having finished setting up. Upset and embarrassed before his 
honored guest, the husband criticized his wife in front of Reb Shraga Feivel for what he 
thought was his wife’s laziness. 
Later, Reb Shraga Feivel pointed out the following. “Really, the halacha is that we 
should make the brachah on the bread before the wine. However, only with regard to 
Kiddush does the brachah over wine come before bread. On Shabbos, the lechem 
mishnah (two Challah loaves) on the Shabbos table are covered, so that they should not 
be shamed since we are passing over them, as they usually come first." 
Reb Shraga Feivel continued. "The halacha is teaching us an important lesson. We 
cover the Challos, these inanimate objects, in order to be sensitive to their “feelings,” so 
64 | P a g e 
they should not ‘feel’ shamed. Should we not be even more careful about and sensitive to 
the feelings of other people who really do have feelings?"  
And here this man was, shaming his wife for not covering the lechem mishnah! 
Reb Shraga Feivel concluded that we must be careful not to perform a mitzvah out 
of habit, but as a way of fulfilling Hashem’s will. In this way, we will think before we act 
and continuously grow as true servants of Hashem.

The Shalom Bayis Kugel 
A husband and wife came to Rabbi Yisroel of Koznitz (the “Koznitzer Maggid,” 
1737–1814). “It’s my wife,” complained the man, “every week for Shabbos she makes a 
delicious kugel. I love that kugel! All week I work and schlep, just to enjoy that kugel! 
When I just think of that kugel, my mouth starts watering. But what does she do to me? 
She bothers me! After I say Kiddush, do I get the kugel? No-o-o-o. First she serves the 
fish, then the soup, then the chicken, and the potatoes. Then, after a couple of other 
dishes, when I’m full and can’t possibly take one more bite, THEN she brings in the kugel! 
Now shouldn’t I be upset with her? A kugel belongs right after hamotzi!”  
The wife explained that in her parents’ home it was always done this way. The kugel 
is served near the end of the meal. She wouldn’t budge on her position. He insisted it 
should be served right after the Challah. 
So, the Koznitzer Maggid decided that from now on. she should make two kugels. 
One to be eaten right after Kiddush, and one to serve after the fish and the soup and the 
chicken and the potatoes. The couple left, both feeling happy with the Maggid’s verdict. 
From that day on, the Koznitzer Maggid always had two kugels at his Shabbos 
table—one right after Kiddush, and another one after the main course.  
They called it the Shalom Bayis Kugel (“Peace in the home kugel”).

Consideration for the Cook 
A student of Harav Yisroel Salanter, zt”l, once invited his Rebbe to spend Shabbos 
at his home. Unsure about whether he should accept the invitation, Rav Yisroel told him 
that he had the custom never to stay in any home unless he was familiar with the way the 
household was run. 
The talmid was quick to reassure Rav Yisroel that his home was run strictly 
according to halacha. “We buy only glatt-kosher meat from the most reliable butcher in 
town,” he said. “The cook in our home is a religious woman, the widow of a great talmid 
chachom. My wife checks everything in the kitchen herself, too. On Friday night, we have 
a beautiful seudah with lots of zemiros and divrei Torah between courses, and the meal 
ends late at night.”  
Rav Yisroel agreed to accept his talmid’s invitation on one condition: that the 
Friday-night seudah last no more than two hours. The talmid was very surprised by the 
request. He would have thought that his Rebbi would enjoy speaking words of Torah late 
into the night. Still, having no choice, he agreed to Rav Yisroel’s condition.  
At the Friday-night seudah, the courses were served quickly, one after the other, 
with very little singing or divrei Torah between them. Before bentching, Rav Yisroel’s 
student asked his Rebbi to please explain why he had insisted on rushing the meal to such 
a degree. By way of response, Rav Yisroel asked that the cook be brought in.     
“I’d like to ask your forgiveness for tiring you out this evening by rushing through 
the meal,” Rav Yisroel said to her.  
“Oh, there’s definitely no need to apologize!” the woman exclaimed. “In fact, I can’t thank 
you enough. I wish you were here every week! I am always so tired on Friday night after 
working all day to cook everything for Shabbos, but I must wait until the family finishes 
their meal. Now I can go home early and get a good night’s sleep.” 
“There is the answer to your question,” Rav Yisroel replied.

The Black Carriage 
On Simchas Torah in the year 1798, an armed Russian officer arrived in Liozna to 
66 | P a g e 
arrest Rabbi Schneur Zalman of Liadi. Simchas Torah fell on Thursday that year and 
within a short time, the Rebbe was seated in the infamous "Black Carriage” which was 
reserved by the Czarist regime for rebels who were under capital sentence. Covered on all 
sides with heavy black metal panels, and with no windows whatsoever, it was designed to 
cast dread on all those who saw it. Guarded by heavily armed soldiers, the black carriage 
pulled out of Liozna on Thursday night and clanked its fearsome way down the highway 
to the city of Petersburg, deep inside Russia, many hundreds of miles away. 
At half past ten the next morning, some six hours before candle-lighting time, the 
Rebbe asked that they stop where they were until after Shabbos. The officer in charge 
ignored his request. A moment later, the axles of the carriage broke. No sooner had they 
repaired them when one of the horses collapsed and died. Fresh horses were brought, but 
they could not move the carriage from its place. By this time the guards realized that it 
would be impossible to continue on with their journey against the Rebbe's will, so they 
asked their prisoner if they could detour to a nearby village, and spend the next day - 
Shabbos - there. The Rebbe refused, but did agree that the carriage be moved off the 
highway to an adjacent field. 
The spot at which the Rebbe spent that Shabbos is about three miles from the 
village of Seliba-Rudnia, which is near the town of Nevel. An old Chassid who survived 
into the twentieth century, Reb Michael of Nevel, used to relate that he knew Chassidim 
who were able to point out the exact spot at which the Rebbe had spent that lonely 
Shabbos. He himself had gone there to see it with his own eyes. All the way there, he had 
seen old and drooping trees on both sides of the road, but that memorable spot was 
marked by a tall tree with luxuriant foliage. 
o
The Gauntlet 
Rabbi Yaakov Yisroel Kanievsky was known as the Steipler Gaon, because he came 
from the town of Haron Steipel. He grew up to become one of the leaders of Klal Yisroel. 
He also wrote many important seforim. 
The Steipler spent his life studying Torah and doing Mitzvos. He used to study until 
very late at night and then get up very early the next morning. He didn’t even notice his 
67 | P a g e 
lack of sleep because he loved to learn Torah so much. Before he became engaged to the 
sister of the Chazon Ish, he shared a story that occurred to him in the Russian army. 
When the Steipler was a young man, he was forced to become a soldier in the 
Russian army. As a soldier forced into the Czar's army, the Steipler was forced to perform 
back-breaking labor in the frigid cold of the Siberian winter. Regardless of the 
overwhelming toil and below-freezing conditions, he continued the difficult work because 
he knew it was the only way that he could continue to serve Hashem. The problem was 
that army demanded that everyone work seven days a week. This created a problem on 
Shabbos. The Steipler emphatically declared that by no means was he going to work on 
Shabbos. The Russian official did not need more than one insolent Jew who refused his 
orders. The captain in charge flew into a rage, typical of the anti-Semitic brute that he was. 
Suddenly, he stopped screaming, as a diabolical smile crossed his face. 
He would grant the Steipler's request on the condition that the soldier pass a little 
test. If he could prove himself to be a strong warrior, he would be permitted to observe 
Shabbos. The test was "simple." The captain ordered his soldiers to form two rows 
opposite each other, arming themselves with short clubs. The Steipler was to "attempt" to 
make it from one end of the row to the other as the soldiers beat him mercilessly with 
their truncheons. If he survived the ordeal, he would be allowed to observe Shabbos. 
The Steipler understood the gravity of the situation. He put his hands over 
his head as protection, whispered a heartfelt tefillah and started to head through the 
gauntlet. The guards began to beat him with all they had: no mercy; no sensitivity. The 
pain was intolerable, but the reward of keeping Shabbos was the pot of gold at the end of 
the rainbow. Blood streamed forth from his cuts and gashes. Inch by inch, he trudged 
forward, blinded by pain and covered with blood. He reached the end of the line and 
collapsed - with a faint smile on his lips. He had made it! The Shabbos that he cared about 
so much must have surely protected him. The captain reluctantly gave in to the Steipler's 
demand to observe Shabbos. The Steipler lay on the ground, a bloodied. Nobody bothered 
to pick him up, but he did not care. He had triumphed over the cruel officer. He had 
triumphed over the yetzer hara, evil-inclination. He had won in honor of Shabbos Kodesh.  
o
68 | P a g e 
Shabbos on Thursday 
Many years ago, there was a certain Rav in the town of Yanov. He was a fine Talmid 
Chacham and Yirei Shamayim, but he was also extremely stubborn.  
One day, the Rav was travelling to another town for the chassuna of his son. It was 
getting late in the afternoon, so the Rav asked the wagon driver to pull over so he could 
daven mincha. The Rav walked into the forest in order to find a quiet spot where he could 
daven. The other people traveling in the carriage waited respectfully for his return.  
Soon however, the sun set and despite all their searching among the trees there was 
no sign of their rav. The other passengers decided to continue on their way, assuming that 
the rav had joined a different carriage traveling to the chassuna. On their arrival, though, 
they were alarmed: the father of the chosson was not there! Everyone waited several hours 
but in the end, having no choice, they celebrated the chassuna without him. They didn't 
find him on their way home, nor when they arrived back in Yanov.  
Actually, the rav had lost his way in the forest's roundabout tracks. He wandered 
for days and weeks, surviving on whatever fruit and berries he could find. So distressed 
was he that he lost track of time and began keeping Shabbos one day early. Hashem 
protected him from harm, and at last he found his way back to Yanov.  
On Thursday afternoon, when he began to prepare for Shabbos, his family tried to 
explain that his calculations had become confused, but he could not be convinced. He was 
stubborn. He treated Friday as if it were Shabbos, and Shabbos he treated like a regular 
weekday! He even reprimanded his family for their stubbornness. Rabbonim and talmidei 
chachomim from all around tried to convince him that he was wrong, but to no avail.  
When Reb Shmelke of Nikolsburg heard of this, he immediately set out for Yanov, 
arriving on Thursday. The Rav of Yanov invited him for Shabbos, and Reb Shmelke 
agreed to be his guest, instructing all the people of Yanov to prepare for Shabbos. On 
Thursday afternoon, the townsmen obediently dressed in their Shabbos clothes and went 
to shul. Reb Shmelke saw to it that his host served as chazzan for Kabbolas Shabbos, 
while he and all the others quietly davened the weekday Maariv. Then, as if it were 
Shabbos, the rav, his family, and many others, sat down for a seudas Shabbos.  
Reb Shmelke suggested that his host turn this occasion into a seudas Hodayah  -  a 
meal of thanks for his miraculous survival by serving a few extra bottles of wine. Reb 
Shmelke saw to it that his host drank glass after glass of strong wine, until he fell into a 
deep sleep. Reb Shmelke thereupon told the townsmen to leave and to return the following 
night, on Friday night. Reb Shmelke stayed in the house to ensure that his host would not 
69 | P a g e 
be disturbed, and did not even go to shul on Friday night.  
After davening the townsmen came to the Shabbos table, and found the rav still 
sound asleep. They had their actual seudas Shabbos, and at midnight Reb Shmelke woke 
up his host. "Rav of Yanov," he said, "please join us for bensching." The rav washed his 
hands and joined his guests. 
When all the local dignitaries later offered Reb Shmelke their whispered thanks, he 
responded by making them promise never to make the slightest mention of the whole 
episode. And indeed, until the day of this death, the rav never discovered what had 
happened.

Splurging On Shabbos Wine 
The mother of Rebbi Zakkai had a special beautiful head covering which she wore 
Shabbos night.  When she put it on she felt it raised the level of Kedushas Shabbos to new 
heights. 
One Erev Shabbos her son Rebbi Zakkai did not have any money to buy 
wine.  Shabbos was getting closer and Rebbi Zakkai went to Shul without having buying 
wine for Kiddush.  When he returned from Shul he noticed his mother was not wearing 
her beautiful Shabbos head covering that she always wore.  Yet she looked as exquisite as 
ever, beaming with happiness.  He then noticed on the table a bottle of wine.  Not just 
any wine, but a bottle of very expensive wine standing tall on the Shabbos table waiting 
for Kiddush.  
Rebbi Zakkai understood what happened and went over to her and gave her a very 
warm bracha for her devotion and great sacrifice L'Kavod Shabbos.  The next year Rebbi 
Zakkai's fortune took a turn upwards and he became a very wealthy man.  The first thing 
he did was to go out and buy his mother a magnificent head covering shining with precious 
stones. 
When Rebbi Zakkai's mother passed away, she left over for her son, three hundred 
barrels of wine for an inheritance.  When Rebbi Zakkai was niftar he left over three 
thousand barrels of wine.  All this in the zchus of being Michabed Shabbos with the best 
wine.  
70 | P a g e 
The Most Beautiful Sukkah 
Each year, visitors that would come to the Finkelman family’s sukkah were always 
surprised; a most unusual decoration was always hanging across the sukkah. It looked like 
a bunch of crumpled papers tied together with string. On the first night of sukkos Mr. 
Finkelman would tell over the story of this interesting decoration.  
“In the early 1900’s many Yidden needed to be moser nefesh to observe Shabbos. 
Many factory owners would demand that all workers come to work on Saturday. When 
the yiddishe workers would come to work on Monday morning after not showing up on 
Shabbos, the owner would hand him a “pink slip” which meant that he was fired. The loss 
of a job meant that the family would go hungry that week.”  
The crowd would listen with amazement as Mr. Finkelman continued. “This would 
happen to certain families each and every week. The father would come home from shul 
on Shabbos, and with tremendous joy and happiness he would make kiddush and show 
the children how lucky they were to be moser nefesh for Shabbos Kodesh. They would 
rejoice in the fact that they overcame the challenge of not desecrating Shabbos, and they 
would sing zemiros with great joy.” 
“I was zoche to overcome this test and I feel that all of these pink slips that I myself 
received, make the most beautiful decoration for our sukkah.”  
It is no wonder that the Finkelmans have generations that follow their illustrious 
Zaideh as erliche yidden. Doing mitzvos and learning Torah with joy has a very positive 
effect on ourselves and those around us.  
o
Shabbos is Mekor Habrocha 
One day in the early winter of 1931, a young Jewish woman sat with her child in 
the waiting room of a very big doctor’s office in the city of Warsaw. She was finally called 
in and the doctor checked the child who seemed to be very ill. “I am sorry, there is nothing 
I can do for your child” the doctor said to the young mother. “He is very sick.” The 
woman left the office and was crying softly as she made her way back to the inn where 
she was staying.  
71 | P a g e 
“Why are you crying?” asked an elderly woman. The young mother explained that 
her child was ill and the doctor had given up on healing him. “Why don’t you go to the 
Chofetz Chaim, he lives in Radin. If you leave now, you’ll arrive in a few hours.” The 
young mother thanked the elderly lady and listened to her advice.  
She arrived at the Chofetz Chaim’s house and asked to be let in. “I am sorry, but I 
cannot let anyone in” said the grandson softly. “The Rav is old and weak and doesn’t really 
see visitors anymore.” The young woman broke down crying and with a pleading cry she 
said, “Please, my baby is ill and I want a bracha.” The grandson allowed her in and she 
stood before the great tzadik holding her baby in her hands.  
After listening to what the mother said, the Chofetz Chaim responded. “Vein nisht 
mein tuchteral, - don’t cry my daughter - if you agree to always have your Shabbos table set 
early and light your Shabbos neiros early as well, Shabbos, which brings bracha, will most 
definitely heal your child.” The woman thanked the Tzadik and accepted upon herself to 
honor Shabbos by greeting it early and lighting the candles early as well.  
Three months later when she brought the child back to the doctor for another visit, 
the doctor exclaimed: “This cannot be the same child that I saw a few months ago, he is 
a healthy boy, while the other child was ill.” Shabbos which brings bracha had indeed 
brought brocha and a refuah shlaima to that young boy.  
o
Shabbos and a Bowling Alley 
One Friday afternoon Chaim was on the subway, leaving plenty of time to arrive at 
his destination before Shabbos. Suddenly, there was a screech . . . the train came to an 
abrupt halt. The announcer said over the speaker that they were having technical 
difficulties with the train! Chaim looked at his watch, there was still plenty of time to get 
to his destination. Apparently, the technical difficulties were quite complicated, and after 
an hour and- a-half they still had not been solved. At this point Chaim was starting to get 
nervous; the hand of his watch was moving quickly and soon Shabbos would be here.  
Finally, after two hours the train started to move again, with only a short time left 
until Shabbos. Chaim made a quick calculation and realized that he was not going to make 
it to where he was going. With about fifteen minutes until sunset, he ran out of the subway 
72 | P a g e 
station and entered the first building he saw: Tony's Bowling Alley.  
Chaim asked to speak to the owner and was introduced to Tony. Chaim explained 
to him that soon it would be a Jewish holy day and he would be prohibited to carry 
anything. Chaim wanted to know if he could leave his bag with him. Even though he was 
not Jewish, Tony gladly agreed, and Chaim deposited his belongings, including $300 in 
cash, in his office. Meanwhile, Chaim tried to figure out where he was. He found a place 
to daven, and then walked to the home of a friend, who lived about forty blocks 
away.Chaim’s good friend was a little surprised to see him but he and his family made him 
feel welcome in their home.  
After the beautiful Shabbos came to an end, his friend drove him back to the 
bowling alley to retrieve his valuables. Everything was topsy-turvy inside. It seemed like a 
storm had hit and everything was thrown all over. Chaim began to worry that he might 
not get his valuables after all. Finally he found Tony, who had a look of amazement on his 
face. Tony explained to Chaim that there had been a robbery in the bowling alley! He took 
him into his office which had been thoroughly ransacked for any valuables.  
He said, "They took everything, but did not touch your bag. I am sure it is because 
you observed your holy day. The Jews are a holy people; may G-d bless you." He handed 
Chaim his bag with the three hundred dollars sitting safely where he had left it. More than 
we keep Shabbos, Shabbos keeps us.
